2:33:2310:0:14:72:545:700:12:Times:12:g_d0_f1:F:We are all Behavioral, More or Less: Measuring and Using Consumer-level Behavioral Sufficient 
2:34:2403:14:14:72:115:686:12:Times:12:Times:F:Statistics
2:35:2413:14:14:72:245:672:12:Times:12:Times:F:Victor Stango and Jonathan Zinman
2:36:2446:14:14:72:234:658:12:Times:12:Times:F:NBER Working Paper No. 25540
2:37:2474:14:14:72:142:644:12:Times:12:Times:F:February 2019
2:38:2487:14:28:72:222:630:12:Times:12:Times:F:JEL No. C83,D1,D6,D9,E7,G4
2:39:2513:28:28:275:340:602:12:Times:12:Times:F:ABSTRACT
2:40:2521:28:14:72:548:574:12:Times:12:Times:F:Can  a  behavioral  sufficient  statistic  empirically  capture  cross-consumer  variation  in  behavioral 
2:41:2628:14:14:72:547:560:12:Times:12:Times:F:tendencies and  help  identify  whether  behavioral  biases,  taken  together,  are  linked  to  material 
2:42:2733:14:14:72:548:546:12:Times:12:Times:F:consumer  welfare losses?  Our  answer  is  yes.  We  construct  simple  consumer-level  behavioral 
2:43:2832:14:14:72:548:532:12:Times:12:Times:F:sufficient  statistics—“B-counts”—by eliciting  seventeen  potential  sources  of  behavioral  biases 
2:44:2933:14:14:72:544:518:12:Times:12:Times:F:per person, in a nationally representative panel, in two separate rounds nearly three years apart. B-
2:45:3033:14:14:72:548:504:12:Times:12:Times:F:counts  aggregate  information  on  behavioral biases  within-person.  Nearly  all  consumers  exhibit 
2:46:3135:14:14:72:548:490:12:Times:12:Times:F:multiple  biases,  in  patterns  assumed  by  behavioral sufficient  statistic  models  (a  la  Chetty),  and 
2:47:3244:14:14:72:548:476:12:Times:12:Times:F:with substantial variation across people. B-counts are stable within-consumer over time, and that 
2:48:3341:14:14:72:548:462:12:Times:12:Times:F:stability  helps  to  address  measurement  error  when  using B-counts  to  model  the  relationship 
2:49:3442:14:14:72:548:448:12:Times:12:Times:F:between  biases,  decision  utility,  and  experienced  utility.  Conditional on  classical  inputs—risk 
2:50:3546:14:14:72:547:434:12:Times:12:Times:F:aversion  and  patience,  life-cycle  factors  and  other  demographics,  cognitive and  non-cognitive 
2:51:3648:14:14:72:547:420:12:Times:12:Times:F:skills,  and  financial  resources—B-counts  strongly  negatively  correlate  with  both objective  and 
2:52:3751:14:14:72:548:406:12:Times:12:Times:F:subjective  aspects  of  experienced  utility.  The  results  hold  in  much  lower-dimensional models 
2:53:3853:14:14:72:548:392:12:Times:12:g_d0_f1:F:employing  “Sparsity  B-counts”  based  on  bias  subsets  (a  la  Gabaix)  and/or  fewer  covariates, 
2:54:3955:14:14:72:548:378:12:Times:12:Times:F:illuminating  lower-cost  ways  to  use  behavioral  sufficient  statistics  to  help  capture  the  combined 
2:55:4065:14:42:72:528:364:12:Times:12:Times:F:influence of multiple behavioral biases for a wide range of research questions and applications.
2:56:4160:42:14:72:139:322:12:Times:12:Times:F:Victor Stango
2:57:4173:14:14:72:231:308:12:Times:12:Times:F:Graduate School of Management
2:58:4202:14:14:72:221:294:12:Times:12:Times:F:University of California, Davis
2:59:4233:14:14:72:171:280:12:Times:12:Times:F:One Shields Avenue
2:60:4251:14:14:72:156:266:12:Times:12:Times:F:Davis, CA 95616
2:61:4266:14:28:72:177:252:12:Times:12:Times:F:vstango@ucdavis.edu
2:62:4285:28:14:72:155:224:12:Times:12:Times:F:Jonathan Zinman
2:63:4300:14:14:72:198:210:12:Times:12:Times:F:Department of Economics
2:64:4323:14:14:72:164:196:12:Times:12:Times:F:Dartmouth College
2:65:4340:14:14:72:173:182:12:Times:12:Times:F:314 Rockefeller Hall
2:66:4360:14:14:72:170:168:12:Times:12:Times:F:Hanover, NH 03755
2:67:4377:14:14:72:124:154:12:Times:12:Times:F:and NBER
2:68:4385:14:0:72:191:140:12:Times:12:Times:F:jzinman@dartmouth.edu
3:69:4406:0:20:72:75:708:144:Times:144:Times:B: 
3:70:4406:20:21:93:522:688:144:Times:144:Times:A:“A  common  criticism  of  behavioral  economics  is  that  it  does  not  offer  a  single  unified  
3:71:4508:21:21:93:466:667:144:Times:144:Times:A:framework as an alternative to the neoclassical model.” (Chetty 2015, p. 25) 
3:72:4585:21:21:93:96:646:144:Times:144:Times:A: 
3:73:4585:21:20:93:521:625:144:Times:144:Times:A:“... behavioral models... do not integrate well with basic microeconomic theory because 
3:74:4673:20:21:93:521:605:144:Times:144:Times:A:they do not develop a general procedure for the basic economic operation of simplifying 
3:75:4761:21:21:93:442:584:144:Times:144:Times:A:reality and acting using that simplified model.” (Gabaix 2014, p. 1662)  
3:76:4834:21:20:94:97:563:144:Times:144:Times:A: 
3:77:4834:20:21:94:543:543:144:Times:144:Times:A:Behavioral  social  scientists  have  identified  myriad  biases  in  decision  making  that  could 
3:78:4933:21:21:72:545:522:144:Times:144:Times:B:reduce  consumer  welfare,  but  capturing myriad  potential  behavioral  influences  in  a  portable  
3:79:5036:21:20:71:543:501:144:Times:144:Times:B:model—a “general  procedure”  per  Gabaix,  or a  “single  unified  framework”  per  Chetty—is 
3:80:5131:20:21:71:136:481:144:Times:144:Times:B:challenging.  
3:81:5145:21:21:94:542:460:144:Times:144:Times:A:Some researchers are responding to that challenge with models where a behavioral sufficient 
3:82:5237:21:21:71:543:439:144:Times:144:Times:B:statistic represents consumer-level behavioral tendencies and their links to decisions/outcomes.1 
3:83:5334:21:20:72:546:418:144:Times:144:Times:B:But  those  models  lack  empirical  validation. Most  empirical  work  in  behavioral  economics  
3:84:5432:20:21:72:543:398:144:Times:144:Times:B:examines only one or two biases at a time, and we do not know of any prior work that seeks to 
3:85:5526:21:21:72:517:377:144:Times:144:Times:B:measure consumer-level behavioral sufficient statistics and their links to consumer welfare.2 
3:86:5619:21:20:94:545:356:144:Times:144:Times:A:An  empirically  valid  behavioral  sufficient  statistic  has  many  applications.  Several  recent  
3:87:5721:20:21:72:539:336:144:Times:144:Times:B:papers show that if behavioral decision making tendencies can be summarized with a consumer-
3:88:5813:21:21:72:543:315:144:Times:144:Times:B:level sufficient statistic, then that statistic becomes a powerful input for intervention design and 
3:89:5914:21:21:72:542:294:144:Times:144:Times:B:welfare analysis.3 Policymakers increasingly formulate high-level strategy and specific regulations 
3:90:6014:21:20:71:540:273:144:Times:144:Times:B:based on assumptions about how multiple biases, taken together, are prevalent and affect decision-
3:91:6111:20:49:72:542:253:144:Times:144:Times:B:making.4 The hundreds of “nudge units” proliferating in public, private, and nonprofit sectors also 
3:92:6211:49:17:72:542:204:49:Times:121:Times:F:1 For  reviews  see,  e.g.,  the  reduced-form  sufficient  statistic  models  in  Chetty  (2009,  2015)  and 
3:93:6321:17:8:72:486:187:121:Times:121:Times:F:Mullainathan, Schwartzstein, and Congdon (2012), and the sparsity models in Gabaix (2019). 
3:94:6412:8:17:72:543:179:49:Times:121:Times:F:2 The  closest  work  to  ours  is  Chapman  et  al.  (2018a,  2018b),  Dean  and  Ortoleva  (2018),  and  Gillen  et  
3:95:6531:17:13:71:543:162:121:Times:121:Times:F:al.   (forthcoming): those papers also measure multiple biases per person and examine relationships among 
3:96:6637:13:8:71:513:149:121:Times:121:Times:F:biases. But they do not link their biases to field outcomes or develop behavioral sufficient statistics. 
3:97:6741:8:17:72:545:141:49:Times:121:Times:F:3 In  addition  to  the  review  articles  cited  in  fn  1,  see  also  Allcott  and  Taubinsky  (2015);  Baicker,  
3:98:6858:17:13:71:542:124:121:Times:121:Times:F:Mullainathan,  and  Schwartzstein  (2015);  Chetty,  Kroft,  and  Looney  (2009);  Farhi  and  Gabaix  (2018); 
3:99:6969:13:8:71:302:111:121:Times:121:Times:F:Gabaix (2014, 2017, 2018), and Spinnewijn (2015). 
3:100:7019:8:17:72:542:103:49:Times:121:Times:F:4 Recent examples include the Department of Energy, Consumer Financial Protection Bureau, and the SEC 
3:101:7121:17:0:72:507:86:121:Times:121:Times:F:in the U.S., the Financial Conduct Authority in the U.K., the World Bank, and the United Nations. 
4:102:7220:0:20:72:543:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:presume that multiple behavioral biases affect behavior.5 A behavioral sufficient statistic can be 
4:103:7319:20:21:72:543:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:used  for  targeting, for theory-testing  (based,  e.g.,  on  predictions  about  heterogeneous  treatment 
4:104:7426:21:21:72:543:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:effects), for estimating model inputs (such as the average marginal bias that is key in Allcott and 
4:105:7526:21:21:72:542:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:Taubinsky (2015)), and for descriptively filling gaps where unobserved heterogeneity in consumer 
4:106:7623:21:20:72:540:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:decision  making  looms  large:  one  example  among  many  is  the  still  largely  unexplained  cross-
4:107:7727:20:21:71:381:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:sectional distribution of wealth (Poterba 2014; Campbell 2016). 
4:108:7791:21:21:94:542:584:144:Times:144:Times:A:We tighten the link between behavioral sufficient statistic theory and empirics by developing, 
4:109:7886:21:20:71:543:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:measuring,   and   examining “B-counts”: consumer-level   statistics   that capture   behavioral 
4:110:7983:20:21:71:539:543:144:Times:144:Times:B:tendencies by aggregating information on multiple biases within-person. We adapt standard lab-
4:111:8077:21:21:71:542:522:144:Times:144:Times:B:style elicitation methods to measure 17 potential sources of behavioral biases per consumer (Table 
4:112:8176:21:20:71:543:501:144:Times:144:Times:B:1), in about 30 minutes of online survey/task time.6 We then administer those elicitations twice, in 
4:113:8277:20:21:72:542:481:144:Times:144:Times:B:two separate rounds of data collection about three years apart, to the same plausibly representative 
4:114:8378:21:21:72:545:460:144:Times:144:Times:B:sample  of  845 U.S.  consumers  from  the  American  Life  Panel.  We  also  collect  rich  data  on  
4:115:8481:21:21:72:546:439:144:Times:144:Times:B:outcomes (various  measures  of  objective  and  subjective  well-being in  financial  and  other  
4:116:8580:21:20:71:543:418:144:Times:144:Times:B:domains), “classical decision  inputs”    (cognitive  and  non-cognitive  skills,  presumed-classical 
4:117:8682:20:21:71:430:398:144:Times:144:Times:B:preferences, life-cycle factors and other demographics), and survey effort. 
4:118:8758:21:21:94:543:377:144:Times:144:Times:A:B-counts summarize  behavioral  tendencies  at  the  consumer-level, simply: they  count  how 
4:119:8852:21:20:71:542:356:144:Times:144:Times:B:many biases  (  deviations  from  the  classical  benchmark)   a  consumer  exhibits.  In  addition  to  a 
4:120:8959:20:21:71:544:336:144:Times:144:Times:B:Chetty-like  “Full”  B-count based  on  all  17 potential  sources  we  measure,  we  also  construct  a  
4:121:9065:21:21:71:543:315:144:Times:144:Times:B:number of “B-sub-counts” motivated by other classes of models, including: “Sparsity B-counts” 
4:122:9159:21:20:71:547:294:144:Times:144:Times:B:focusing  on  limited  attention/memory  and  related  phenomena  following  Gabaix;   an  “Expected  
4:123:9261:20:21:71:540:274:144:Times:144:Times:B:Direction B-   count”  including  present-bias  but  not  future-bias,  over-confidence  but  not  under-
4:124:9366:21:21:71:547:253:144:Times:144:Times:B:confidence,  and  so  on;7 a  “Preference  B-count” that  includes  inconsistency  with  revealed  
4:125:9465:21:21:72:543:232:144:Times:144:Times:B:preference, loss aversion, etc., but not biased beliefs or problem-solving approaches; and a “Math 
4:126:9564:21:20:72:435:211:144:Times:144:Times:B:B-count” including only exponential growth biases and statistical fallacies. 
4:127:9641:20:21:94:543:191:144:Times:144:Times:A:We then present five sets of results showing that B-counts are useful and practical tools for 
4:128:9735:21:54:72:382:170:144:Times:144:Times:B:developing and applying models of consumer decision making.  
4:129:9796:54:12:72:311:116:49:Times:121:Times:F:5 See, e.g. Afif et al. (2018) and Guntner et al. (2019). 
4:130:9854:12:17:72:543:104:49:Times:121:Times:F:6 We chose the 17 based on prior work linking biases to consumer decisions, particularly in the financial 
4:131:9960:17:9:71:462:87:121:Times:121:Times:F:domain, and on practical considerations and constraints. See Section 3-A for discussion. 
4:132:10048:9:0:72:358:78:49:Times:121:Times:F:7 We borrow the “expected” label from Chapman et al. (2018a).  
5:133:10112:0:20:94:543:708:144:Times:144:Times:A:First, B-counts validate assumptions required for behavioral sufficient statistic models to be 
5:134:10207:20:21:72:543:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:relevant and useful for policy and welfare analysis. One example is that behavioral tendencies are 
5:135:10306:21:21:72:543:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:more  common  than  rare:  nearly  everyone  exhibits  multiple  biases,  and  a   consumer  at  the  10th 
5:136:10412:21:21:72:544:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:percentile  has  a  B-count  of  7  out  of  a  possible  17  in  each  of  our  two  survey  rounds.  Another  
5:137:10524:21:20:72:543:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:example is that consumers are biased on average (Allcott and Taubinsky 2015), in the sense that 
5:138:10620:20:21:72:543:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:expected-direction biases (e.g., preference for certainty)   emphasized by prior literature are more 
5:139:10721:21:21:72:551:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:prevalent than “non-expected direction” biases (e.g., preference for uncertainty).    Also prevalent 
5:140:10822:21:20:72:542:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:are limited attention and memory, Gabaix’s  postulated  “psychological  foundations”  for  sparsity 
5:141:10922:20:21:72:545:543:144:Times:144:Times:B:models, as well as “behavioral phenomena” (other biases) that can emerge from sparsity models.8  
5:142:11019:21:21:94:543:522:144:Times:144:Times:A:Second,  B-counts are  stable  within-consumer  over  time.  The  within-person cross-round 
5:143:11111:21:20:72:542:501:144:Times:144:Times:B:correlation in our Full B-count is 0.44—a high number relative to prior work estimating temporal 
5:144:11208:20:21:72:542:481:144:Times:144:Times:B:stability  in  behavioral  biases  or presumed-classical  preferences.  B-count stability presents 
5:145:11307:21:21:72:543:460:144:Times:144:Times:B:opportunities to account for measurement error when estimating conditional correlations between 
5:146:11403:21:21:72:542:439:144:Times:144:Times:B:outcomes and B-counts, either by instrumenting for one round’s B-count with the other (“standard 
5:147:11500:21:20:72:543:418:144:Times:144:Times:B:IV”),  or  better  yet  by  instrumenting  both  ways  with  Obviously  Related  Instrumental  Variables 
5:148:11604:20:21:72:287:398:144:Times:144:Times:B:(Gillen, Snowberg, and Yariv forthcoming). 
5:149:11647:21:21:94:543:377:144:Times:144:Times:A: Third, and perhaps most fundamentally for sufficient statistic and other behavioral models, 
5:150:11740:21:20:72:543:356:144:Times:144:Times:B:B-counts strongly  and  negatively  correlate  with  both subjective  and  objective  well-being, 
5:151:11838:20:21:71:542:336:144:Times:144:Times:B:conditional  on  our  rich  sets   of covariates.9 A simple  framework,  drawing  on  Chetty  (2015), 
5:152:11940:21:21:72:543:315:144:Times:144:Times:B:Benjamin et al. (2014) and Benjamin et al. (2014), clarifies conditions under which the B-count 
5:153:12036:21:21:72:543:294:144:Times:144:Times:B:captures a systematic wedge between decision utility (what consumers expect to gain from their 
5:154:12131:21:20:72:545:273:144:Times:144:Times:B:decisions)  and  experienced  utility  (what  they  actually  gain  from  their  decisions).  This  in  turn  
5:155:12241:20:21:72:544:253:144:Times:144:Times:B:clarifies what one can  and  cannot  infer  about  consumer  welfare  from  conditional  correlations  
5:156:12344:21:40:72:543:232:144:Times:144:Times:B:between different outcome measures and B-counts. We show how our approach addresses classical 
5:157:12437:40:17:72:543:192:49:Times:121:Times:F:8 Our  direct  elicitation  data  thus  complements  empirical  work  using  other  methods  to  explore  the  nature  
5:158:12556:17:13:71:543:175:121:Times:121:Times:F:and  economic  importance  of  limited  attention  and  other  behavioral  biases;  for  attention-focused  reviews  
5:159:12673:13:12:71:543:162:121:Times:121:Times:F:see Gabaix (2019) and Handel and Schwartzstein (2018), for broader reviews see Bernheim et al. (2018, 
5:160:12775:12:13:71:542:150:121:Times:121:Times:F:2019). The prevalence of limited attention and price misperceptions also makes it plausible that bias-born 
5:161:12882:13:13:71:542:137:121:Times:121:Times:F:mistakes can persist because learning is slow or otherwise imperfect (e.g., Ali 2011; Bordalo, Gennaioli, 
5:162:12988:13:8:71:314:124:121:Times:121:Times:F:and Shleifer 2017; Gabaix 2017; Schwartzstein 2014). 
5:163:13041:8:17:72:542:116:49:Times:121:Times:F:9 Quantitatively speaking, in our nine main specifications estimating the conditional correlation between an 
5:164:13150:17:12:71:542:99:121:Times:121:Times:F:outcome and a B-count, the B-count always has a p-value<0.01, and a one standard deviation change in a 
5:165:13253:12:13:71:543:87:121:Times:121:Times:F:B-count  is  associated  with  a  22  to  30%  reduction  in  objective  financial  condition  and  a  26%  to  43%  
5:166:13370:13:0:71:263:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:reduction in subjective financial condition. 
6:167:13415:0:20:72:542:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:measurement error and also consider various non-classical measurement error structures. They are 
6:168:13511:20:21:71:536:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:unlikely to confound our inferences, given our research design and the overall pattern of results. 
6:169:13609:21:21:94:545:667:144:Times:144:Times:A:The  negative  conditional  correlations  between  outcomes  and  B-counts  hold  for  different  
6:170:13707:21:21:71:546:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:covariate  specifications  (a  la  Altonji et  al.  (2005)),  including  ones  where  we  allow  for  
6:171:13809:21:20:71:544:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:measurement error in classical inputs,  add  objective  financial  condition  as  an  additional  control  
6:172:13916:20:21:71:542:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:when subjective financial condition is the outcome of interest, or drop all other covariates entirely. 
6:173:14018:21:21:71:543:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:We  draw  outcomes  from  other  ALP  surveys  as  well  as  our  own,  and  find that  the  negative 
6:174:14120:21:20:71:542:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:conditional correlations hold for different “aspects” of experienced utility (per Benjamin et al.)— 
6:175:14220:20:21:71:543:543:144:Times:144:Times:B:life  satisfaction,  happiness,  and  health  status—at  least  when  we  use the  Sparsity  B-counts as 
6:176:14325:21:21:71:543:522:144:Times:144:Times:B:sufficient statistics. The results are too imprecise to characterize for non-financial aspects when 
6:177:14425:21:20:71:542:501:144:Times:144:Times:B:we  use  the Full  B-count as  the  sufficient  statistic,  perhaps  because we  chose  our  17  potential 
6:178:14532:20:21:71:471:481:144:Times:144:Times:B:sources of behavioral biases with financial decision making in particular in mind.   
6:179:14617:21:21:94:542:460:144:Times:144:Times:A:Decomposing the Full B-count into couplet sub-counts sheds further light on mechanisms and 
6:180:14708:21:21:71:543:439:144:Times:144:Times:B:welfare implications. The Full B-count’s correlations with outcomes are driven more robustly by 
6:181:14804:21:20:71:543:418:144:Times:144:Times:B:the thirteen  non-math  biases  than by the  four math biases  that  are  more  arguably  reflections  of  
6:182:14911:20:21:71:542:398:144:Times:144:Times:B:classical math/cognitive skills. The results are not driven by the seven preference biases, which is 
6:183:15012:21:21:71:539:377:144:Times:144:Times:B:notable because  behavioral  preferences  are  less  clearly  welfare-reducing  than the ten  non-
6:184:15110:21:20:71:542:356:144:Times:144:Times:B:preference   biases   (e.g., biased expectations,   price   perceptions, limited attention/memory). 
6:185:15210:20:21:71:542:336:144:Times:144:Times:B:Expected-d irection biases (e.g., present-bias) robustly negatively correlate   with outcomes, while 
6:186:15311:21:21:71:340:315:144:Times:144:Times:B:non-expected direction biases (e.g. future-bias) do not.  
6:187:15369:21:20:94:546:294:144:Times:144:Times:A:Fourth,  B-counts capture  information  distinct  from  that measured  by  classical  decision  
6:188:15465:20:21:71:544:274:144:Times:144:Times:B:inputs/covariates. As noted above, our estimated conditional correlations between outcomes and 
6:189:15560:21:21:71:544:253:144:Times:144:Times:B:B-counts are  virtually  invariant  to  the  set  of  covariates. Moreover, those  other  covariates  only  
6:190:15668:21:20:71:544:232:144:Times:144:Times:B:weakly  explain  B-counts themselves;  e.g., regressing  a  B-count  measure  on  the  complete  set  of  
6:191:15774:20:21:71:543:212:144:Times:144:Times:B:other covariates yields an R-squared of 0.25 or less for the Sparsity B-counts and 0.33 for the Full 
6:192:15875:21:21:71:124:191:144:Times:144:Times:B:B-   count. 
6:193:15887:21:21:94:543:170:144:Times:144:Times:A:Fifth,  one  can  add  B-counts to  many  research  designs with simple,  quick elicitations.  The 
6:194:15986:21:20:71:543:149:144:Times:144:Times:B:Narrow Sparsity  B-count  takes  less  than  two  minutes to  elicit,  and  in  our  main  specification  it 
6:195:16095:20:21:71:540:129:144:Times:144:Times:B:correlates with experienced utility aspects at least as strongly as the Full B-count does. Even B-
6:196:16193:21:21:71:543:108:144:Times:144:Times:B:counts  constructed  using  subsets  of  our  17  behavioral elicitations  selected at  random  deliver 
6:197:16297:21:0:71:543:87:144:Times:144:Times:B:average coefficient magnitudes and precision similar to those obtained by using the Full B-count. 
7:198:16396:0:20:72:543:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:Altogether, our results suggest that measuring a handful of behavioral biases can usefully capture 
7:199:16494:20:21:72:274:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:consumer-level behavioral heterogeneity. 
7:200:16535:21:21:94:543:667:144:Times:144:Times:A:Summarizing   our   results:    Our   simple   behavioral   sufficient   statistics,   B-counts,   have 
7:201:16639:21:21:72:540:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:distributional properties required for them to be useful for welfare analysis. They are stable within-
7:202:16741:21:20:72:542:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:consumer over time, and one can use that stability to allow for measurement error when estimating 
7:203:16839:20:21:72:543:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:relationships  between  outcomes  and  B-counts.  B-counts capture consumer  heterogeneity  that  is  
7:204:16941:21:21:72:545:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:distinct  from  classical  decision  inputs  like  cognitive  and  non-cognitive  skills.  Relatedly  and  
7:205:17048:21:20:72:544:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:perhaps  most  importantly,  B-counts strongly  conditionally  correlate  with  both  quantitative  and  
7:206:17153:20:21:72:543:543:144:Times:144:Times:B:subjective  measures  of  well-being, in  ways  that  are  consistent  with  behavioral  biases  having 
7:207:17257:21:21:72:543:522:144:Times:144:Times:B:negative, reinforcing effects on consumer welfare. Measuring low-dimensional B-counts can be a 
7:208:17352:21:20:72:543:501:144:Times:144:Times:B:valuable and practical addition to many research designs. Efficient measurement likely involves 
7:209:17448:20:21:72:542:481:144:Times:144:Times:B:conducting two elicitations of a B-count (because repeated elicitation has great value in accounting 
7:210:17549:21:21:72:542:460:144:Times:144:Times:B:for measurement error), and using theory to guide the selection of outcomes and biases to measure 
7:211:17647:21:21:72:539:439:144:Times:144:Times:B:(as we have done, e.g., in linking financial outcomes to our 17 potential biases, and in defining B-
7:212:17747:21:20:72:260:418:144:Times:144:Times:B:sub-counts based on sparsity models).  
7:213:17786:20:27:94:532:398:144:Times:144:Times:A:The next section formalizes our approach and provides a roadmap for the rest of the paper. 
7:214:17877:27:27:72:215:371:144:Times:144:Times:B:1. Conceptual Framework 
7:215:17899:27:20:94:542:344:144:Times:144:Times:A:Conceptually, a  behavioral  sufficient  statistic  is  a  consumer-level metric  reflecting overall 
7:216:18000:20:21:72:542:324:144:Times:144:Times:B:behavioral tendencies and their links to consumer decision making and welfare. Such a behavioral 
7:217:18097:21:21:72:543:303:144:Times:144:Times:B:sufficient statistic is a linchpin of models such as those in Chetty (2015) and Gabaix (various) but 
7:218:18198:21:20:72:311:282:144:Times:144:Times:B:has not been measured directly in previous work. 
7:219:18247:20:21:94:542:262:144:Times:144:Times:A:Chetty-style models  employ  a  sufficient  statistic as  a  proxy  for many  different  behavioral 
7:220:18347:21:21:72:542:241:144:Times:144:Times:B:phenomena. This approach “does not require specifying the exact behavioral model that describes 
7:221:18443:21:20:72:544:220:144:Times:144:Times:B:agents’  choices” (Chetty  2015,  p.  25),  so  long  as  the  statistic  captures  the  combined  effects  of  
7:222:18555:20:21:72:476:200:144:Times:144:Times:B:multiple behavioral biases and satisfies other assumptions discussed in Section 3-E. 
7:223:18640:21:21:94:543:179:144:Times:144:Times:A:Gabaix’s sparsity and behavioral inattention models are more oriented toward fundamentals, 
7:224:18731:21:21:72:543:158:144:Times:144:Times:B:employing as a sufficient statistic a single, “psychologically founded” attention cost parameter m 
7:225:18829:21:0:72:543:137:144:Times:144:Times:B:that  “condenses”  behavioral  tendencies  toward  simplification,  inattention,  and  disproportionate 
8:226:18933:0:20:72:546:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:salience (Gabaix 2014,  p.  1662).10 From  that  foundation  “a large  number  of  behavioral  
8:227:19028:20:21:72:543:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:phenomena” emerge (Gabaix 2019, p.5), including price misperceptions, statistical fallacies, and 
8:228:19125:21:21:72:543:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:time-inconsistent discounting. Gabaix’s sufficient statistic approach allows one to tractably recast 
8:229:19226:21:21:72:345:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:basic micro and macro theory through a behavioral lens. 
8:230:19282:21:21:94:542:625:144:Times:144:Times:A:Following the broad strokes of Methods 1 and 2 in Chetty (2015), suppose a consumer i faces 
8:231:19374:21:20:72:546:604:144:Times:144:Times:B:market  prices  푝푝푖푖 and  resource  endowments  푤푤푖푖.  A  classical  consumer—one  with  standard  
8:232:19473:20:21:72:542:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:preferences, unbiased beliefs, and unbiased perceptions of prices—will make a utility-maximizing 
8:233:19570:21:28:72:343:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:set of consumption decisions 푥푥푖푖∗, giving decision utility: 
8:234:19631:28:28:239:375:535:144:g_d0_f8:144:Times:A:푉푉푖푖퐷퐷(푝푝푖푖,푤푤푖푖)=푉푉푖푖[푥푥푖푖∗(푝푝푖푖,푤푤푖푖)] 
8:235:19671:28:21:94:542:507:144:Times:144:Times:A:Now, suppose that consumers can also vary in a behavioral sufficient statistic 휃휃푖푖 that generates 
8:236:19770:21:28:72:465:486:144:Times:144:Times:B:a wedge between experienced utility  푉푉푖푖퐸퐸 and decision utility 푉푉푖푖퐷퐷, in which case:11 
8:237:19859:28:27:227:410:458:144:Times:144:Times:A:(1)   푉푉푖푖퐸퐸(푝푝푖푖,푤푤푖푖)=푉푉푖푖퐷퐷(푝푝푖푖,푤푤푖푖)−푓푓(휃휃푖푖) 
8:238:19908:27:21:94:542:431:144:Times:144:Times:A:This equation lays bare our empirical goal: to use B-counts to capture the behavioral sufficient 
8:239:20005:21:22:72:542:410:144:Times:144:Times:B:statistic 푓푓(휃휃푖푖), thereby explaining any wedge between decision and experienced utility. We do so 
8:240:20105:22:21:72:543:388:144:Times:144:Times:B:by empirically specifying  both  푉푉푖푖퐸퐸 (experienced  utility)  and  푉푉푖푖퐷퐷(decision  utility),  and  testing 
8:241:20214:21:21:72:543:367:144:Times:144:Times:B:whether B-counts empirically correlate with the difference between them, at the consumer level. 
8:242:20309:21:20:72:543:346:144:Times:144:Times:B:We  construct  and  use  B-counts  based  on  a  broad  set  of  biases  a  la  Chetty,  on  narrower  sets  of  
8:243:20422:20:21:72:543:326:144:Times:144:Times:B:biases emphasized by Gabaix’s sparsity and behavioral inattention models, and on other subsets 
8:244:20517:21:21:72:432:305:144:Times:144:Times:B:of biases suggested by other theories and taxonomies of behavioral biases. 
8:245:20592:21:20:94:543:284:144:Times:144:Times:A:Section  2 briefly  describes  our  research  design  and  data  collection  strategy  at  a  high  level, 
8:246:20699:20:21:72:538:264:144:Times:144:Times:B:focusing on sampling and elicitation strategies for collecting rich data on all three objects in (1).  
8:247:20802:21:21:94:542:243:144:Times:144:Times:A:Section 3 details how we measure our B-counts, in Sections 3-A and 3-B. We establish that 
8:248:20892:21:42:71:541:222:144:Times:144:Times:B:B-counts are stable within-person over time in Section 3-C, and that behavioral tendencies are both 
8:249:20992:42:18:72:543:180:49:Times:121:Times:F:10 Gabaix  uses “sparsity”  to  mean  consumer-level  whittling  of  the  set  of  economic  phenomena  used  for  
8:250:21107:18:12:71:542:162:121:Times:121:Times:F:decisionmaking, focusing on the most relevant ones and ignoring the less relevant ones, and consequently 
8:251:21212:12:13:71:542:150:121:Times:121:Times:F:incurring welfare costs (while saving attention costs). For example, concentrating on some prices but not 
8:252:21318:13:8:71:252:137:121:Times:121:Times:F:others is captured by the sparsity model. 
8:253:21359:8:17:72:542:129:49:Times:121:Times:F:11 This formulation is very similar Fahri and Gabaix’s (2018) equation (3), where “the behavioral wedge is 
8:254:21466:17:13:71:543:112:121:Times:121:Times:F:simply  given  by  the  wedge  between  the  decision  and  experienced  marginal  utilities.”  As with  a  wedge  
8:255:21581:13:12:71:544:99:121:Times:121:Times:F:expressed  in  level  utilities  or  prices,  the  overall  effect  is  to  create  a  gap  between  decision  utility  and  
8:256:21706:12:13:71:542:87:121:Times:121:Times:F:experienced utility. In both the (Fahri-)Gabaix representation and ours, decision utility and the wedge are 
8:257:21814:13:0:71:514:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:separable. We discuss the empirical validity of the separability assumption in the Results Appendix. 
9:258:21916:0:20:72:543:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:prevalent,  and distinct  from  classical  decision  inputs,  in  Section  3-D. We discuss  modeling 
9:259:22017:20:21:71:208:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:implications in Section 3-E. 
9:260:22046:21:21:94:542:667:144:Times:144:Times:A:Section 4 specifies our econometric model, with particular attention to measurement error in 
9:261:22139:21:21:71:541:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:the behavioral sufficient statistic, decision utility, and/or experienced utility, and lays out the ORIV 
9:262:22244:21:21:71:545:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:estimator and other features of our research design, including controls for respondent survey effort.  
9:263:22347:21:22:94:543:604:144:Times:144:Times:A:Section  5 presents various  specifications  of  empirics correlating 푉푉푖푖퐸퐸 with  B-counts 푓푓(휃휃푖푖), 
9:264:22448:22:21:72:540:582:144:Times:144:Times:B:conditional  on  classical  decision  inputs  푉푉푖푖퐷퐷and survey  effort.  Section  6  further  details  how  B-
9:265:22558:21:21:72:544:561:144:Times:144:Times:B:counts  are  empirically  distinct  from  other covariates.  Section  7 shows  that  one  can  elicit  and  
9:266:22666:21:20:72:542:540:144:Times:144:Times:B:employ B-counts with minimal survey time/expense. Section 8 concludes and offers directions for 
9:267:22762:20:27:72:149:520:144:Times:144:Times:B:future research. 
9:268:22778:27:27:71:286:493:144:Times:144:Times:B:2. Research Design and Data Collection  
9:269:22817:27:20:94:542:466:144:Times:144:Times:A:This section provides an overview of our research design, focusing on sampling and elicitation 
9:270:22912:20:21:72:543:446:144:Times:144:Times:B:strategies for constructing rich, consumer-level measures on all three objects in (1): experienced 
9:271:23011:21:27:72:357:425:144:Times:144:Times:B:utility, decision utility, and behavioral sufficient statistics.  
9:272:23077:27:21:72:185:398:144:Times:144:Times:B:A. Variables overview 
9:273:23097:21:20:94:543:377:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  measure  four multi-dimensional sets  of  consumer  characteristics.  One set includes  the 
9:274:23192:20:22:72:542:357:144:Times:144:Times:B:behavioral  biases we  use  to  construct  B-counts  (detailed  in  Section  3-A).  A  second  includes 
9:275:23296:22:21:72:542:335:144:Times:144:Times:B:outcomes used to construct measures of experienced utility  푉푉푖푖퐸퐸: objective and subjective measures 
9:276:23396:21:20:72:542:314:144:Times:144:Times:B:of financial well-being, and standard measures of other aspects of subjective well-being (Section 
9:277:23494:20:22:71:542:294:144:Times:144:Times:B:4-A). A third includes classical decision inputs (as distinct from behavioral decision inputs) used 
9:278:23594:22:21:72:542:272:144:Times:144:Times:B:to construct measures of decision utility 푉푉푖푖퐷퐷: demographics (including life-cycle factors), classical 
9:279:23698:21:21:71:543:251:144:Times:144:Times:B:time and risk preferences/attitudes, and cognitive and non-cognitive skills (Section 4-B). A fourth 
9:280:23798:21:20:71:542:230:144:Times:144:Times:B:set includes “survey effort”: measures of time spent on our elicitations, and of item non-response 
9:281:23897:20:21:71:143:210:144:Times:144:Times:B:(Section 4-A). 
9:282:23912:21:27:71:212:189:144:Times:144:Times:B:B. The American Life Panel 
9:283:23938:27:21:94:543:162:144:Times:144:Times:A:We administered our survey through the RAND American Life Panel (ALP). The ALP is an 
9:284:24023:21:20:71:543:141:144:Times:144:Times:B:online survey panel established in 2003. RAND regularly offers panel members opportunities to 
9:285:24117:20:21:72:543:121:144:Times:144:Times:B:participate in surveys, designed by researchers for purposes spanning the range of social sciences. 
9:286:24217:21:21:71:543:100:144:Times:144:Times:B:Over 400 surveys have been administered in the ALP, and RAND makes data publicly available 
9:287:24308:21:0:71:543:79:144:Times:144:Times:B:after a period of initial embargo. We use data from some of those other modules to complement 
10:288:24403:0:20:72:247:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:our data, as detailed in Section 5-D. 
10:289:24441:20:21:94:543:688:144:Times:144:Times:A:The ALP takes great pains to obtain a nationally representative sample, combining standard 
10:290:24532:21:21:72:543:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:sampling techniques with offers of hardware and a broadband connection to potential participants 
10:291:24628:21:21:72:542:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:who lack adequate Internet access. ALP sampling weights match the distribution of age, gender, 
10:292:24723:21:20:72:543:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:ethnicity, and income to the Current Population Survey. We show that our main results are robust 
10:293:24820:20:27:72:369:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:to using these weights (Sections 3-C and Results Appendix).  
10:294:24881:27:27:72:229:578:144:Times:144:Times:B:C. Research design and sample 
10:295:24910:27:20:94:546:551:144:Times:144:Times:A:Two  principles  guided  our  research  design.  First,  measure the  richest  set  of  individual  
10:296:25010:20:21:72:543:531:144:Times:144:Times:B:characteristics possible, to minimize confounds from omitted variables and allow exploration of 
10:297:25105:21:21:72:543:510:144:Times:144:Times:B:relationships between B-counts and classical/standard covariates such as demographics, cognitive 
10:298:25202:21:20:71:545:489:144:Times:144:Times:B:skills,  non-cognitive  skills  and  classical  preferences.  Second, take  repeated  measurements  at  
10:299:25306:20:21:71:543:469:144:Times:144:Times:B:different  points  in  time,  to  describe  the  temporal  stability  of  behavioral  sufficient  statistics  and  
10:300:25421:21:21:71:359:448:144:Times:144:Times:B:account for measurement error in consumer characteristics. 
10:301:25480:21:21:94:543:427:144:Times:144:Times:A:To those ends we administered our surveys to the same set of panelists twice, roughly three 
10:302:25571:21:20:72:543:406:144:Times:144:Times:B:years apart. Each survey round required about one hour of survey time per panelist on average, 
10:303:25666:20:21:72:544:386:144:Times:144:Times:B:with substantial  cross-  panelist  heterogeneity  in  response  time  that  we  observe  and  control  for,  
10:304:25776:21:21:72:343:365:144:Times:144:Times:B:using the ALP’s panelist-question-level “timings” data.  
10:305:25833:21:20:94:543:344:144:Times:144:Times:A:Per standard ALP practice, we paid panelists $10 per completed module. Beyond that, all but 
10:306:25925:20:21:71:543:324:144:Times:144:Times:B:one  of  our  elicitations  are unincentivized  on  the  margin  (limited  prospective  memory  being  the  
10:307:26033:21:21:71:544:303:144:Times:144:Times:B:exception; see Table 1  for details). We made this choice deliberately, based on research  budget  
10:308:26132:21:20:71:540:282:144:Times:144:Times:B:tradeoffs  between  various  approaches  to  dealing  with  measurement  error  and  identification—
10:309:26232:20:21:71:543:262:144:Times:144:Times:B:incentives  vs.  sample  size  vs.  measuring  a  broad  set  of  consumer  characteristics  vs.  repeated 
10:310:26339:21:21:71:542:241:144:Times:144:Times:B:elicitation   over   time—and scrutiny   of   usual   motivations   for   paying   marginal   incentives. 
10:311:26445:21:20:71:543:220:144:Times:144:Times:B:Researchers often hypothesize that subjects find direct elicitation tasks unpleasant and hence need 
10:312:26545:20:21:71:542:200:144:Times:144:Times:B:marginal incentives to engage with the tasks, but the ALP measures panelist engagement and finds 
10:313:26642:21:21:71:543:179:144:Times:144:Times:B:evidence to the contrary.12 Researchers often hypothesize that unincentivized elicitations change 
10:314:26740:21:21:72:543:158:144:Times:144:Times:B:inferences,  but  that  hypothesis  is  not  robustly  supported empirically (e.g.,  Von  Gaudecker,  Van  
10:315:26847:21:33:71:543:137:144:Times:144:Times:B:Soest, and Wengström 2011; Gneezy, Imas, and List 2015). In any case, our repeated elicitations 
10:316:26943:33:17:72:543:104:49:g_d0_f4:121:Times:F:12 For example, each ALP survey ends with “Could you tell us how interesting or uninteresting you found 
10:317:27046:17:13:71:544:87:121:Times:121:Times:F:the  questions  in  this  interview?”  and  roughly  90%  of  our  sample  replies  that  our  modules  are  “Very  
10:318:27162:13:0:71:482:74:121:Times:121:g_d0_f4:F:interesting” or “Interesting,” with only 3% replying “Uninteresting” or “Very uninteresting.” 
11:319:27256:0:20:72:542:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:and measurement error models should suffice to address concerns about noise. Researchers often 
11:320:27351:20:21:72:543:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:assume that marginal incentive mechanisms are the best way to mimic real-world stakes, but this 
11:321:27447:21:21:71:543:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:is not generally true; given research budget constraints, tasks with hypothetical rewards like ours 
11:322:27546:21:21:72:456:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:can offer some conceptual advantages (e.g., Montiel Olea and Strzalecki 2014). 
11:323:27625:21:20:94:543:625:144:Times:144:Times:A:To reduce survey fatigue, we worked with ALP staff to break each round into two separate 
11:324:27714:20:21:72:546:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:30-minute  surveys (modules,  in  ALP  parlance),  offered about  two  weeks  apart  for  most  
11:325:27810:21:21:71:543:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:respondents. Respondents had some flexibility in choosing when to take a survey module once it 
11:326:27905:21:20:72:138:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:was on offer. 
11:327:27919:20:21:94:543:543:144:Times:144:Times:A:After extensive piloting, the ALP fielded our first two Round 1 instruments (ALP modules 
11:328:28008:21:21:71:543:522:144:Times:144:Times:B:315  and  352) starting  in  November  2014.  We targeted 1,500  working-age respondents,  sending 
11:329:28107:21:20:71:545:501:144:Times:144:Times:B:2,103 initial  invitations,  and  ultimately  received  1,515  responses  to  Module  315,  and  1,427  
11:330:28211:20:21:71:542:481:144:Times:144:Times:B:responses to both 315 and 352. 95% of respondents completing both modules did so by the end of 
11:331:28306:21:21:71:542:460:144:Times:144:Times:B:February 2015. We then re-administered those same two modules (with some additional questions 
11:332:28400:21:21:71:543:439:144:Times:144:Times:B:at  the  end,  eliciting  non-cognitive  skills),  seeking  responses  from  the  1,427  panelists  who 
11:333:28504:21:20:72:543:418:144:Times:144:Times:B:completed both Round 1 modules, beginning in October 2017. Of the 1,427, 1308 remained in the 
11:334:28598:20:21:72:543:398:144:Times:144:Times:B:ALP at Round 2 inception. Of those 1,308, we received 967 responses to the first module and 845 
11:335:28694:21:27:72:323:377:144:Times:144:Times:B:responses to both modules (ALP #474 and #472).13  
11:336:28744:27:26:72:302:350:144:Times:144:Times:B:3. B-   Counts: Behavioral sufficient statistics 
11:337:28791:26:21:94:542:324:144:Times:144:Times:A:Here we develop our behavioral sufficient statistics.  We define B-counts motivated by Chetty, 
11:338:28886:21:21:71:543:303:144:Times:144:Times:B:Gabaix and other behavioral models, describe their cross-sectional distributions across panelists, 
11:339:28985:21:26:71:391:282:144:Times:144:Times:B:and estimate their within-consumer persistence across three years. 
11:340:29052:26:21:71:236:256:144:Times:144:Times:B:A. Components of the B-count(s) 
11:341:29083:21:21:94:540:235:144:Times:144:Times:A:Each B-count aggregates information on 2-17 potential sources of behavioral biases, within-
11:342:29174:21:34:71:545:214:144:Times:144:Times:B:consumer.14 A finite  research  budget  forces tradeoffs  between  the  depth  and  breadth  of  bias  
11:343:29277:34:18:72:543:180:49:Times:121:Times:F:13 Modules 352 (Round 1 Module 2) and 472 (Round 2 Module 2) also included invitations to complete a 
11:344:29378:18:12:71:543:162:121:Times:121:Times:F:short  follow-up  survey  the  next  day.  We  use  responses  to  the  invitation  and  actual  next-day behavior to 
11:345:29496:12:8:71:287:150:121:Times:121:Times:F:measure limited memory, as detailed in Table 1. 
11:346:29543:8:18:72:544:142:49:Times:121:Times:F:14 In  previous  work,  based  on  just  our  first  round  of  data,  we  referred  to  potential  sources  of  biases  as  
11:347:29668:18:12:71:543:124:121:Times:121:Times:F:behavioral “factors.” A potential source/factor is e.g.,”money discounting”: we have 17 of these. A bias is 
11:348:29776:12:13:71:543:112:121:Times:121:Times:F:e.g.,  “present-biased  money  discounting”  or  “future-biased  money  discounting,”  measured  in  a  way  that  
11:349:29891:13:12:71:543:99:121:Times:121:Times:F:allows for people to be unbiased: we measure 25 biases, with a maximum of 17 per person because our 
11:350:29991:12:13:71:544:87:121:Times:121:Times:F:elicitations  of  8  of  the  potential  sources  allow  for  mutually  exclusive  bi-directional  biases.  We  use  the  
11:351:30113:13:0:71:326:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:“potential source” label here to keep the focus on biases.  
12:352:30174:0:20:72:543:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:measurements, incentives, and sample size; we prioritized biases that had been linked to financial 
12:353:30273:20:21:72:542:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:decisions in prior work, and measured with elicitation methods that have been featured recently in 
12:354:30372:21:21:72:544:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:top journals, are adaptable to an online environment, and could practicably fit into modules that 
12:355:30470:21:21:71:543:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:would also measure   other decision inputs and outcomes. We do not seek to measure all possible 
12:356:30566:21:20:71:542:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:biases; rather, our focus is on starting with large set (by the standards of behavioral research), and 
12:357:30669:20:21:71:476:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:on identifying whether and how that set and subsets can be empirically informative. 
12:358:30753:21:21:94:545:584:144:Times:144:Times:A:Among  our  17  potential  sources  of  behavioral  biases,  one subset  relates  to  preferences:  
12:359:30853:21:20:71:544:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:present-biased  discounting  (Read  and  van  Leeuwen  1998;  Andreoni  and  Sprenger  2012),  loss  
12:360:30954:20:21:71:542:543:144:Times:144:Times:B:aversion (Fehr and Goette 2007), preference for certainty (Callen et al. 2014), ambiguity aversion 
12:361:31053:21:21:71:542:522:144:Times:144:Times:B:(Dimmock et al. 2016), and choice inconsistency (Choi et al. 2014). Other subsets capture biased 
12:362:31150:21:20:71:542:501:144:Times:144:Times:B:beliefs, biased perceptions, and behavioral decision rules: three varieties of overconfidence (Moore 
12:363:31251:20:21:71:543:481:144:Times:144:Times:B:and  Healy  2008),  narrow  bracketing  (Rabin  and  Weizsäcker  2009),  exponential  growth  biases 
12:364:31352:21:21:72:543:460:144:Times:144:Times:B:(Stango  and  Zinman  2009;  Levy  and Tasoff  2016), statistical fallacies (Dohmen  et  al.  2009;  D.  
12:365:31457:21:21:72:545:439:144:Times:144:Times:B:Benjamin,  Moore,  and  Rabin  2017;  D.  Benjamin,  Rabin,  and  Raymond  2016),  and  limited  
12:366:31554:21:20:72:248:418:144:Times:144:Times:B:attention/memory (Ericson 2011).15  
12:367:31590:20:21:94:542:398:144:Times:144:Times:A:Table 1 summarizes our 17 potential sources of biases, along with our elicitation methods and 
12:368:31684:21:21:71:543:377:144:Times:144:Times:B:their  antecedents. Each  bias  is  identified  relative  to  the  classical  benchmark  established  in  prior  
12:369:31796:21:20:72:547:356:144:Times:144:Times:B:work  (e.g.,  time-consistent  discounting,  consistency  with  the  General  Axiom  of  Revealed  
12:370:31895:20:21:72:544:336:144:Times:144:Times:B:Preference,  unbiased  beliefs  about  one’s  own  performance,  unbiased  perceptions  of  statistical  
12:371:32000:21:21:72:542:315:144:Times:144:Times:B:properties,  etc.).  The Data  Appendix Section  1  provides  details  on  each  of  the  17,  including 
12:372:32105:21:20:72:542:294:144:Times:144:Times:B:granular data descriptions, comparisons of data quality indicators and descriptive statistics to prior 
12:373:32208:20:21:72:544:274:144:Times:144:Times:B:work, and  discussions  of  prior  theory  and  evidence  linking  each  behavioral  bias  to  consumer  
12:374:32313:21:21:72:192:253:144:Times:144:Times:B:decisions and outcomes. 
12:375:32337:21:27:72:352:232:144:Times:144:Times:B:B. B-counts and sub-counts: Definitions and motivations 
12:376:32392:27:20:94:551:205:144:Times:144:Times:A:B-   counts  classify  behavioral  biases  simply:  for  each  of  the  17  potential  sources  of  bias  we  
12:377:32502:20:21:72:540:185:144:Times:144:Times:B:measure, we classify a consumer as displaying a bias (1) or not (0). A B-count simply sums, within-
12:378:32601:21:60:71:546:164:144:Times:144:Times:B:consumer,  a number  of  biases  exhibited.  This  approach  to  creating  summary  statistics  is  
12:379:32701:60:17:72:544:104:49:Times:121:Times:F:15 Following  a  common  delineation  in  behavioral  economics,  we  do  not  measure  social  preferences.  See  
12:380:32816:17:13:71:543:87:121:Times:121:Times:F:Dean and Ortoleva (2018) and Chapman et al. (2018a) for evidence on relationships between behavioral 
12:381:32917:13:0:72:205:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:biases and social preferences. 
13:382:32949:0:20:72:542:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:transparent and easy to implement, here and in future work. (We consider alternate, and potentially 
13:383:33049:20:21:72:320:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:more efficient, approaches in subsequent sections.) 
13:384:33101:21:21:94:543:667:144:Times:144:Times:A:As discussed in Section 1, one can view a behavioral sufficient statistic as a within-consumer 
13:385:33196:21:21:72:542:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:aggregation of many different behavioral influences, or as a psychological underpinning for many 
13:386:33293:21:20:72:441:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:different behavioral biases. Different B-counts nod to these different views.  
13:387:33372:20:21:94:543:605:144:Times:144:Times:A:Our Full B-count is  a broad Chetty-type aggregation of all 17 of our potential sources of bias. 
13:388:33468:21:21:72:543:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:Figures 1a and 1b provide the first of many indications that B-counts capture information that is 
13:389:33566:21:20:72:543:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:largely distinct from classical decision inputs. Figure 1a plots raw, consumer-level variation in the 
13:390:33668:20:21:72:546:543:144:Times:144:Times:B:“B-proportion”:  the  share  of  our  17  biases  a  consumer  exhibits.  Figure  1b  plots  consumer- level 
13:391:33777:21:21:72:542:522:144:Times:144:Times:B:residuals from regressing the B-proportion on the complete set of other covariates in our data (see 
13:392:33877:21:20:72:542:501:144:Times:144:Times:B:Appendix Table 1 for the list). These residuals are rescaled to the mean of the raw B-proportion in 
13:393:33977:20:21:72:539:481:144:Times:144:Times:B:Figure 1a for comparability. Comparing the figures illustrates how little variation in the Full B-
13:394:34075:21:21:72:543:460:144:Times:144:Times:B:count  is  explained  by  our  complete  set  of  other  covariates;  although  partialing  out  variation 
13:395:34182:21:21:71:543:439:144:Times:144:Times:B:explained by other covariates produces a more normal B-count distribution, it does little to reduce 
13:396:34282:21:20:71:499:418:144:Times:144:Times:B:dispersion (the raw vs. residualized interquartile ranges are [0.56, 0.75] vs. [0.59, 0.74]). 
13:397:34376:20:21:94:544:398:144:Times:144:Times:A:Our Sparsity  B-counts  aggregate subsets  of  potential  biases motivated  by  the  foundational  
13:398:34475:21:21:71:539:377:144:Times:144:Times:B:role of the limited attention/memory parameter in Gabaix’s models.16 Our “Narrow Sparsity” B-
13:399:34568:21:20:72:543:356:144:Times:144:Times:B:count sums only limited attention and limited memory. Besides mapping fairly neatly from theory, 
13:400:34665:20:21:72:542:336:144:Times:144:Times:B:the Narrow Sparsity B-count has the added benefit of being easy and quick to elicit; this is reflected 
13:401:34768:21:21:72:542:315:144:Times:144:Times:B:in  the  elicitation  time  statistics  in  Table  2  Column  5  and  discussed  in  Section  7.  The  “Broad 
13:402:34878:21:20:72:543:294:144:Times:144:Times:B:Sparsity” B-count adds six more biases that can emerge from limited attention/memory in Gabaix’s 
13:403:34975:20:21:71:542:274:144:Times:144:Times:B:models:  our  two  measures  of  present-biased  discounting  (for  money  and  for  consumption),  and 
13:404:35079:21:21:72:540:253:144:Times:144:Times:B:our four measures of price misperceptions and statistical biases: exponential growth biases, non-
13:405:35176:21:21:72:382:232:144:Times:144:Times:B:belief in the law of large numbers, and the gambler’s fallacies.17 
13:406:35242:21:20:94:542:211:144:Times:144:Times:A:The four price misperception and statistical biases are what we call math biases. They have 
13:407:35334:20:21:72:543:191:144:Times:144:Times:B:objectively correct  answers,  but  they  do  not  simply measure  math  mistakes or  cognitive  skills, 
13:408:35439:21:28:72:543:170:144:Times:144:Times:B:because they are tendencies to err in a particular direction. As an example, work on Exponential 
13:409:35536:28:18:72:544:142:49:Times:121:Times:F:16 Gabaix  describes  limited  attention  as  a  “central,  unifying  theme  for  much  of  behavioral  economics”  
13:410:35652:18:12:71:543:124:121:Times:121:Times:F:(2019, p. 1). He does not explicitly mention limited memory, but it is implicit: a consumer might fail to 
13:411:35758:12:8:71:358:112:121:Times:121:Times:F:“consider” an economic variable by forgetting it, and vice versa. 
13:412:35823:8:17:72:542:104:49:Times:121:Times:F:17 Narrow bracketing also seems very much in the spirit of the Gabaix models, but we do not include it in 
13:413:35929:17:13:71:544:87:121:Times:121:Times:F:our  Broad  Sparsity  B-count  because  we  could  not  find  any  mention  of  it  in  Gabaix’s  papers.  Several  
13:414:36045:13:0:71:465:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:untabulated robustness checks suggest that including it would not change our inferences. 
14:415:36135:0:20:72:543:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:Growth Bias shows that more people underestimate the effects of compounding than overestimate 
14:416:36229:20:21:72:543:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:it, and that people who underestimate it do so systematically across a range of financial decisions, 
14:417:36330:21:21:72:542:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:with plausibly welfare-reducing consequences (Stango and Zinman 2009; Levy and Tasoff 2016). 
14:418:36423:21:21:72:543:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:So, EG biases are not just mathematical mistakes  : they are biases. Limited math/cognitive skills, 
14:419:36523:21:20:72:543:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:on the other hand, generate mistakes that are non-systematic, mean-zero, and hence less likely to 
14:420:36620:20:21:72:542:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:push people toward particular decisions (such as less saving and more borrowing) on average. We 
14:421:36716:21:21:72:543:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:draw the distinction for two reasons. One is to confirm that our non-math biases are empirically 
14:422:36813:21:20:72:543:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:relevant, and that math biases alone do not drive our observed correlations between B-counts and 
14:423:36910:20:21:72:542:543:144:Times:144:Times:B:outcomes (Section 5-C). We also conduct a variety of other empirical tests in Sections 5-B and 6 
14:424:37007:21:21:72:543:522:144:Times:144:Times:B:to  confirm  that  the  math  biases  themselves  are distinct  from  numeracy  and  other math-related 
14:425:37111:21:20:72:154:501:144:Times:144:Times:B:cognitive skills.  
14:426:37130:20:21:94:543:481:144:Times:144:Times:A:Each of our 17 behavioral biases has an expected direction emphasized in prior work (Table 
14:427:37221:21:21:72:542:460:144:Times:144:Times:B:1 Column 3; details in Data Appendix Section 1); e.g., present-bias or underestimating exponential 
14:428:37320:21:21:72:540:439:144:Times:144:Times:B:growth is expected while future-bias or overestimating EG is not.18 Below we detail how expected-
14:429:37417:21:20:71:543:418:144:Times:144:Times:B:direction  biases  actually  are  more  prevalent  (Section 3-D),  and  more  strongly  correlated  with 
14:430:37522:20:21:71:367:398:144:Times:144:Times:B:outcomes (Section 5-C), than non-expected direction biases.  
14:431:37583:21:21:94:543:377:144:Times:144:Times:A:Our third B-sub-count couplet (besides math vs. non-math, and expected vs. non-expected) is 
14:432:37674:21:20:72:542:356:144:Times:144:Times:B:preference vs. non-preference B-sub-counts. The latter includes   biases pertaining to beliefs, price 
14:433:37776:20:21:72:547:336:144:Times:144:Times:B:perceptions,   and   problem-solving   approaches. The   former   includes   our   two   measures   of   
14:434:37881:21:21:72:546:315:144:Times:144:Times:B:discounting  biases,19 ambiguity  aversion,  loss  aversion/small-stakes  risk  aversion,  our  two  
14:435:37982:21:21:72:543:294:144:Times:144:Times:B:measures  of  inconsistency  with  GARP  and  dominance  avoidance,  and  preference  for  certainty. 
14:436:38084:21:20:72:543:273:144:Times:144:Times:B:The mapping from preference biases to welfare implications is less clear than for non-preference 
14:437:38181:20:27:72:419:253:144:Times:144:Times:B:biases, both theoretically and empirically, as we discuss in Section 5-C. 
14:438:38255:27:27:72:307:226:144:Times:144:Times:B:C. B-counts are stable within-person, over time 
14:439:38302:27:20:94:539:199:144:Times:144:Times:A:Table  2  Column  7  reports  estimates  of  B-count  temporal  stability  (correlations)  round-to-
14:440:38402:20:63:71:543:179:144:Times:144:Times:B:round, within-person over our three-year sample period. Such correlations are important because 
14:441:38498:63:13:72:468:116:49:Times:121:Times:F:18 Chapman et al. (2018a) also find that expected direction biases are relatively prevalent. 
14:442:38590:13:17:72:543:103:49:Times:121:Times:F:19 Keeping in mind that discounting is more than time preference per se: it is a reduced-form combination 
14:443:38696:17:0:71:339:86:121:Times:121:Times:F:of preferences, expectations, and (perceived) rates of return. 
15:444:38760:0:20:72:545:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:we  use  within-person  stability  in  B-counts  to  deal  with  measurement  error  when  estimating  
15:445:38863:20:21:72:525:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:correlations between outcomes (measures of experienced utility) and B-counts (Section 4-C).  
15:446:38956:21:21:94:542:667:144:Times:144:Times:A:The Full B-count has a within-person correlation of 0.44 across the two rounds, which is high 
15:447:39050:21:21:72:543:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:by psychometric standards; given measurement error, even a measure that captures a stable trait is 
15:448:39149:21:20:72:550:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:likely to have serial correlation below one.20 Within-sample, the Full B-   count is more stable than 
15:449:39251:20:21:72:543:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:our measure of patience, has about the same stability as our measures of risk aversion, and is less 
15:450:39351:21:21:72:543:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:stable than our measures of cognitive skills.21 (It is an open question whether cognitive skills are 
15:451:39452:21:21:71:542:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:truly more stable (trait-like), and/or just measured more accurately. The latter explanation seems 
15:452:39551:21:20:72:543:542:144:Times:144:Times:B:quite likely from a “testing” perspective, given that researchers have devoted orders of magnitude 
15:453:39650:20:21:72:544:522:144:Times:144:Times:B:more  effort  to  refining  measures  of  cognitive  skills  than  to  refining  the  measures  of  behavioral  
15:454:39762:21:21:72:170:501:144:Times:144:Times:B:biases we use here.) 
15:455:39783:21:20:94:544:480:144:Times:144:Times:A:The  B-sub-counts  have  estimated round-to-round  within-person  correlations  ranging  from  
15:456:39878:20:21:72:542:460:144:Times:144:Times:B:0.18 to 0.49 (Table 2 Column 7). Two comparisons between B-sub-count couplets are particularly 
15:457:39973:21:21:71:543:439:144:Times:144:Times:B:noteworthy. Expected direction biases are more than twice as stable as non-expected ones (0.41 
15:458:40068:21:20:72:543:418:144:Times:144:Times:B:vs. 0.18), suggesting that expected biases are more trait-like and/or easier to measure accurately. 
15:459:40168:20:21:72:544:398:144:Times:144:Times:B:And  non-preference  biases  are  more  than  twice  as  stable as  preference  biases (0.49  vs.  0.23),  
15:460:40275:21:21:72:539:377:144:Times:144:Times:B:despite us devoting more time to measuring preference biases (3 minutes per vs. 1 minute per non-
15:461:40372:21:26:72:154:356:144:Times:144:Times:B:preference bias). 
15:462:40389:26:27:72:252:330:144:Times:144:Times:B:D. B-count distributional properties 
15:463:40425:27:21:94:542:303:144:Times:144:Times:A:Table 2 presents additional descriptive statistics for B-counts within and across our two survey 
15:464:40522:21:21:72:543:282:144:Times:144:Times:B:rounds. The mean and “share>0” columns address the prevalence of biases, showing whether our 
15:465:40615:21:43:72:543:261:144:Times:144:Times:B:data  match  theoretical assumptions  of behavioral  sufficient  statistic  models (Section 3-E). The 
15:466:40717:43:18:72:543:218:49:Times:121:Times:F:20 In the absence of prior work measuring the stability of behavioral summary statistics, the most relevant 
15:467:40825:18:12:71:543:200:121:Times:121:Times:F:out-of-sample  comparisons  are  studies  of  the  temporal  stability  of  single  behavioral  biases.  Meier  and  
15:468:40942:12:13:71:544:188:121:Times:121:Times:F:Sprenger  (2015)  finds  a  one-year  within-person  correlation  of  0.36  for  a  short-run  money  discounting  
15:469:41057:13:13:71:543:175:121:Times:121:Times:F:parameter that is strongly present-biased on average. Chapman et al.  (2018b) finds a 6-month within-person 
15:470:41165:13:12:71:543:162:121:Times:121:Times:F:correlation of 0.21 for a measure of ambiguity aversion. Chapman et al. (2018a) elicits multiple measures 
15:471:41271:12:13:71:544:150:121:Times:121:Times:F:(“duplicates”)  of  12  biases  that  are  conceptually  similar  to  ours,  at  a  single  point  in  time,  and  finds  an  
15:472:41397:13:8:71:485:137:121:Times:121:Times:F:average within-bias, within-person, across-measure correlation of about 0.6 (our calculation). 
15:473:41491:8:17:72:542:129:49:Times:121:Times:F:21 More specifically, some of the relevant within-person round-to-round correlations in our sample are: 0.30 
15:474:41600:17:13:71:543:112:121:Times:121:Times:F:for patience, 0.58 for the Dohmen et al. (2010, 2011) measure of risk aversion, 0.32 for the Barsky et al. 
15:475:41707:13:12:71:543:99:121:Times:121:Times:F:(1997) measure of risk aversion, 0.75 for the number series measure of fluid intelligence, and 0.70 for the 
15:476:41815:12:13:72:548:87:121:Times:121:Times:F:first principal component of our four cognitive skills test scores. See Section 4-B and Appendix Table 1   for 
15:477:41926:13:0:72:233:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:details on these variable definitions. 
16:478:41966:0:20:72:542:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:standard deviations describe cross-consumer heterogeneity; we examine how this heterogeneity is 
16:479:42062:20:21:72:545:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:conditionally  correlated  with  the  cross-section  of  consumer  outcomes  (experienced  utility)  in  
16:480:42167:21:21:72:543:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:Section 5. The “median mins survey time” illustrates how long it takes panelists, on average, to 
16:481:42264:21:21:72:543:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:supply  enough  information  to  calculate  the  B-count  in  question. That  information  is  useful  for  
16:482:42372:21:20:72:542:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:researchers and practitioners interested in measuring B-counts. Note that our measured survey time 
16:483:42471:20:21:72:544:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:is  an  upper  bound on  the  true  time  panelists  spend  on  our  elicitations,  because respondents  can  
16:484:42581:21:21:71:520:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:take breaks that are imperfectly captured by the ALP’s click-to-click measures of time spent. 
16:485:42674:21:20:94:543:563:144:Times:144:Times:A:Table 2 Panel A describes the Full B-count. The mean panelist exhibits about 10 biases out

16:490:43158:21:21:72:543:460:144:Times:144:Times:B:exhibits the maximum possible 17 (Column 4). The standard deviation is roughly 2 on a mean of 
16:491:43252:21:21:72:544:439:144:Times:144:Times:B:10,  suggesting  that  consumers  exhibit  multiple  behavioral  biases  even  on  the  low  end  of  the  
16:492:43359:21:20:72:543:418:144:Times:144:Times:B:distribution. Median survey time for eliciting the full B-count is about 34 minutes (Column 5); we

16:494:43492:21:21:94:543:377:144:Times:144:Times:A:Table 2 Panel B shows that our lower-dimensional Sparsity B-counts are also prevalent. The

16:496:43691:20:21:72:543:336:144:Times:144:Times:B:Critically, the Narrow Sparsity B-count only takes about a minute of survey/task time to measure

16:498:43885:21:20:72:542:294:144:Times:144:Times:B:5), suggests that measuring the Narrow Sparsity B-count could be a valuable and practical addition 
16:499:43984:20:21:71:543:274:144:Times:144:Times:B:to many studies of consumer decision making. The Broad Sparsity B-count takes on a value greater 
16:500:44081:21:21:71:542:253:144:Times:144:Times:B:than  or  equal  to  one  for nearly everyone  in  our  sample, with  a  mean  of  roughly  4.2 out  of  a 
16:501:44187:21:26:71:279:232:144:Times:144:Times:B:maximum possible 8 biases and SD of 1.3. 
16:502:44228:26:21:94:542:206:144:Times:144:Times:A:Table 2 Panel C describes our three B-sub-count couplets. Expected-direction biases are far 
16:503:44320:21:21:71:543:185:144:Times:144:Times:B:more  prevalent  than  non-expected  ones:  the  Expected-Direction  B-sub-count mean  is nearly  as  
16:504:44422:21:21:71:543:164:144:Times:144:Times:B:high as the Full B-count (roughly 8.5, with a SD of about 2) while the Non-expected mean is only 
16:505:44519:21:20:72:544:143:144:Times:144:Times:B:1.5.  And  while  nearly  everyone  exhibits  multiple  expected-direction  biases,  roughly  15%  our  
16:506:44623:20:21:72:543:123:144:Times:144:Times:B:sample exhibits  zero non-expected biases (out of a possible 8). Expected-direction biases drive the 
16:507:44724:21:21:72:543:102:144:Times:144:Times:B:Full B-count, in that the two are correlated 0.87; in contrast, the Non-expected Direction B-count 
16:508:44823:21:0:72:543:81:144:Times:144:Times:B:correlation  with  the  Full is  only  0.26.  Both  math  and  non-math  biases  are prevalent  and 
17:509:44925:0:20:72:542:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:heterogeneous,  with  cross-sectional  variation  in  the  Full  B-count  driven  less  by the  Math

17:514:45409:26:21:94:542:599:144:Times:144:Times:A:Table 2 Panel D suggests that item non-response does not overly complicate interpretation of

17:517:45705:20:21:72:543:537:144:Times:144:Times:B:more bias questions. Below we control directly for the missing B-count inputs and other measures 
17:518:45802:21:21:72:221:516:144:Times:144:Times:B:of survey effort (Section 4-A).

17:521:45956:27:27:72:424:448:144:Times:144:Times:B:E. Interpreting B-count descriptive statistics: Implications for modeling 
17:522:46028:27:21:94:542:421:144:Times:144:Times:A:Table  2  speaks  to  various  assumptions  underlying  behavioral  sufficient  statistic  models. 
17:523:46127:21:20:72:542:400:144:Times:144:Times:B:Behavioral tendencies are more common than rare, as in models with a behavioral representative 
17:524:46222:20:21:72:542:380:144:Times:144:Times:B:agent a la Gabaix. The “psychological foundations” of the sparsity models (limited attention and 
17:525:46318:21:21:72:544:359:144:Times:144:Times:B:limited  memory,  summarized  in  our  Narrow  Sparsity  B-count)  are  indeed  prevalent,  as  are  the  
17:526:46424:21:20:72:544:338:144:Times:144:Times:B:“behavioral phenomena” that can emerge from sparsity models (price misperceptions, statistical 
17:527:46519:20:21:72:543:318:144:Times:144:Times:B:fallacies,  and  present-biases,  summarized  in  our  Broad  Sparsity  B-count).  Biases  are more 
17:528:46619:21:21:72:544:297:144:Times:144:Times:B:common in expected than non-expected directions and hence not mean-zero, as required in e.g., 
17:529:46713:21:20:72:331:276:144:Times:144:Times:B:Allcott and Taubinsky (2015) and the Chetty models. 
17:530:46765:20:21:94:545:256:144:Times:144:Times:A:Along  with  informing  modeling  assumptions, our  empirical  sufficient  statistics  could  be  
17:531:46863:21:21:72:543:235:144:Times:144:Times:B:useful for estimating model inputs. B-counts clearly speak to the prevalence of behavioral agents 
17:532:46961:21:20:72:543:214:144:Times:144:Times:B:(Mullainathan, Schwartzstein, and Congdon 2012), and B-counts and their components could be 
17:533:47053:20:21:72:543:194:144:Times:144:Times:B:useful for estimating the average marginal bias distribution: the number of behavioral agents on a 
17:534:47152:21:21:72:542:173:144:Times:144:Times:B:given margin and the extent of their biases. Relatedly, one property not exhibited by our B-counts 
17:535:47251:21:21:72:543:152:144:Times:144:Times:B:is  the  homogeneity  in  person-level  bias  required  to  use  Chetty,  Looney,  and  Kroft’s  (2009) 
17:536:47355:21:20:72:543:131:144:Times:144:Times:B:equivalent price metric to identify the average marginal bias distribution used for welfare analysis. 
17:537:47457:20:21:72:545:111:144:Times:144:Times:B:Allowing  for  heterogeneity  can  considerably  complicate  modeling  policy  welfare  effects  with  
17:538:47560:21:0:72:545:90:144:Times:144:Times:B:behavioral  sufficient  statistics  (Allcott  and  Taubinsky  2015;  Mullainathan,  Schwartzstein,  and  
18:539:47667:0:20:72:543:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:Congdon 2012), and the rest of our paper focuses on understanding that heterogeneity and how 
18:540:47760:20:21:72:543:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:one  can  use  it  to  help  test  what  is  arguably  the  most  fundamental  assumption  of  behavioral 
18:541:47866:21:27:71:511:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:sufficient statistic models: that behavioral biases, taken together, reduce consumer welfare. 
18:542:47959:27:27:71:494:640:144:Times:144:Times:B:4. Using B-counts to Model the Wedge Between Decision and Experienced Utility 
18:543:48035:27:20:94:543:613:144:Times:144:Times:A:B-counts have the summary properties of a behavioral sufficient statistic, so we now ask the 
18:544:48128:20:21:72:544:593:144:Times:144:Times:B:more  important  question:  can  B-counts help  identify  any  behavioral  wedge  between  decision  
18:545:48229:21:21:72:494:572:144:Times:144:Times:B:utility and experienced utility? We address this question by formalizing how we map... 
18:546:48316:21:21:216:398:551:144:g_d0_f9:144:Times:A:(1) 푉푉푖푖퐸퐸(푝푝푖푖,푤푤푖푖)=푉푉푖푖퐷퐷(푝푝푖푖,푤푤푖푖)−푓푓(휃휃푖푖) 
18:547:48363:21:22:72:543:530:144:Times:144:Times:B:into our data. We pay particular attention to identifying assumptions given measurement error in 
18:548:48460:22:21:72:543:508:144:Times:144:Times:B:one  or  more  of  its  three objects:  experienced  utility  푉푉푖푖퐸퐸,  decision  utility  푉푉푖푖퐷퐷,  and  any  behavioral  
18:549:48581:21:27:72:220:487:144:Times:144:Times:B:wedge between the two 푓푓(휃휃푖푖). 
18:550:48613:27:27:72:356:460:144:Times:144:Times:B:A. Empirical specification: Measuring experienced utility 
18:551:48669:27:22:94:543:433:144:Times:144:Times:A:Our  approach  to  the  left  hand  side  of  (1)  is  to  consider  individual-level outcomes 푌푌푖푖푎푎 
18:552:48770:22:21:72:543:411:144:Times:144:Times:B:measuring various  aspects of  experienced  utility  푉푉푖푖퐸퐸,  following Benjamin  et  al.  (2014)  and 
18:553:48873:21:22:72:543:390:144:Times:144:Times:B:Benjamin et al. (2014). We focus on financial measures here, describe measures of other aspects 
18:554:48969:22:21:72:543:368:144:Times:144:Times:B:in  Section  5-D,  and  consider  relationships  between  aspects  and  overall  푉푉푖푖퐸퐸 in  Section  4-D. We 
18:555:49077:21:21:72:546:347:144:Times:144:Times:B:scale all outcomes on the [0,1] interval, with higher values indicating better outcomes (Table 3).22  
18:556:49179:21:21:94:539:326:144:Times:144:Times:A:Our primary outcome is an index of subjective financial condition—a   financial aspect of 푉푉푖푖퐸퐸—
18:557:49275:21:21:72:542:305:144:Times:144:Times:B:that averages responses to four sets of questions about retirement savings adequacy, non-retirement 
18:558:49375:21:21:72:542:284:144:Times:144:Times:B:savings adequacy, overall financial satisfaction, and financial stress.23 The four index components 
18:559:49475:21:20:72:542:263:144:Times:144:Times:B:correlate strongly  and  positively  with  each  other  (Appendix  Table  3  Panel  B):  the  pairwise 
18:560:49578:20:51:72:386:243:144:Times:144:Times:B:correlations range from 0.31 to 0.53, each with p-values < 0.001. 
18:561:49644:51:17:72:542:192:49:Times:121:Times:F:22 Re-scaling provides comparability, and we chose the [0, 1] scale because most of our outcome variables 
18:562:49750:17:13:71:542:175:121:Times:121:Times:F:are either indicators or summary indexes. We do not standardize, because dividing a variable by its standard 
18:563:49859:13:8:71:506:162:121:Times:121:Times:F:deviation can introduce additional measurement error (Gillen, Snowberg, and Yariv forthcoming). 
18:564:49955:8:17:72:544:154:49:Times:121:Times:F:23 We  drew  the  content  and  wording  for  our  financial  condition  questions  from  previous  American  Life  
18:565:50071:17:13:71:542:137:121:Times:121:Times:F:Panel modules and other surveys (including the National Longitudinal Surveys, the Survey of Consumer 
18:566:50172:13:12:71:542:124:121:Times:121:Times:F:Finances, the National Survey of American Families, the Survey of Forces, and the World Values Survey). 
18:567:50276:12:13:71:544:112:121:Times:121:Times:F:Each  of  our  outcomes  is  quick  and  easy  to  measure:  Appendix  Table  3  and  Table  3  show  that  each  
18:568:50390:13:12:71:542:99:121:Times:121:Times:F:individual/component outcome takes strictly less than a minute to elicit on average, and that even our most 
18:569:50498:12:13:71:543:87:121:Times:121:Times:F:elaborate  index  has  a  median  elicitation  time  of  only  2.67  minutes.  Further  details  on  outcome  variable  
18:570:50618:13:0:71:385:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:definitions can be found in the notes to Table 3 and Appendix Table 3. 
19:571:50690:0:20:94:545:708:144:Times:144:Times:A:We also  measure objective  financial  condition  by averaging  five  indicators:  positive  net  
19:572:50788:20:21:71:542:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:worth, owning retirement assets, owning stocks, having saved over the past 12 months, and not

19:574:50980:21:21:71:543:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:positively correlated with each other: the range is 0.35 to 0.56 (Appendix Table 3 Panel A). The 
19:575:51077:21:21:71:410:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:objective index is correlated 0.57 with the subjective index (Table 3).  
19:576:51150:21:21:94:542:604:144:Times:144:Times:A:Our empirics allow that any 푌푌푖푖푎푎 measures 푉푉푖푖퐸퐸 with error. Putting aside issues with aggregating

19:584:51863:27:28:71:221:430:144:Times:144:Times:B:B. Measuring decision utility  
19:585:51893:28:21:94:543:402:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  specify  decision  utility  푉푉푖푖퐷퐷 following  Chetty’s  (2015) Method  2,  by  constructing  a  rich  
19:586:51999:21:20:71:542:381:144:Times:144:Times:B:vector of classical decision inputs thought to affect utility in the absence of behavioral biases (see 
19:587:52102:20:21:71:543:361:144:Times:144:Times:B:also, e.g., Allcott and Taubinsky). The inputs of interest here are (life-cycle) demographics such 
19:588:52201:21:21:71:542:340:144:Times:144:Times:B:as income, gender, age, education, etc. (Demoi), patience and risk tolerance (Prefi), and cognitive 
19:589:52301:21:21:72:543:319:144:Times:144:Times:B:and non-cognitive skills (Cogi and NonCogi).  Altogether we measure 20 consumer characteristics 
19:590:52396:21:20:72:545:298:144:Times:144:Times:B:with  121  variables  (many  of  them  categorical,  see  Appendix  Table  1).26 We  allow  for  the  
19:591:52498:20:36:72:352:278:144:Times:144:Times:B:possibility that decision utility too is measured with error: 
19:592:52559:36:17:72:544:242:49:Times:121:Times:F:24 Specifically,  we  measure  respondent  survey  effort  with  three  types  of  variables.  One  is  the  count  of  
19:593:52679:17:12:71:540:225:121:Times:121:Times:F:missing  inputs  to  our  B-count,  as  described  in  Section  2-D.  The  second  type  is  indicators  for  item  non-
19:594:52799:12:13:71:542:213:121:Times:121:Times:F:response, for elicitations with non-trivial item non-response rates. In our main analysis sample, these rates 
19:595:52909:13:13:71:543:200:121:Times:121:Times:F:range from zero for many demographics, to 5% for Stroop. Our third measure of survey effort is based on 
19:596:53013:13:12:71:543:187:121:Times:121:Times:F:the ALP’s tracking of a panelist’s time spent on each screen. We use decile indicators of survey time spent 
19:597:53121:12:9:71:530:175:121:Times:121:Times:F:per survey round, either overall across both of our modules, or counting just our behavioral elicitations. 
19:598:53227:9:17:72:544:166:49:Times:121:Times:F:25 In  untabulated  results,  we  also  have  explored  whether  shorter/longer  survey  times  are  related  to  the  
19:599:53346:17:12:71:542:149:121:Times:121:Times:F:variance of reported well-being. As an example, we have estimated our models excluding the top and/or 
19:600:53448:12:13:71:543:137:121:Times:121:Times:F:bottom deciles of survey response time, or by using survey response times as weights. Neither approach 
19:601:53551:13:8:71:249:124:121:Times:121:Times:F:has a meaningful effect on the findings. 
19:602:53591:8:17:72:542:116:49:Times:121:Times:F:26 Including such a rich set of classical covariates might over-control if classical covariates are correlated 
19:603:53701:17:13:71:542:99:121:Times:121:Times:F:with behavioral tendencies, but we show below that in practice our estimated links between B-counts and 
19:604:53805:13:0:71:394:86:121:Times:121:Times:F:experienced utility are quite robust to the set of covariates (Section 5-B).
