20:606:53947:22:21:94:545:685:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  measure  demographics  using  the  ALP’s  standard  set,  collected  when  a  panelist  first  
20:607:54046:21:21:72:542:664:144:Times:144:Times:B:registers  and  refreshed  quarterly.  We  measure  the  other  elements  of   푓푓(. ) with  widely-used 
20:608:54149:21:21:72:553:643:144:Times:144:Times:B:elicitations incorporated     into our     modules. We     measure     presumed-classical     risk     
20:609:54251:21:20:72:544:622:144:Times:144:Times:B:attitudes/preferences using the adaptive lifetime income gamble task developed by Barsky et al. 
20:610:54347:20:21:72:541:602:144:Times:144:Times:B:(1997), and the financial risk-taking scale from Dohmen et al. (2010, 2011).27 We measure patience 
20:611:54446:21:21:72:543:581:144:Times:144:Times:B:using the average savings rate across the 24 choices in our version of the Convex Time Budget 
20:612:54540:21:20:72:543:560:144:Times:144:Times:B:task  (Andreoni  and  Sprenger  2012).  We  measure  cognitive  skills  using  4  standard  tests for 
20:613:54642:20:21:71:544:540:144:Times:144:Times:B:general/fluid  intelligence (McArdle,  Fisher,  and  Kadlec  2007),  numeracy  (Banks  and  Oldfield  
20:614:54744:21:21:71:544:519:144:Times:144:Times:B:2007), financial literacy (crystalized intelligence for financial decision making) per Lusardi and 
20:615:54843:21:21:71:542:498:144:Times:144:Times:B:Mitchell (2014),   and   executive   function/working   memory (MacLeod   1991).28 Pairwise 
20:616:54935:21:20:72:545:477:144:Times:144:Times:B:correlations  between  these  four  test  scores  range  from  0.16  to  0.42.  In  our  second  round  of  
20:617:55043:20:21:72:490:457:144:Times:144:Times:B:surveying we add elicitations of noncognitive skills to the end of our second module.29 
20:618:55130:21:21:94:542:436:144:Times:144:Times:A:In some specifications, we add the objective financial index to 푓푓(. ). This makes conceptual 
20:619:55224:21:21:71:543:415:144:Times:144:Times:B:sense if, as is likely the case, financial resources are an important component of the consumer’s 
20:620:55322:21:21:71:544:394:144:Times:144:Times:B:endowment 푤푤푖푖 and/or affect prices 푝푝푖푖. In any case,  conditioning  on  financial  resources provides 
20:621:55426:21:21:72:542:373:144:Times:144:Times:B:an even more stringent test of the relationship between subjective financial condition and 휃휃푖푖, albeit 
20:622:55530:21:27:72:286:352:144:Times:144:Times:B:one that errs on the side of over-controlling. 
20:623:55577:27:26:72:477:325:144:Times:144:Times:B:C. Measuring any behavioral wedge and accounting for the overall error structure 
20:624:55656:26:21:94:543:299:144:Times:144:Times:A:Completing our empirical specification requires a measure of the behavioral sufficient statistic 
20:625:55752:21:22:72:542:278:144:g_d0_f8:144:Times:B:휃휃푖푖, and a estimator that helps account for measurement error. We measure 휃휃푖푖 using our one or more 
20:626:55854:22:21:71:340:256:144:Times:144:Times:B:of our B-counts, again allowing for measurement error: 
20:627:55908:21:43:260:354:235:144:g_d0_f8:144:Times:A:퐵퐵퐵퐵퐷퐷푆푆푁푁퐵퐵푖푖=휃휃푖푖+휐휐푖푖 
20:628:55932:43:17:72:542:192:49:Times:121:Times:F:27 These Barsky and Dohmen et al. measures are correlated 0.14 in our main analysis sample. We also elicit 
20:629:56039:17:9:71:472:175:121:Times:121:Times:F:Dohmen et al.’s general risk taking scale, which is correlated 0.68 with the financial scale. 
20:630:56132:9:12:72:408:166:49:Times:121:Times:F:28 The Data Appendix Section 2 provides details on each of these measures. 
20:631:56206:12:17:72:542:154:49:Times:121:Times:F:29 Specifically, we use the validated 10-item version of the Big Five personality trait inventory (Rammstedt 
20:632:56315:17:13:71:543:137:121:Times:121:Times:F:and John 2007). We initially decided against eliciting non-cognitve skills, given our resource constraints 
20:633:56422:13:13:71:542:124:121:Times:121:Times:F:and the lack of prior evidence of the correlations between them and behavioral biases (see, e.g., Becker et 
20:634:56530:13:12:71:544:111:121:Times:121:Times:F:al’s (2012)  review  article)  that  would  be  required  to  confound  our  key  inferences.  But  in  Round  2  we  
20:635:56648:12:13:71:542:99:121:Times:121:Times:F:decided to err on the side of caution and take some non-cognitive skills measures, after seeing Kuhnen and 
20:636:56755:13:0:71:492:86:121:Times:121:Times:F:Melzer’s (2018) evidence on correlations between financial outcomes and non-cognitive skills.

21:646:57204:14:0:446:468:653:64:g_d0_f8:64:g_d0_f8:F:푒푒푒푒푒푒푒푒푒푒
21:647:57214:0:38:500:503:667:121:Times:121:Times:F: 
21:648:57214:38:22:94:542:629:144:Times:144:Times:A:The coefficient of interest here is 훽훽휃휃, the conditional relationship between an outcome 푌푌푖푖푎푎 and 
21:649:57314:22:21:71:543:607:144:Times:144:Times:B:a  B-count.31 훽훽휃휃 identifies  a  wedge  between  decision  and  experienced  utility if f(.)  captures  the  
21:650:57423:21:21:72:542:586:144:Times:144:Times:B:relationship between other covariates and 푌푌푖푖푎푎, and under assumptions about the measurement error 
21:651:57523:21:22:72:218:565:144:Times:144:Times:B:structure that we now clarify.  
21:652:57555:22:21:94:544:543:144:Times:144:Times:A:A  challenge to estimation  is that 휐휐푖푖 can  attenuate 훽훽휃휃,  or  falsely identify such a relationship 
21:653:57658:21:20:72:545:522:144:Times:144:Times:B:where  none  exists  (Fuller  2009;  Gillen,  Snowberg,  and  Yariv  forthcoming).  We  address  this  
21:654:57761:20:21:72:429:502:144:Times:144:Times:B:challenge using the repeated measurements across our two survey rounds. 
21:655:57833:21:21:94:542:481:144:Times:144:Times:A:The   standard approach to dealing with measurement error in a B-count would be to use its 
21:656:57924:21:22:72:542:460:144:Times:144:Times:B:non-contemporaneous elicitation (Round 2 in the Round 1 model, and vice versa) as an instrument 
21:657:58020:22:21:72:545:438:144:Times:144:Times:B:for the contemporaneous elicitation. The instrument will lead to an unbiased estimate of 훽훽푖푖휃휃 if  the 
21:658:58124:21:21:72:544:417:144:Times:144:Times:B:measurement errors in the behavioral sufficient statistic are uncorrelated, i.e. 퐸퐸[휐휐1푖푖휐휐2푖푖] = 0, and 
21:659:58229:21:21:72:375:396:144:Times:144:Times:B:if in each round 휃휃푖푖,휐휐푖푖,휀휀푖푖, and 휏휏푖푖 are independently distributed.  
21:660:58300:21:21:94:432:375:144:Times:144:Times:A:Such an approach would yield two “single IV” models for estimation: 
21:661:58367:21:21:123:536:354:144:Times:144:Times:A:Model Outcome Covariates B-count B-count IV 
21:662:58406:21:21:136:530:333:144:Times:144:Times:A:1 Round 1 Round 1 Round 1 Round 2 
21:663:58435:21:20:136:530:312:144:Times:144:Times:A:2 Round 2 Round 2 Round 2 Round 1 
21:664:58464:20:21:94:543:292:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  go  beyond  the single  IV  approach by  implementing  the  “both-ways”  approach  of 
21:665:58553:21:21:72:543:271:144:Times:144:Times:B:Obviously  Related  Instrumental  Variables (Gillen,  Snowberg,  and  Yariv  forthcoming).  ORIV 
21:666:58649:21:21:72:545:250:144:Times:144:Times:B:stacks the  data, using  both  the first elicitation  to  instrument for  the  second  and the  second  
21:667:58753:21:22:72:544:229:144:Times:144:Times:B:elicitation  to instrument for  the  first.  As  with  the  single  IV  approach,  ORIV  will  produce  an  
21:668:58861:22:21:72:546:207:144:Times:144:Times:B:unbiased  estimate  of  훽훽푖푖휃휃 if  the  measurement  errors  in  the  behavioral  sufficient  statistic  are  
21:669:58971:21:81:72:206:186:144:Times:144:Times:B:uncorrelated across rounds. 
21:670:58998:81:14:72:453:105:49:Times:121:Times:F:30 The Results Appendix discusses several findings consistent with these assumptions. 
21:671:59083:14:0:72:382:91:49:g_d0_f4:121:Times:F:31 Equation (3) suppresses coefficients on f(.) and g(.) to avoid clutter. 
22:672:59158:0:20:94:543:708:144:Times:144:Times:A:In our setting we elicit two rounds of data for nearly all outcomes and covariates as well as 
22:673:59252:20:21:72:543:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:for our variables of greatest interest (in our case, those used to construct the B-count). Thus we 
22:674:59351:21:21:72:260:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:have four “replicates” per Gillen et al.: 
22:675:59393:21:21:117:536:646:144:Times:144:Times:A:Replicate Outcome Covariates B-count B-count IV 
22:676:59436:21:21:136:530:625:144:Times:144:Times:A:1 Round 1 Round 1 Round 1 Round 2 
22:677:59465:21:20:136:530:604:144:Times:144:Times:A:2 Round 1 Round 1 Round 2 Round 1 
22:678:59494:20:21:136:530:584:144:Times:144:Times:A:3 Round 2 Round 2 Round 2 Round 1 
22:679:59523:21:21:136:530:563:144:Times:144:Times:A:4 Round 2 Round 2 Round 1 Round 2 
22:680:59552:21:21:94:97:542:144:Times:144:Times:A: 
22:681:59552:21:21:94:542:521:144:Times:144:Times:A:We first estimate the model separately for replicates 1 and 2 (“round 1 ORIV”) and compare 
22:682:59643:21:20:71:542:500:144:Times:144:Times:B:those estimates to those obtained using replicates 3 and 4 (“round 2 ORIV”). We do not reject the 
22:683:59741:20:21:71:542:480:144:Times:144:Times:B:restriction that the empirical relationships are identical for round 1 ORIV and round 2 ORIV. Thus 
22:684:59840:21:21:71:542:459:144:Times:144:Times:B:most of our empirics in Section 5 pool the four replicates, clustering standard errors by panelist. 
22:685:59940:21:20:71:544:438:144:Times:144:Times:B:In  some  specifications  we  also  treat  other covariates  such  as  cognitive skills as  measured  with  
22:686:60048:20:21:71:542:418:144:Times:144:Times:B:error, and employ ORIV for them as well (i.e., by instrumenting for round 2 covariates with round 
22:687:60146:21:27:71:222:397:144:Times:144:Times:B:1 covariates, and vice versa).32 
22:688:60178:27:27:72:343:370:144:Times:144:Times:B:D. Interpretation and non-classical measurement error 
22:689:60231:27:20:94:543:343:144:Times:144:Times:A:Here we consider some issues of interpretation and some potential sources of non-classical 
22:690:60322:20:21:72:418:323:144:Times:144:Times:B:measurement error, none of which seems to be problematic in practice.  
22:691:60392:21:21:94:540:302:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  intend  for  our  outcomes  to  measure  what  Benjamin  et  al.  call  different  aspects—
22:692:60487:21:20:72:543:281:144:Times:144:Times:B:components—of  overall  well-being  and  hence  experienced  utility.  Our  objective  and  subjective  
22:693:60591:20:21:71:542:261:144:Times:144:Times:B:financial indexes measure a financial aspect of well-being; in Benjamin et al. (2014) the financial 
22:694:60691:21:21:72:542:240:144:Times:144:Times:B:aspect has a high weight in terms of overall relative marginal utility (ranking 6th out of 113 aspects 
22:695:60794:21:20:72:140:219:144:Times:144:Times:B:on their list).  
22:696:60811:20:21:94:544:199:144:Times:144:Times:A:Interpreting  our  outcomes  as  aspects  of  experienced  utility,  rather  than  total  experienced  
22:697:60914:21:21:72:544:178:144:Times:144:Times:B:utility,  comes  with  one  cost  and  two  benefits.  The  cost  of  course  is  that  our  estimated  linkages  
22:698:61028:21:21:72:543:157:144:Times:144:Times:B:between behavioral biases and outcomes are aspect-specific (consumer-aspect level), not holistic 
22:699:61125:21:45:71:543:136:144:Times:144:Times:B:(consumer-level). The benefits are that identification is easier and more transparent. The “easier” 
22:700:61225:45:17:72:543:91:49:Times:121:Times:F:32 Here we rely on the fact that our other covariates are also stable within-person over time, as detailed in 
22:701:61335:17:0:71:127:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:footnote 21. 
23:702:61350:0:20:72:542:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:piece is that we avoid having to extrapolate from aspect-level to overall experienced utility, as the 
23:703:61452:20:21:72:539:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:Benjamin et al.  papers warn against. The transparency piece is that we can identify  how  the  B-
23:704:61550:21:21:72:535:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:count links differently with different aspects of utility (Sections 5-D and the Results Appendix). 
23:705:61649:21:22:94:542:646:144:Times:144:Times:A:One  potential  problem  is  misspecification  of  an  outcome  index’s  component  weights.  Our 
23:706:61747:22:20:72:543:624:144:Times:144:Times:B:indexes weight each component equally, which could bias 훽훽휃휃in either direction depending on the 
23:707:61844:20:21:72:545:604:144:Times:144:Times:B:relationships   between  index  component  correlations  with  the  B-count  and  index  component  
23:708:61944:21:22:72:543:583:144:Times:144:Times:B:contributions to (weights in) experienced utility. We check this potential confound by examining 
23:709:62040:22:21:72:543:561:144:Times:144:Times:B:whether 훽훽휃휃differs dramatically across components of the indexes, and find that it does not, at least 
23:710:62143:21:20:71:255:540:144:Times:144:Times:B:not qualitatively (Results Appendix).  
23:711:62182:20:21:94:543:520:144:Times:144:Times:A:A second potential issue is omitting an important component of aspect-level well-being from 
23:712:62274:21:21:71:543:499:144:Times:144:Times:B:an index. This seems unlikely to be a material problem, at least for our financial condition indexes, 
23:713:62376:21:21:71:552:478:144:Times:144:Times:B:given the breadth of our measures. But    even if there were such an omitted component, it also would 
23:714:62478:21:20:71:542:457:144:Times:144:Times:B:have to (a) have a high marginal utility weight for that aspect, and (b) have a weaker correlation 
23:715:62577:20:21:72:163:437:144:Times:144:Times:B:with our B-count.  
23:716:62596:21:21:94:545:416:144:Times:144:Times:A:A third  potential  problem  would  arise  if  it  were  somehow  easier  for  low-effort  survey  
23:717:62695:21:20:72:543:395:144:Times:144:Times:B:respondents to indicate worse outcomes than better ones, since it is presumably easier to indicate 
23:718:62794:20:21:72:547:375:144:Times:144:Times:B:behavioral  tendencies  (thereby  upping  one’s  B-count)  than  classical  ones.  Controlling  for  
23:719:62895:21:21:72:542:354:144:g_d0_f8:144:Times:B:푔푔(푆푆푆푆푆푆푆푆푖푖) accounts for any systematic relationships between survey effort, self-reported outcomes, 
23:720:62999:21:21:71:542:333:144:Times:144:Times:B:and B-counts. The survey time variables may over-control, if they reflect behavioral tendencies or 
23:721:63098:21:21:71:542:312:144:Times:144:Times:B:other characteristics like cognitive ability, but including them is a “better safe than sorry” approach 
23:722:63202:21:20:71:542:291:144:Times:144:Times:B:and  in  practice  does  not  change  our  inferences  (Section  5-B). Further,  as  the  Data  Appendix 
23:723:63307:20:21:72:545:271:144:Times:144:Times:B:Section  3 details, our  survey  user-interfaces  do  not  make  it  easier  for  respondents  to  indicate  
23:724:63416:21:21:72:270:250:144:Times:144:Times:B:systematically better or worse outcomes. 
23:725:63456:21:0:72:219:229:144:Times:144:Times:B:  
24:726:63458:0:20:72:439:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:5. Results: Links between B-counts and outcomes (experienced utility) 
24:727:63526:20:21:94:544:688:144:Times:144:Times:A:This  section  presents  and  discusses  estimates  of  equation  (3),  for  the  sample  of  845  ALP  
24:728:63630:21:21:71:421:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:panelists who completed all four modules across our two survey rounds. 
24:729:63701:21:21:71:287:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:A. Primary results: B-counts and outcomes 
24:730:63742:21:20:94:543:625:144:Times:144:Times:A:Table 4 uses our primary, pooled ORIV specification.33 The models here regress objective or 
24:731:63834:20:22:72:544:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:subjective  financial  condition34 on one  of  our  three  main B-counts35 and  our  complete  set  of  
24:732:63938:22:21:72:543:583:144:Times:144:Times:B:additional covariates,36 with each column presenting 훽훽휃휃from a single ORIV regression. Columns 
24:733:64034:21:21:72:542:562:144:Times:144:Times:B:3,  6,  and  9  regress subjective  financial  condition  on  the  same  covariates  and  add  the  objective 
24:734:64144:21:20:72:542:541:144:Times:144:Times:B:financial condition as an additional covariate; those specifications   allow for financial resources to 
24:735:64248:20:21:72:370:521:144:Times:144:Times:B:serve as an input to utility, per our discussion in Section 4-B.  
24:736:64314:21:22:94:542:500:144:Times:144:Times:A:The Full or Sparsity B-count strongly and negatively conditionally correlates with outcomes 
24:737:64406:22:21:72:542:478:144:Times:144:Times:B:(p-value <0.01) in each of Table 4’s nine specifications, and the economic magnitude of 훽훽휃휃is large 
24:738:64507:21:20:72:543:457:144:Times:144:Times:B:in every specification. We report marginal effects in the “d(LHS)/d(1 SD B-count)” row, and the 
24:739:64603:20:21:72:543:437:144:Times:144:Times:B:smallest one of the nine implies a 22% decline in average financial condition associated with a one 
24:740:64703:21:21:72:547:416:144:Times:144:Times:B:standard deviation increase in a B-count (the -0.119 in Column 4, divided by the LHS mean).  The 
24:741:64800:21:20:72:543:395:144:Times:144:Times:B:d(LHS)/d(1 SD B-count) row also shows similar magnitudes across the Full and Sparsity B-counts, 
24:742:64896:20:21:72:401:375:144:Times:144:Times:B:with 8 of the 9 estimated marginal effects within [-0.179, -0.119].37  
24:743:64967:21:21:94:544:354:144:Times:144:Times:A:In  all,  Table  4  shows  that  B-counts  have  economically  and  statistically  strong  conditional  
24:744:65071:21:21:72:253:333:144:Times:144:Times:B:correlations with financial outcomes.

24:750:65443:8:17:72:548:206:49:Times:121:Times:F:34 Appendix Table 6   estimates equation (3) for each of our index component outcomes, and finds similar 
24:751:65548:17:13:72:543:189:121:Times:121:Times:F:qualitative results for the B-counts but with some quantitative differences. See the Results Appendix for 
24:752:65654:13:8:72:123:176:121:Times:121:Times:F:discussion. 
24:753:65665:8:17:72:543:168:49:Times:121:Times:F:35 Appendix Table 7   estimates equation (3) for alternative functional forms of the Full B-count and finds 
24:754:65773:17:9:72:322:151:121:Times:121:Times:F:similar results. See the Results Appendix for discussion. 
24:755:65830:9:17:72:542:142:49:Times:121:Times:F:36 The  additional  covariates  are  defined  in  Appendix  Table  1 and  detailed  in  Sections  4-A  and  4-B. 
24:756:65943:17:12:71:543:125:121:Times:121:Times:F:Appendix Table 8 displays the estimated correlations for these covariates from the specifications used in 
24:757:66049:12:9:71:353:113:121:Times:121:Times:F:Table 4 Columns 1-3. See the Results Appendix for discussion. 
24:758:66110:9:17:72:542:104:49:g_d0_f4:121:Times:F:37 Appendix Table 9 compares sampling-weighted estimates to unweighted ones from Table 4 and reveals 
24:759:66210:17:13:71:543:87:121:Times:121:Times:F:that  the  weighted  coefficients  are  larger  in  point  terms  but  less  precise.  See  the  Results  Appendix  for  
24:760:66331:13:0:71:122:74:121:Times:121:g_d0_f4:F:discussion. 
25:761:66344:0:27:72:347:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:B. Results are invariant to the set of covariates/controls 
25:762:66401:27:20:94:543:681:144:Times:144:Times:A:Table 5 examines robustness to the set of covariates and shows that 훽훽휃휃is nearly invariant to 
25:763:66496:20:21:72:544:661:144:Times:144:Times:B:the  composition  of  our  rich  set  of controls. These  results  suggest  that  researchers  interested  in  
25:764:66607:21:21:72:543:640:144:Times:144:Times:B:cross-sectional heterogeneity can economize on measuring other covariates alongside a B-count: 
25:765:66702:21:20:72:543:619:144:Times:144:Times:B:the overall cost of adding measurement of a behavioral sufficient statistic to a research design can 
25:766:66803:20:22:72:544:599:144:Times:144:Times:B:be low (Section 7). Moreover, the stability of the B-count coefficient across specifications with 
25:767:66901:22:21:72:542:577:144:Times:144:Times:B:vastly different controls provides reassurance that 훽훽휃휃captures a behavioral wedge and not omitted 
25:768:67001:21:21:72:386:556:144:Times:144:Times:B:components of other covariates (Altonji, Elder, and Taber 2005). 
25:769:67066:21:20:94:543:535:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  consider  27  specifications  in  Table  5,  each  using  the  subjective  financial  index  on  the 
25:770:67170:20:21:72:543:515:144:Times:144:Times:B:LHS, with a panel per each of our three main B-counts and columns permuting whether and how 
25:771:67262:21:21:72:543:494:144:Times:144:Times:B:we include other covariates. Column 1 in each panel reproduces our main specification. Column 
25:772:67356:21:20:72:543:473:144:Times:144:Times:B:2 drops the demographic variables, Column 3 further drops classical preferences, Column 4 drops 
25:773:67452:20:21:72:542:453:144:Times:144:Times:B:cognitive skills, and Column 5 drops non-cognitive skills. Column 6 drops the survey time spent 
25:774:67548:21:21:72:488:432:144:Times:144:Times:B:deciles, leaving the count of missing biases as the only covariate besides the B-count.  
25:775:67637:21:20:94:544:411:144:Times:144:Times:A:The  B-count  estimate  in  each  of  those  more  parsimonious  specifications  is  very  similar  to  
25:776:67741:20:21:72:542:391:144:Times:144:Times:B:those from our main specification; e.g., for the Full B-count the coefficient is -0.078 (SE 0.009) in 
25:777:67843:21:21:71:543:370:144:Times:144:Times:B:column 6 vs. -0.084 (SE 0.018) in column 1. (This invariance to other covariates does not hold if 
25:778:67941:21:21:72:546:349:144:Times:144:Times:B:one fails to account for measurement error in the B-count by, e.g., using OLS instead of ORIV.)38  
25:779:68040:21:20:94:542:328:144:Times:144:Times:A:Columns 7-9 further address robustness by using ORIV to allow for measurement error in not 
25:780:68131:20:21:72:543:308:144:Times:144:Times:B:just  the  B-count,  but  also  in  one  or  both  of  two  groups  of  classical  inputs:  presumed-classical 
25:781:68242:21:21:72:546:287:144:Times:144:Times:B:preferences and cognitive skills.39 These results are similar to those from the other specifications.40  
25:782:68347:21:21:94:542:266:144:Times:144:Times:A:In all, Table 5 helps solidify the inference that B-   counts capture a distinctly behavioral wedge 
25:783:68447:21:53:71:276:245:144:Times:144:Times:B:between decision and experienced utility.  
25:784:68490:53:17:72:542:192:49:Times:121:Times:F:38 E.g., Appendix Table 10 shows that the OLS estimate of the correlation between the subjective financial 
25:785:68597:17:13:71:543:175:121:Times:121:Times:F:condition index and the Full B-count, using both rounds of data and the full set of covariates, is roughly 
25:786:68704:13:8:71:512:162:121:Times:121:Times:F:half of that in the most parsimonious OLS speficiation (-0.019 vs. -0.035, each with a SE of 0.003). 
25:787:68804:8:17:72:542:154:49:Times:121:Times:F:39 Ideally we would allow measurement error in non-cognitive skills as well, but we lack multiple measures 
25:788:68911:17:8:71:354:137:121:Times:121:Times:F:for personality traits because we did not elicit them in Round 1. 
25:789:68976:8:17:72:543:129:49:Times:121:Times:F:40 Appendix Table 11 estimates the same 27 specifications with the objective financial condition index as 
25:790:69082:17:13:71:543:112:121:Times:121:Times:F:the  LHS  variable  instead  of  the  subjective  index,  and  reveals  a  similar  pattern  to  Table  5  with  two  key  
25:791:69205:13:12:71:542:99:121:Times:121:Times:F:exceptions:  dropping  all  of  the  other  covariates  makes  the  Full  B-count  and  Broad  Sparsity  B-count 
25:792:69317:12:13:71:542:87:121:Times:121:Times:F:correlations with objective financial condition substantially more negative (i.e., compare Column 6 to the 
25:793:69424:13:0:71:226:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:other columns in Panels A and B). 
26:794:69459:0:20:72:231:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:C. Full B-count decompositions 
26:795:69489:20:21:94:542:688:144:Times:144:Times:A:Table 6 decomposes the Full B-count in three ways shown/discussed earlier (Table 2 Panel C 
26:796:69580:21:21:72:543:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:and  Section  3-B),  to  shed  light  on  some  nuances  of  identification  and  interpretation. Each 
26:797:69683:21:21:72:543:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:regression here takes one of our main specifications (Table 4, Columns 1 and 2) and replaces the 
26:798:69780:21:20:72:494:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:Full B-count with a couplet: with two mutually exclusive and exhaustive B-sub-counts. 
26:799:69866:20:21:94:540:605:144:Times:144:Times:A:The first two regressions here decompose the Full B-count into the Math-Bias and Non-Math-
26:800:69956:21:21:71:542:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:Bias  sub-counts.  The  non-math  biases  have  strong  negative  conditional  correlations  with  both 
26:801:70060:21:20:71:542:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:objective and subjective financial condition, while the point estimates on math biases are close to 
26:802:70160:20:21:71:543:543:144:Times:144:Times:B:zero (albeit imprecisely estimated).41 These results offer further reassurance that B-counts are not 
26:803:70261:21:21:71:544:522:144:Times:144:Times:B:“just  math”:  they  capture  something  distinct  from  classical  conceptions/measures of  cognitive  
26:804:70365:21:20:71:164:501:144:Times:144:Times:B:skills/math ability. 
26:805:70386:20:21:94:542:481:144:Times:144:Times:A:The  next  two  regressions  decompose  the  Full B-   count  into  expected-  and  non-expected 
26:806:70483:21:21:72:543:460:144:Times:144:Times:B:directions. Recall that expected-direction biases are those held to be more common/impactful in 
26:807:70579:21:21:72:543:439:144:Times:144:Times:B:prior  work,  such  as  present-bias,  overconfidence  and underestimating  exponential  growth. The 
26:808:70680:21:20:72:543:418:144:Times:144:Times:B:expected-direction  B-count  has  strong  negative  conditional  correlations  with both  financial 
26:809:70780:20:21:72:543:398:144:Times:144:Times:B:condition indexes, with point estimates almost identical to those of the Full B-count in Table 4. 
26:810:70878:21:21:72:543:377:144:Times:144:Times:B:The Non-expected Direction B-count (future bias, under-confidence, overestimating exponential 
26:811:70972:21:20:72:542:356:144:Times:144:Times:B:growth, etc.) also has negative and large point estimates, but they are imprecisely estimated. Taken 
26:812:71073:20:21:72:544:336:144:Times:144:Times:B:together  these results  validate  behavioral  economics’  focus  on  expected  direction  biases,  while  
26:813:71180:21:21:71:544:315:144:Times:144:Times:B:leaving  unresolved  the  question  of  whether  measures  of  non-expected  direction  biases  capture  
26:814:71285:21:21:71:269:294:144:Times:144:Times:B:something substantive or merely noise.42 
26:815:71325:21:20:94:543:273:144:Times:144:Times:A:The last two columns in Table 6 compare Preference vs. Non-preference B-sub-counts. This 
26:816:71414:20:21:72:544:253:144:Times:144:Times:B:decomposition is  informative  because  the  welfare  implications  of  behavioral  preference  biases  
26:817:71518:21:66:72:544:232:144:Times:144:Times:B:(loss  aversion,  ambiguity  aversion,  etc.)  are  less  clear  than  for  non-preferences  (biased  price  
26:818:71627:66:17:72:546:166:49:Times:121:Times:F:41Appendix  Table  12 shows  similar  results  from  dropping  the  cognitive  skills  covariates  and/or  
26:819:71734:17:8:72:394:149:121:Times:121:Times:F:decomposing the math biases into expected vs. non-expected directions.  
26:820:71805:8:17:72:543:141:49:Times:121:Times:F:42 How one interprets the Non-expected Bias B-sub-count results is highly contingent on one’s prior: our 
26:821:71910:17:13:71:542:124:121:Times:121:Times:F:prior was agnostic, and hence our interpretation is that these noisy results tell us little about the economic 
26:822:72021:13:12:71:545:111:121:Times:121:Times:F:importance  of  non-expected  directional  biases.  But  if  one  had  a  strong  prior  that  non-expected  bias  
26:823:72136:12:13:71:542:99:121:Times:121:Times:F:measures reflect noise rather than true biases, then these results provide some support for that hypothesis. 
26:824:72245:13:0:71:234:86:121:Times:121:Times:F:We explore this further in Section 7. 
27:825:72285:0:20:72:543:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:perceptions and expectations, limited attention, etc.).43 Consequently the important tests in Table 
27:826:72385:20:21:72:543:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:6 Columns 5 and 6 are on the Non-preference B-sub-count. One would question whether the Full 
27:827:72477:21:21:72:542:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:B-   count results are truly indicative of consumer welfare losses i f it turned out that only behavioral 
27:828:72583:21:21:72:542:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:preferences  were  driving  the  negative  conditional  correlation  between  our financial  condition 
27:829:72686:21:20:72:543:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:measures and the Full B-count. But columns 5 and 6 show that is not the case; the Non-preference 
27:830:72783:20:21:72:507:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:Bias B-sub-count is strongly negatively correlated with both financial condition indexes.44 
27:831:72874:21:21:94:542:584:144:Times:144:Times:A:In all, Table 6 helps further solidify the inference that B-counts capture something distinctly 
27:832:72970:21:20:72:433:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:behavioral—a behavioral wedge between decision and experienced utility. 
27:833:73042:20:27:72:442:543:144:Times:144:Times:B:D. Other outcomes: Different (and broader) measures of experienced utility 
27:834:73115:27:21:94:543:516:144:Times:144:Times:A:Table 7 expands the set of outcomes to include additional aspects of experienced utility: life 
27:835:73210:21:20:72:545:495:144:Times:144:Times:B:satisfaction,  happiness,  and  health  status.  These  aspects,  like  the  financial  aspect,  have  high  
27:836:73319:20:21:72:543:475:144:Times:144:Times:B:marginal utility rankings per Benjamin et al. (2014): life satisfaction ranks 11th, health ranks 3rd 
27:837:73419:21:21:72:544:454:144:Times:144:Times:B:and  happiness  ranks  as  high  as  2nd.   In  Column  1  we  also  reproduce  the  Table  4  results  with  
27:838:73529:21:21:72:363:433:144:Times:144:Times:B:subjective financial condition as the outcome, for reference. 
27:839:73591:21:20:94:544:412:144:Times:144:Times:A:The  pairwise  correlations  between  our  measures  of  life  satisfaction,  happiness,  and  health  
27:840:73694:20:21:72:543:392:144:Times:144:Times:B:status range from 0.32 to 0.65 (Appendix Table 3 Panel C; Table 3). Table 3 also shows that these 
27:841:73792:21:21:72:542:371:144:Times:144:Times:B:measures are strongly positively correlated with our indexes of subjective financial condition (the 
27:842:73892:21:20:72:545:350:144:Times:144:Times:B:range  is  0.29  to  0.50)  and  objective  financial  condition  (from  0.29  to  0.35).  Except  for  one  
27:843:74001:20:21:72:542:330:144:Times:144:Times:B:elicitation of life satisfaction, all of these other elicitations come from modules other than ours, in 
27:844:74105:21:21:72:545:309:144:Times:144:Times:B:periods  roughly  coincident  with  our  study  period.45 Varying  response  rates  across  these  other  
27:845:74211:21:33:72:396:288:144:Times:144:Times:B:modules produces varying sample sizes across columns in Table 7. 
27:846:74276:33:17:72:542:255:49:Times:121:Times:F:43 A policymaker has clearer normative grounds for correcting non-preference biases. In contrast, one might 
27:847:74384:17:12:71:543:238:121:Times:121:Times:F:consider  preferences  inviolate,  even  if  they  are  not  classically  normative.  E.g.,  a  policymaker  may  lack  
27:848:74504:12:13:71:542:226:121:Times:121:Times:F:grounds for trying to debias someone who is ambiguity averse, but she probably has grounds for trying to 
27:849:74609:13:13:71:544:213:121:Times:121:Times:F:debias  someone  who  underestimates  the  power  of  the  Law  of  Large  Numbers.  Hewing  closer  to  our  
27:850:74719:13:13:71:542:200:121:Times:121:Times:F:framework,  the  point  is  simply  that  if  behavioral  preferences  are  truly  preferences,  then  the  preference 
27:851:74838:13:12:71:543:187:121:Times:121:Times:F:components of 휃휃푖푖 may not drive a wedge between decision utility and experienced utility. Related, if the 
27:852:74944:12:13:72:543:175:121:Times:121:Times:F:only material behavioral components of decision making were grounded in preferences, one might still rely 
27:853:75050:13:8:72:267:162:121:Times:121:Times:F:on revealed preference for welfare analysis. 
27:854:75094:8:17:72:541:154:49:Times:121:Times:F:44 Meanwhile, the Preference Bias B-sub-count has a less robust relationship with financial condition. There 
27:855:75203:17:13:71:541:137:121:Times:121:Times:F:are various ways to interpret these results, depending on one’s priors. Our view is that the consumer welfare 
27:856:75313:13:8:71:530:124:121:Times:121:Times:F:consequences of behavioral preferences remains an open question. We explore this further in Section 7. 
27:857:75415:8:17:72:542:116:49:Times:121:Times:F:45 In deciding which measures to merge in from other modules, we define “study period” as post-our Round 
27:858:75520:17:13:71:543:99:121:Times:121:Times:F:1 (we could not find any relevant measure post-our Round 2), and select questions that have: a) been used 
27:859:75626:13:0:71:542:86:121:Times:121:Times:F:in other studies; b) measure highly rated “aspects” of subjective well-being in the marginal utility sense per 
28:860:75739:0:20:94:551:708:144:Times:144:Times:A:The Full B-count coefficients    in Table 7 Panel A are imprecisely estimated zeroes, while the 
28:861:75835:20:21:72:543:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:Sparsity B-count coefficients are more clearly negative, with all of the eight new point estimates 
28:862:75934:21:21:72:543:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:in Panels B and C implying marginal effects <-0.04 (on bases of 0.50 to 0.70), and six of them 
28:863:76029:21:21:72:543:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:having p-values <0.05. For the Sparsity counts, a one standard deviation increase in the B-count 
28:864:76126:21:20:72:545:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:is  associated  with  life  satisfaction  5-15%  lower  on  the  mean.  For  health  and  happiness,  the  
28:865:76233:20:21:72:542:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:corresponding  declines  are  roughly  10%  on  the  mean.  For  comparison,  the Broad  Sparsity 
28:866:76331:21:21:72:543:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:coefficients are roughly the same magnitude as moving down the income distribution by one to 
28:867:76424:21:20:72:464:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:four deciles (depending on the outcome and position in the income distribution).  
28:868:76506:20:21:94:542:543:144:Times:144:Times:A:What might explain the pattern here of non-results for the Full B-count coupled with stronger 
28:869:76600:21:21:71:542:522:144:Times:144:Times:B:results on the Sparsity counts? One explanation is that we chose the full bias collection specifically 
28:870:76703:21:20:71:543:501:144:Times:144:Times:B:with links to financial decision making/outcomes in mind (as the Data Appendix Section 1 details). 
28:871:76802:20:21:71:542:481:144:Times:144:Times:B:The Sparsity Biases are motivated somewhat more broadly, although Gabaix too focuses to a great 
28:872:76898:21:21:71:549:460:144:Times:144:Times:B:extent   on   financial   choices. In   any   case,   further examining   links   between   different   
28:873:77002:21:21:71:543:439:144:Times:144:Times:B:definitions/conceptions of behavioral summary statistics and different outcome domains (different 
28:874:77100:21:20:71:396:418:144:Times:144:Times:B:aspects of experienced utility) is a promising line of future inquiry. 
28:875:77171:20:27:71:267:398:144:Times:144:Times:B:E. Additional results/robustness checks 
28:876:77210:27:21:94:548:371:144:Times:144:Times:A:The   Appendix  discusses  several  additional  results  and  robustness  checks  mentioned  above 
28:877:77309:21:26:71:333:350:144:Times:144:Times:B:that require some elaboration (Appendix Tables 4-9).  
28:878:77363:26:21:71:339:324:144:Times:144:Times:B:F. Summary interpretation of conditional correlations 
28:879:77416:21:21:94:547:303:144:Times:144:Times:A:Altogether,  the  results  in  Tables  4-7   (and   accompanying   Appendix   Tables)  indicate  
28:880:77513:21:20:71:543:282:144:Times:144:Times:B:economically large negative conditional correlations between B-counts and various measures of 
28:881:77607:20:21:71:543:262:144:Times:144:Times:B:outcomes in the financial domain and beyond. These results are consistent with the foundational 
28:882:77703:21:21:71:543:241:144:Times:144:Times:B:presumption of behavioral sufficient statistic models that behavioral biases, taken together, drive 
28:883:77803:21:20:71:347:220:144:Times:144:Times:B:a wedge between decision utility and experienced utility. 
28:884:77861:20:27:71:337:200:144:Times:144:Times:B:6. B-   counts are distinct from other decision inputs 
28:885:77914:27:21:94:544:173:144:Times:144:Times:A:This  section  ties  together  several  sets  of  results  showing  that  B-counts  capture  something  
28:886:78018:21:21:71:544:152:144:Times:144:Times:B:about  decision  making  that  is  distinct  from  measures   of  classical  decision  inputs  and  our  other  
28:887:78130:21:44:71:126:131:144:Times:144:Times:B:covariates. 
28:888:78141:44:13:72:542:87:121:Times:121:Times:F:Benjamin et al. (2014); c) are answered at least once by at least 2/3 of our sample. See Table 3 and Appendix 
28:889:78251:13:0:72:318:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:Table 3 for details on the construction of each variable. 
29:890:78310:0:20:94:542:708:144:Times:144:Times:A:Recapping what we have learned already: 1) B-counts are strongly correlated with outcomes 
29:891:78400:20:21:72:543:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:(measures  of  experienced  utility),  conditional  on  our  rich set  of  additional  covariates;  2)  Those  
29:892:78511:21:21:71:542:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:correlations are robust to very different specifications of the additional covariates, suggesting that 
29:893:78614:21:21:71:544:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:any  correlations  between  B-counts  and  other  covariates  do  not  confound  inferences  on  the  link  
29:894:78722:21:20:71:542:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:between B-counts and decisions/outcomes; 3) Variation in the Full B-count poorly explained by 
29:895:78816:20:21:71:299:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:our rich set of additional covariates (Figure 1). 
29:896:78866:21:21:94:543:584:144:Times:144:Times:A:This section expands on the last finding by focusing on the extent to which other covariates 
29:897:78959:21:20:71:542:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:can explain variation in each of the B-counts we use in the main tables. In addition to rounding out 
29:898:79060:20:21:71:543:543:144:Times:144:Times:B:our  description  of  B-counts’  statistical  properties  (see  also  Section  3),  this  exercise  adds  to  the  
29:899:79175:21:21:71:543:522:144:Times:144:Times:B:“Who is (more) behavioral” literature (e.g., D. Benjamin, Brown, and Shapiro 2013), by adding 
29:900:79269:21:20:71:543:501:144:Times:144:Times:B:evidence on fit to a literature that has focused on correlations, and by adding evidence based on 
29:901:79367:20:21:71:545:481:144:Times:144:Times:B:consumer-level  metrics  of  behavioral  tendencies  to  a  literature  that  has  considered  behavioral  
29:902:79474:21:21:72:159:460:144:Times:144:Times:B:biases piecemeal. 
29:903:79491:21:21:94:544:439:144:Times:144:Times:A:Our analysis in this section uses B-count proportions instead of levels, and OLS instead of 
29:904:79583:21:20:71:544:418:144:Times:144:Times:B:ORIV.  The  former  specification  choice  accounts  for  missingness  without  overfitting.  The  latter  
29:905:79690:20:21:71:543:398:144:Times:144:Times:B:estimator choice is motivated by the fact that ORIV does not produce an interpretable fit statistic; 
29:906:79791:21:21:71:543:377:144:Times:144:Times:B:results above (Table 5, Columns 7-9) offer reassurance that measurement error in the additional 
29:907:79887:21:26:72:324:356:144:Times:144:Times:B:covariates is unlikely to affect OLS inferences here. 
29:908:79940:26:21:94:544:330:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  start  here  with  some  simple  univariate  comparisons  further highlighting  the  extent  to  
29:909:80041:21:21:72:544:309:144:Times:144:Times:B:which B-counts are not simply proxies for other covariates thought to correlate with behavioral 
29:910:80137:21:20:72:543:288:144:Times:144:Times:B:biases.46 Figures  2a-2d show distributions of our Full  B-count  proportion,  broken out for  paired 
29:911:80238:20:21:72:543:268:144:Times:144:Times:B:groups  at  the  opposite  ends  of  the  income, risk  aversion,  education,  and cognitive  skills 
29:912:80339:21:21:71:543:247:144:Times:144:Times:B:distributions. These  do  show  the  expected  level  differences  on  average;  e.g.,  the  B-count 
29:913:80440:21:21:71:542:226:144:Times:144:Times:B:distribution is shifted somewhat rightward for those in the lowest cognitive skills quartile relative 
29:914:80542:21:20:71:539:205:144:Times:144:Times:B:to the highest. But also noteworthy is that the B-count varies substantially within each of the sub-
29:915:80642:20:21:72:542:185:144:Times:144:Times:B:groups we examine. Indeed, within-group variation in the B-count dwarfs cross-group variation, 
29:916:80737:21:60:71:453:164:144:Times:144:Times:B:even between these four pairs of groups that are very different by construction. 
29:917:80818:60:17:72:541:104:49:Times:121:Times:F:46 See, e.g., Benjamin et al. (2013), Burks et al. (2009), Cesarini et al. (2012), Chapman et al. (2018a), Dean 
29:918:80930:17:13:71:544:87:121:Times:121:Times:F:and  Ortoleva  (2018),  Frederick  (2005),  Li  et  al.  (2013).  See  also  Dohmen  et  al.  (2010)  on  correlations  
29:919:81050:13:0:71:375:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:between measures of classical preferences/attitudes and intelligence. 
30:920:81122:0:20:94:543:708:144:Times:144:Times:A:Table 8 uses OLS to estimate the amount of variation explained by other covariates for each 
30:921:81214:20:21:72:543:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:of our nine B-count proportions. These range from 0.12 for the Narrow Sparsity B-count to 0.40 
30:922:81309:21:21:72:542:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:for  the  Non-preference  B-count,  with  0.33  for  the  Full  B-count. The  subsequent  rows show 
30:923:81409:21:21:72:549:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:estimated   partial   R-squareds   for   subsets   of   covariates:   demographics,   cognitive   skills,   
30:924:81517:21:20:72:548:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:noncognitive   skills   (for   which   we   have   Round   2   data   only),   classical   preferences   (risk   
30:925:81630:20:21:72:543:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:preferences/attitudes, and patience estimated from our money discounting task), state of residence, 
30:926:81730:21:21:72:544:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:and the deciles capturing time spent on our behavioral elicitations. Demographics and cognitive 
30:927:81826:21:20:72:539:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:skills tend  to  explain  the  most  variation,  although  their  fit  varies  widely  across  the different  B-
30:928:81938:20:21:72:542:543:144:Times:144:Times:B:counts. The demographics’ R-squared ranges from 0.03 for the Preference B-count to 0.24 for the 
30:929:82034:21:21:72:544:522:144:Times:144:Times:B:Math  and  Non-preference  B-counts.  The  cognitive  skills’  R-squared  ranges  from  0.01  for  the  
30:930:82138:21:20:72:546:501:144:Times:144:Times:B:Preference  B-count  to  the  0.33  for  the  Non-preference  B-count.  The  other  four  groups  of  
30:931:82240:20:21:72:540:481:144:Times:144:Times:B:covariates—noncognitive skills, classical preferences, state of residence, and survey time spent—
30:932:82337:21:21:72:543:460:144:Times:144:Times:B:never explain more than 7% of the variation in a B-count across their 36 estimates (4 groups of 
30:933:82433:21:21:72:543:439:144:Times:144:Times:B:covariates x 9 B-counts). Particularly striking is that, having adjusted for non-response on the LHS, 
30:934:82535:21:20:72:543:418:144:Times:144:Times:B:respondent time spent completing our behavioral elicitations explains <=1% of the variation in the 
30:935:82634:20:21:72:544:398:144:Times:144:Times:B:B-count  proportions. This  is  consistent  with  our  earlier  hypothesis  that  nearly  all  respondents  
30:936:82742:21:21:72:309:377:144:Times:144:Times:B:seriously engage with our surveys (Section 2-C). 
30:937:82791:21:20:94:546:356:144:Times:144:Times:A:Comparing  these  fit  estimates  across  the  various  B-counts  reveals  several  additional  
30:938:82887:20:21:71:542:336:144:Times:144:Times:B:noteworthy patterns. The Sparsity B-counts are relatively poorly explained by our other covariates, 
30:939:82987:21:21:71:544:315:144:Times:144:Times:B:consistent  with  Sparsity  constructs  capturing  especially  distinct  and  behavioral  influences  on  
30:940:83093:21:20:71:545:294:144:Times:144:Times:B:decision  making.  Math  biases  are  much  better  explained  by  demographics  (which  include  
30:941:83191:20:21:71:542:274:144:Times:144:Times:B:education)  and  cognitive  skills  than  non-math  biases,  as  one  would  expect.  Expected-direction 
30:942:83295:21:21:71:542:253:144:Times:144:Times:B:biases  are  much  better  fit  by  other  covariates  (0.25)  than  non-expected direction  biases  (0.13). 
30:943:83404:21:20:71:543:232:144:Times:144:Times:B:Coupled  with  the  latter’s  very  imprecisely  estimated  correlations  with  outcomes  (Table  6)  and  
30:944:83511:20:21:71:539:212:144:Times:144:Times:B:relatively low within-person temporal stability (Table 2), the overall picture is consistent with non-
30:945:83613:21:21:71:542:191:144:Times:144:Times:B:expected direction biases reflecting more noise than signal. Preference biases (Column 8) are no 
30:946:83710:21:21:71:543:170:144:Times:144:Times:B:better explained by the other covariates than non-expected direction biases, with only 1/3 of the fit 
30:947:83812:21:20:71:263:149:144:Times:144:Times:B:of non-preference biases (Column 9).47 
30:948:83850:20:38:72:219:129:144:Times:144:Times:B:  
30:949:83850:38:17:72:542:91:49:g_d0_f4:121:Times:F:47 As discussed above, this is not for lack of elicitation intensity: we have roughly three minutes per source 
30:950:83960:17:0:71:370:74:121:Times:121:g_d0_f4:F:of potential preference bias vs. one minute per non-preference bias. 
31:951:84030:0:26:72:356:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:7. Efficiently measuring, and modeling with, B-counts 
31:952:84082:26:21:94:548:682:144:Times:144:Times:A:For   researchers   interested using behavioral   sufficient   statistics—for   welfare   analysis,   
31:953:84184:21:21:72:544:661:144:Times:144:Times:B:targeting, theory-testing, and/or describing cross-sectional heterogeneity—the practical question 
31:954:84282:21:21:72:543:640:144:Times:144:Times:B:is how to efficiently capture 휃휃푖푖. Can one do so with a narrower and cheaper set of elicitations than 
31:955:84385:21:27:72:540:619:144:Times:144:Times:B:our full set? Several of our results thus far suggest yes, yielding three pieces of concrete advice:   
31:956:84488:27:21:90:542:592:144:g_d0_f12:144:Times:A:• Lower-dimensional  behavioral  sufficient  statistics  are  informative.  The  Narrow  Sparsity 
31:957:84585:21:21:107:551:571:144:Times:144:Times:A:B-   count is a good example of how theory can guide construction of a sufficient statistic 
31:958:84677:21:21:107:393:550:144:Times:144:Times:A:based on only two underlying biases (Tables 4, 5, and 7).  48 
31:959:84738:21:21:89:547:529:144:g_d0_f12:144:Times:A:• Other   covariates   may   not   be   necessary   to   estimate   empirically   stable   conditional   
31:960:84842:21:22:107:483:508:144:Times:144:Times:A:correlations between outcomes and behavioral sufficient statistics (Table 5).49 
31:961:84921:22:21:90:543:486:144:g_d0_f12:144:Times:A:• Having at least two sets of plausibly independent behavioral bias elicitations is crucial, as 
31:962:85016:21:20:108:542:465:144:Times:144:Times:A:multiple elicitations yield results stable enough to obviate the need to elicit and control for 
31:963:85112:20:27:108:389:445:144:Times:144:Times:A:other covariates (compare Table 5 to Appendix Table 10). 
31:964:85168:27:21:94:542:418:144:Times:144:Times:A:A further point we establish here is that even a randomly selected subset of our biases can be 
31:965:85263:21:20:72:542:397:144:Times:144:Times:B:useful. (It is important to keep in mind that a randomly selected subset of our biases is not the same 
31:966:85366:20:21:72:542:377:144:Times:144:Times:B:as a random sample of the universe of behavioral biases; recall that we chose our full set based on 
31:967:85466:21:21:72:545:356:144:Times:144:Times:B:prior  work  linking  our  17  biases  to  financial  decision  making.) Figures  3a  and  3b  show  the  
31:968:85572:21:20:72:543:335:144:Times:144:Times:B:distributions of coefficients and standard errors from lower-dimensional B-counts using  j:[1, 17] 
31:969:85671:20:21:72:543:315:144:Times:144:Times:B:of potential behavioral biases in our data, where for any j we randomly sample up to 2,500 bias 
31:970:85767:21:21:72:543:294:144:Times:144:Times:B:combinations, construct a B-count from the potential sources of behavioral bias in that draw, and 
31:971:85865:21:29:72:544:273:144:Times:144:Times:B:estimate  our  main  ORIV  specification  with  subjective  financial  condition  on  the  LHS  for  that  
31:972:85972:29:18:72:540:244:49:Times:121:Times:F:48 A  quantitative  if  not  qualitative  caveat  re:  research  budgets  is  that  measuring  the  Narrow  Sparsity  B-
31:973:86092:18:12:71:542:226:121:Times:121:Times:F:count, and other B-sub-counts including our limited prospective memory elicitation (Table 1), does require 
31:974:86199:12:13:71:542:214:121:Times:121:Times:F:additional  resources  beyond  the  survey  time  described  in  Table  2:  1)  An  additional,  very  brief,  survey 
31:975:86316:13:13:71:542:201:121:Times:121:Times:F:module for the follow-up task; 2) A financial incentive to complete the follow-up task. As measuring limited 
31:976:86425:13:12:72:542:188:121:Times:121:Times:F:prospective memory is in its infancy (at least in broad samples and for economic applications), we would 
31:977:86530:12:13:72:540:176:121:Times:121:Times:F:not  presume  that  our  elicitation  is  (cost-)efficient:  there  may  be  ways  to  elicit  a  useful  measure  within-
31:978:86652:13:13:72:542:163:121:Times:121:Times:F:survey, and/or with lower incentive payments per-respondent (e.g, by using a lottery instead of a piece rate). 
31:979:86763:13:12:72:542:150:121:Times:121:Times:F:Having said that, in our implementation the marginal cost of the limited memory elicitation ended up being 
31:980:86870:12:13:72:543:138:121:Times:121:Times:F:modest, because only about 15% of the sample actually completes the follow up task. We ended up paying 
31:981:86973:13:8:72:470:125:121:Times:121:Times:F:about 1427*.15*$10 in Round 1 and 845*.15*$10 in Round 2, for a total of about $3,400. 
31:982:87059:8:17:72:543:117:49:Times:121:Times:F:49 A weaker recommendation, from the perspective of economizing on measurement of other covariates, is 
31:983:87162:17:14:71:543:100:121:Times:121:Times:F:that  one  round  of  data  on  them  may  well  be  sufficient  (Table  5,  Column  7-9  suggests  that  one  need  not  
31:984:87284:14:0:71:409:86:121:Times:121:Times:F:worry about measurement error in other covariates biasing estimates of 훽훽휃휃). 
32:985:87364:0:20:72:543:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:draw.50 These figures show that coefficients and standard errors from B-counts based on small j 
32:986:87460:20:21:72:542:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:converge fairly quickly to what we obtain with j=17, with especially large gains in precision from 
32:987:87559:21:21:72:218:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:measuring at least two biases. 
32:988:87590:21:21:94:542:646:144:Times:144:Times:A:There  is,  of  course,  much  more  work  to  be  done  to  derive  truly  optimized  measurement 
32:989:87689:21:20:72:540:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:dirategies and research designs for behavioral sufficient statistics. To take one example, while B-
32:990:87788:20:21:71:546:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:counts  are  simple  by  design,  model  selection  techniques  could  increase  power  by  guiding  
32:991:87889:21:21:71:543:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:definitional, functional form, and other specification choices. (Having said that, such techniques 
32:992:87988:21:20:71:541:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:would need to account for measurement error, and we are not aware of any that do.) To take another 
32:993:88087:20:21:71:542:543:144:Times:144:Times:B:example, while repeated elicitations of a behavioral sufficient statistics are critical for dealing with 
32:994:88192:21:21:71:544:522:144:Times:144:Times:B:measurement  error,  it  remains  to  be  determined  which  combination  of  timing  and  elicitation  
32:995:88296:21:20:71:545:501:144:Times:144:Times:B:methods  produces the  most  accurate  and/or  cost-effective  measures.  More broadly,  efficient  
32:996:88396:20:21:71:541:481:144:Times:144:Times:B:measurement requires consideration of tradeoffs between multiple margins of costly measurement, 
32:997:88492:21:27:71:230:460:144:Times:144:Times:B:as we discuss in the Conclusion. 
32:998:88524:27:21:71:149:433:144:Times:144:Times:B:8. Conclusion 
32:999:88537:21:20:94:544:412:144:Times:144:Times:A:An  ardent  classical  economist  might  argue  that  behavioral  tendencies  are  a  collection  of  
32:1000:88639:20:21:71:544:392:144:Times:144:Times:B:theoretically incoherent  and/or empirically innocuous  deviations  from  classical  rationality.  An  
32:1001:88742:21:21:71:539:371:144:Times:144:Times:B:ardent  behavioral  economist  might  argue  that  behavioral  biases  are  important,  but  so  multi-
32:1002:88845:21:20:71:542:350:144:Times:144:Times:B:dimensional in how they affect decisions and welfare that a single consumer-level parameter could 
32:1003:88943:20:21:71:542:330:144:Times:144:Times:B:not hope to summarize them usefully. While strange bedfellows, they might agree that seeking to 
32:1004:89038:21:21:71:405:309:144:Times:144:Times:B:measure and model a behavioral sufficient statistic is a fool’s errand. 
32:1005:89110:21:20:94:542:288:144:Times:144:Times:A:We conclude here, alternatively and with others such as Chetty and Gabaix, that behavioral 
32:1006:89201:20:21:71:547:268:144:Times:144:Times:B:economics   can   advance   by   capturing   cross-consumer   heterogeneity   in   overall   behavioral   
32:1007:89307:21:21:71:542:247:144:Times:144:Times:B:tendencies using a single sufficient statistic parameter. Specifically, we construct consumer-level 
32:1008:89406:21:20:71:543:226:144:Times:144:Times:B:behavioral sufficient statistics—B-counts—by aggregating information, within-person, across as 
32:1009:89501:20:21:71:544:206:144:Times:144:Times:B:many  as  17  and  as  few  as  2  potential  sources  of  behavioral  biases. We  measure biases  using  
32:1010:89607:21:21:71:542:185:144:Times:144:Times:B:streamlined, portable, and low-cost elicitations, and so our sufficient statistics are easy to measure, 
32:1011:89711:21:60:71:203:164:144:Times:144:Times:B:construct, and understand.  
32:1012:89739:60:17:72:543:104:49:Times:121:Times:F:50 As j gets closer to 2 or 17, the number of possible combinations falls below 2,500—sampling 16 of our 
32:1013:89844:17:13:71:543:87:121:Times:121:Times:F:17 potential sources of bias can have only 17 possible combinations, for example, as can sampling only 
32:1014:89947:13:0:71:436:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:one. Drawing 8 or 9 has 24,310 possibilities, the max, from which we draw 2,500. 
33:1015:90029:0:20:94:543:708:144:Times:144:Times:A:Our B-counts have distributional properties required for behavioral sufficient statistics to be 
33:1016:90125:20:21:72:543:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:useful for various applications, are strongly conditionally correlated with various outcomes, and 
33:1017:90223:21:21:72:542:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:are quite distinct empirically from measures of classical decision inputs and other covariates. One 
33:1018:90323:21:21:72:544:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:need  not  measure  our  full  set  of  17  potential  behavioral  biases  to  produce  a  valid  and  powerful  
33:1019:90436:21:20:72:543:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:behavioral sufficient statistic, and indeed it seems that measuring 2 suffices, at least when guided 
33:1020:90537:20:21:72:543:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:by theory as we are with our Narrow Sparsity B-count. Most fundamentally, our framework and 
33:1021:90629:21:21:72:544:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:results  suggest  that  one  can  use  directly  measured  sufficient  statistics  like  B-counts  to  identify  
33:1022:90742:21:20:71:542:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:consumer welfare loss associated with multiple, reinforcing behavioral biases, and that the welfare 
33:1023:90842:20:21:71:548:543:144:Times:144:Times:B:loss—the   behavioral   wedge   between   decision   and   experienced   utility—is   substantial   in   
33:1024:90947:21:21:71:128:522:144:Times:144:Times:B:magnitude. 
33:1025:90958:21:20:94:542:501:144:Times:144:Times:A:We close by highlighting some opportunities for future research using our data and methods, 
33:1026:91050:20:21:72:544:481:144:Times:144:Times:B:by  way  of  acknowledging  some  limitations  of  our  work  here. Our  results  linking  B-counts  to  
33:1027:91155:21:21:71:543:460:144:Times:144:Times:B:outcomes are probably better qualitative than quantitative estimates of the total consumer welfare 
33:1028:91254:21:21:71:543:439:144:Times:144:Times:B:loss  from  behavioral  biases,  given  aggregation  issues  with  both  utility  proxies  and  biases  that 
33:1029:91363:21:20:72:543:418:144:Times:144:Times:B:remain to be sorted out. Consumer welfare loss is more clearly due to non-preference biases than 
33:1030:91460:20:21:72:543:398:144:Times:144:Times:B:preference ones, in our results as well as in theory, highlighting the need for more work on how to 
33:1031:91560:21:21:72:544:377:144:Times:144:Times:B:evaluate  the  welfare  consequences  of  behavioral  preferences.  Consumer  welfare  loss  may  not  
33:1032:91663:21:20:72:543:356:144:Times:144:Times:B:equal social welfare loss, as behavioral biases can create opportunities for efficiency gains when 
33:1033:91762:20:21:72:544:336:144:Times:144:Times:B:there  are  market  failures  or  redistributive  motives  (see  Rees-Jones  and  Taubinsky  2018  for  a  
33:1034:91869:21:21:72:542:315:144:Times:144:Times:B:review), highlighting the need for more comprehensive welfare analysis. We use (partial) temporal 
33:1035:91967:21:20:72:541:294:144:Times:144:Times:B:stability in B-counts methodologically without addressing important substantive issues of how and 
33:1036:92065:20:21:72:542:274:144:Times:144:Times:B:why stability is incomplete: are behavioral sufficient statistics not fully trait-like, or is it more the 
33:1037:92171:21:21:72:542:253:144:Times:144:Times:B:case that summarizing behavioral tendencies is difficult to do with complete accuracy, or best done 
33:1038:92271:21:20:72:258:232:144:Times:144:Times:B:with reference to state-dependencies?  
33:1039:92310:20:21:94:543:212:144:Times:144:Times:A:Relatedly, our initial guidance on efficient measurement highlights that more work is needed 
33:1040:92403:21:21:72:548:191:144:Times:144:Times:B:to optimize   the  mapping  from  a  given  set  of  elicitation  data  into  a  sufficient  statistic,  and  on 
33:1041:92516:21:21:72:544:170:144:Times:144:Times:B:elicitation design.  Progress  on  the  former  may  require  further  development  of  model  selection  
33:1042:92622:21:20:71:545:149:144:Times:144:Times:B:techniques  that  allow  for  measurement  error.  Progress  on  the  latter  can  be  achieved  with  
33:1043:92725:20:21:71:542:129:144:Times:144:Times:B:experimentation. Although our elicitations are largely unincentivized on the margin—we elected 
33:1044:92820:21:21:72:544:108:144:Times:144:Times:B:to  allocate  more  of  our  scarce  research  budget  to  measuring  more  variables  for  a  larger  sample  
33:1045:92931:21:0:72:542:87:144:Times:144:Times:B:size—one might obtain better power by trading off sample size and/or the number of biases elicited 
34:1046:93032:0:20:72:543:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:for marginal incentives on a smaller number of elicitations. These tradeoffs are worth exploring, 
34:1047:93130:20:21:72:540:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:especially given the informativeness of B-counts that are based on elicitations of only a handful—
34:1048:93228:21:21:72:376:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:or even as few as two—potential sources of behavioral biases.  
34:1049:93291:21:21:94:542:646:144:Times:144:Times:A:Our main takeaway for future work is that measuring a behavioral sufficient statistic can be a 
34:1050:93386:21:20:72:550:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:valuable  and  practical  addition  to  many  research  designs   concerned  with  consumer  decision  
34:1051:93489:20:0:72:214:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:making and its implications.  
35:1052:93521:0:26:279:357:708:144:Times:144:Times:A:REFERENCES

51:1579:126332:0:26:72:380:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:Results Appendix. Additional results and robustness checks 
51:1580:126390:26:21:94:542:682:144:Times:144:Times:A:Appendix Table 4 confirms that the data warrant pooling. Each column presents an estimate 
51:1581:126480:21:21:71:544:661:144:Times:144:Times:B:of  a  B-count  coefficient  for  either  Round  1  ORIV  (odd-numbered  columns)  or  Round  2  ORIV  
51:1582:126583:21:20:71:540:640:144:Times:144:Times:B:(even-numbered  columns),  varying  outcomes  (objective or  subjective financial index)  and  B-
51:1583:126680:20:21:71:551:620:144:Times:144:Times:B:counts  (Full  B-count  and  the  two  Sparsity  B-counts). The B-   count  coefficients  are  qualitatively  
51:1584:126790:21:27:72:463:599:144:Times:144:Times:B:similar across rounds, and do not reject equality at conventional p-value cutoffs.  
51:1585:126874:27:20:94:543:572:144:Times:144:Times:A:Appendix Table 5 is one of several ways we address the possibility of spurious correlation. 
51:1586:126966:20:21:72:542:552:144:Times:144:Times:B:Here we focus on a reverse causality interpretation of our main results, through which having lower 
51:1587:127066:21:21:72:542:531:144:Times:144:Times:B:financial resources produces behavioral biases.51 We consider this hypothesis by varying our main 
51:1588:127164:21:21:72:543:510:144:Times:144:Times:B:specification in two ways. One way is using Round 2 data only and instrumenting for the Round 
51:1589:127258:21:20:72:543:489:144:Times:144:Times:B:2 B-count with the Round 1 B-count. That “Standard IV” approach uses only the 3-year-earlier 
51:1590:127351:20:21:72:542:469:144:Times:144:Times:B:measurements  of  behavioral  biases  to  identify  the  correlation  between  our  three  main  B-counts 
51:1591:127457:21:21:72:545:448:144:Times:144:Times:B:and  experienced  utility  (Columns  1,  4,  and  7).  The  second  way  is  conditioning  on  objective  
51:1592:127563:21:21:72:543:427:144:Times:144:Times:B:financial condition while using the subjective financial index as our experienced utility measure 
51:1593:127661:21:20:72:544:406:144:Times:144:Times:B:(as we do in Table 4); that may err on the side of over-controlling, but allows us to address the 
51:1594:127759:20:21:72:543:386:144:Times:144:Times:B:possibility that (objectively) low financial resources produce behavioral tendencies by controlling 
51:1595:127859:21:21:72:542:365:144:Times:144:Times:B:for the former (Columns 2, 5, and 8).52 Granting that possibility, we then instrument for Round 2 
51:1596:127957:21:20:71:543:344:144:Times:144:Times:B:objective financial condition with Round 1 objective financial condition in Columns 3, 6, and 9. 
51:1597:128054:20:21:71:542:324:144:Times:144:Times:B:The B-count conditional correlation with experienced utility remains strongly negative in each of 
51:1598:128151:21:21:71:524:303:144:Times:144:Times:B:these nine specifications, suggesting that our main results are not driven by reverse causality.  
51:1599:128248:21:21:94:539:282:144:Times:144:Times:A:Re: other spurious correlation hypotheses, we refer the reader back to Table 5 and Section 4-
51:1600:128341:21:20:71:544:261:144:Times:144:Times:B:D.   The  former  addresses  the  standard  omitted-variable,  unobserved  heterogeneity  concern  by  
51:1601:128444:20:21:71:543:241:144:Times:144:Times:B:varying control variable specifications. The latter details how our survey design and controls for 
51:1602:128543:21:21:71:543:220:144:Times:144:Times:B:survey  effort  minimize  the  likelihood  of  spurious  correlations  between  outcome  measures  and 
51:1603:128646:21:70:71:201:199:144:Times:144:Times:B:behavioral bias measures.  
51:1604:128673:70:17:72:542:129:48:Times:122:Times:F:51 We say “interpretation” instead of “concern” here, because if reverse causality were to drive the results, 
51:1605:128783:17:9:72:542:112:122:Times:122:Times:F:that would be important to discover in the sense that it would motivate a revamp of most behavioral models. 
51:1606:128891:9:16:72:542:103:48:Times:122:Times:F:52 We use “produce” instead of “exacerbate” here intentionally, to highlight another benefit of relying on 
51:1607:128998:16:13:72:545:87:122:Times:122:Times:F:discrete  measures  of  behavioral  biases:  in  our  setup  it  would  need  to  be  the  case  the  worse  financial  
51:1608:129118:13:0:72:497:74:122:Times:122:Times:F:condition increases the likelihood that people indicate any deviation from classical benchmarks. 
52:1609:129216:0:20:94:544:708:144:Times:144:Times:A:Appendix  Table 6  decomposes  the  subjective  and  objective  indexes  into  their  components,  
52:1610:129315:20:21:72:545:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:and  shows  that  links  between  B-counts  and  different  outcomes  are  robustly  negative  for  the  
52:1611:129420:21:21:72:539:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:component outcomes: all 27 coefficients are negative, 17 of the 27 B-count coefficients have p-
52:1612:129515:21:20:72:543:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:values <0.01, and each implies an economically large marginal change in the outcome variable per 
52:1613:129612:20:21:72:548:626:144:Times:144:Times:B:one   standard   deviation   change   in   the   B-count.   There   is   evidence   of   some   quantitative   
52:1614:129723:21:21:72:551:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:heterogeneity,  however,  including  within-index.  E.g.,  the  Full  B-c ount  coefficients  on  the  
52:1615:129826:21:20:72:543:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:subjective financial condition index components (each of which have p-values<0.01) range from 
52:1616:129920:20:27:72:264:564:144:Times:144:Times:B:-0.04 to -0.14 (Panel A, Columns 6-9).  
52:1617:129960:27:21:94:543:537:144:Times:144:Times:A:Appendix Table 7 confirms robustness to other functional forms for the B-count: the natural 
52:1618:130052:21:21:72:543:516:144:Times:144:Times:B:logarithm of the B-count, the ratio of the panelist’s B-count to their count of non-missing sources 
52:1619:130152:21:20:72:545:495:144:Times:144:Times:B:of  potential  behavioral  biases,  B-count  quartiles  (the  results  on  which  do  not  reject  a  linear  
52:1620:130262:20:21:72:544:475:144:Times:144:Times:B:relationship between outcomes and the B-count), and the “B-tile,” a consumer-level measure of 
52:1621:130356:21:21:72:542:454:144:Times:144:Times:B:the magnitude of behavioral deviations from classical benchmarks.53 The marginal effects in these 
52:1622:130454:21:21:72:545:433:144:Times:144:Times:B:alternative  specifications  hardly  differ  from  those  in  Table  4  at  all—note  how  similar  are  the  
52:1623:130564:21:20:72:355:412:144:Times:144:Times:B:d(Outcome)/d(1 SD B-count) levels across specifications.  
52:1624:130622:20:21:94:543:392:144:Times:144:Times:A:Untabulated results, where we estimate the specifications in Table 4 separately for different 
52:1625:130716:21:21:72:543:371:144:Times:144:Times:B:sub-groups based on demographics, etc., do not reject equality of the B-count coefficient across 
52:1626:130813:21:20:72:544:350:144:Times:144:Times:B:sub-groups.  Subject  to  the  caveat  that  these  tests  are  under-powered,  these  results  support  the  
52:1627:130923:20:21:72:543:330:144:Times:144:Times:B:assumption of a separable behavioral wedge (Section 4-C ). They also fail to support a knife-edge 
52:1628:131021:21:21:72:542:309:144:Times:144:Times:B:interpretation of our results in which a narrow subset of panelists drives the results. And they cast 
52:1629:131123:21:20:72:546:288:144:Times:144:Times:B:doubt  on  the  efficacy  of  targeting  behavioral  consumers  based  on  more  readily  observable  
52:1630:131225:20:51:72:243:268:144:Times:144:Times:B:characteristics (see also Section 7). 
52:1631:131262:51:17:72:542:217:48:Times:122:Times:F:53 Some of our bias measures are continuous, permitting percentiles to take on the full range of values from 
52:1632:131371:17:12:72:542:200:122:Times:122:Times:F:1 to 100. For discrete-response and uni-directional outcomes like loss aversion, the B–tiles take on fewer 
52:1633:131478:12:13:71:543:188:122:Times:122:Times:F:values but still measure the degree of deviation from classical benchmarks in useful ways. For example, 
52:1634:131582:13:13:71:543:175:122:Times:122:Times:F:loss  aversion  takes  on  four  values:  unbiased,  and  then  three  ordered  responses  (whether  the  individual  
52:1635:131700:13:12:71:543:162:122:Times:122:Times:F:respondent rejects the compound but not the single lottery, rejects the single but not the compound lottery, 
52:1636:131809:12:13:71:542:150:122:Times:122:Times:F:or rejects both) coded as 1/2/3. Any respondent accepting both lotteries receives a 0 (meets the classical 
52:1637:131916:13:12:71:543:137:122:Times:122:Times:F:benchmark),  and  37%  of  individuals  share  that  response.  Anyone  with  the  smallest  deviation  from  the  
52:1638:132031:12:13:71:541:125:122:Times:122:Times:F:benchmark therefore is in the 37th percentile, and 13% of responses fall into that category. Summing, anyone 
52:1639:132140:13:13:72:543:112:122:Times:122:Times:F:in  the  next  category  is  in  the  50th(=37th+13th),  and  so  on.  The  B-tile  calculates  each  person’s  percentile  
52:1640:132264:13:12:71:542:99:122:Times:122:Times:F:ranking for each of the 17 potential sources of behavioral bias, relative to others in the sample, and sums 
52:1641:132372:12:13:71:540:87:122:Times:122:Times:F:them.  If a person were to be the most biased person in the sample on all 17, that person would have a B-
52:1642:132477:13:0:71:160:74:122:Times:122:Times:F:tile of (close to) 17. 
53:1643:132502:0:20:94:543:708:144:Times:144:Times:A:Appendix Table 8 shows the full set of coefficients on the covariates in specifications (1)-(3) 
53:1644:132598:20:21:72:542:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:in Table 4. The table sheds light on the conditional correlations of other variables with outcomes, 
53:1645:132698:21:21:72:545:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:which  are  intuitive  for  the  most  part.  Income  is  positively  correlated  with  objective  financial  
53:1646:132808:21:20:72:543:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:condition  and,  consistent  with  research  on  happiness,  more  weakly  so with  subjective  financial  
53:1647:132915:20:21:72:542:626:144:Times:144:Times:B:well-being,  and  (weakly)  negatively  once  we  control  for  objective  financial  condition.  Other 
53:1648:133019:21:21:72:546:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:coefficients  in  the  first  and  second  columns  reverse  once  we  control  for  objective  financial  
53:1649:133126:21:20:72:545:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:condition  in  column  3,  showing  its  power  as  a  control  and  highlighting  the  robustness  of  the  
53:1650:133235:20:21:72:542:564:144:Times:144:Times:B:correlation between the B-count and financial condition in relative as well as absolute terms. These 
53:1651:133336:21:21:72:543:543:144:Times:144:Times:B:columns also highlight the relative magnitude of the coefficient on the B-count (subject to caveats 
53:1652:133436:21:21:72:543:522:144:Times:144:Times:B:re: over-controlling). E.g., for the objective financial condition outcome, a one-SD increase in the 
53:1653:133537:21:20:72:543:501:144:Times:144:Times:B:B-   count has roughly  the  same implied correlation as moving down two or three income deciles. 
53:1654:133635:20:21:72:547:481:144:Times:144:Times:B:For  subjective  financial  condition,  the  most  noteworthy  pattern  is  that  the  B-count,  and  
53:1655:133737:21:21:72:543:460:144:Times:144:Times:B:missingness thereon, have correlations that are more robust to the inclusion of objective financial 
53:1656:133837:21:20:72:543:439:144:Times:144:Times:B:condition as an additional covariate than any other variable or group of variables, with the possible 
53:1657:133939:20:27:72:247:419:144:Times:144:Times:B:exception of survey response times. 
53:1658:133975:27:21:94:543:392:144:Times:144:Times:A:Appendix  Table  9 examines whether  using  the  ALP’s  sampling  weights  changes  our  main  
53:1659:134070:21:20:72:542:371:144:Times:144:Times:B:empirical results (see Appendix Table 2 for a similar exercise re: B-count descriptive statistics). 
53:1660:134170:20:21:72:542:351:144:Times:144:Times:B:AT 9 compares weighted estimates to our main unweighted ones from Table 4 and reveals that the 
53:1661:134265:21:21:72:542:330:144:Times:144:Times:B:weighted  coefficients  are  uniformly more  negative  (i.e.,  larger  in  an  economic  sense)  in  point 
53:1662:134372:21:21:72:542:309:144:Times:144:Times:B:terms, but less precise (e.g., while each of the six unweighted coefficients has a p-value<0.01, two 
53:1663:134473:21:20:72:542:288:144:Times:144:Times:B:of the weighted coefficients has p-value <0.01 and one has a p>0.10). Mechanically, it must be the 
53:1664:134572:20:21:72:543:268:144:Times:144:Times:B:case that panelists who are under-sampled by RAND (and therefore over-weighted) have noisier 
53:1665:134665:21:21:72:542:247:144:Times:144:Times:B:relationships  between  our  outcomes and  covariates.  Re:  external  validity,  the  glass  half-empty 
53:1666:134770:21:20:71:545:226:144:Times:144:Times:B:interpretation  of  these  results  and  our  setup  is  that  ALP  sampling  weights  produce  noisier  
53:1667:134875:20:21:71:543:206:144:Times:144:Times:B:inferences on behavioral summary statistics and, in any case, are based on demographics but not 
53:1668:134971:21:21:71:543:185:144:Times:144:Times:B:our  variables  of  greatest  interest;  therefore,  the  extent  to  which  our  inferences  our  valid  for  the  
53:1669:135087:21:20:71:543:164:144:Times:144:Times:B:entire  U.S.  population  is  an  open  question.  The  glass  half-full  interpretation  is  that  we  have  an  
53:1670:135201:20:21:71:543:144:144:Times:144:Times:B:unusually broad sample compared to most studies in the behavioral social sciences, and that our 
53:1671:135297:21:21:71:544:123:144:Times:144:Times:B:results  on  B-count  properties  and  their  conditional  correlations  with  outcomes  are  not  unduly  
53:1672:135404:21:21:71:510:102:144:Times:144:Times:B:sensitive to weighting that is designed to produce valid inferences for the U.S. population.  
53:1673:135498:21:0:94:97:81:144:Times:144:Times:A:

56:1791:141132:619:13:87:1145:260:121:Times:121:Times:F:Retirement and non-retirement savings adequacy questions are placed one each in the two different modules, with different wording, to mitigate mechanical correlations. The questions are: "Using any number from one to five, where one 
56:1792:141365:13:14:87:1164:247:121:Times:121:Times:F:equals not nearly enough, and five equals much more than enough, do you feel that your household is saving and investing enough for retirement? Please consider the income you and any other members of your household expect to receive from 
56:1793:141603:14:14:87:1160:233:121:Times:121:Times:F:Social Security, 401(k) accounts, other job retirement accounts and job pensions, and any additional assets you or other members of your household have or expect to have" and "Now, apart from retirement savings, please think about how your 
56:1794:141843:14:13:87:1158:219:121:Times:121:Times:F:household typically uses the money you have: how much is spent and how much is saved or invested. Now choose which statement best describes your household". These questions are variants on standard ones, but in each case our 5 response 
56:1795:142079:13:14:87:1126:206:121:Times:121:Times:F:options are framed to encourage people to recognize tradeoffs between saving and consumption: any response that includes "saving more" also includes "and borrowing/spending less", and vice versa. In mapping the 5 responses into the 
56:1796:142311:14:29:87:1145:192:121:Times:121:Times:F:variables used here, we code: saved-enough, more-than-enough, and much-more-than-enough as 1 (the latter two responses are rare: 3% of the sample for retirement, and 4% for non-retirement); saved < enough as 0.5; saved << enough as 0.

56:1800:143264:14:0:87:996:121:121:Times:121:Times:F:question across the four ALP modules in which it has appeared subsequent to our round 1 modules, as of this writing. Of the 509/845 panelists with at least one non-missing response, 474 have at least two, .
