67:2177:163170:0:28:267:347:708:144:Times:144:Times:A:Data Appendix 
67:2178:163183:28:29:72:242:680:144:Times:144:Times:B:1. Measuring Behavioral Biases 
67:2179:163212:29:29:72:497:651:144:Times:144:Times:B:This section details, for each of the 17 potential sources of behavioral bias we measure:  
67:2180:163302:29:21:90:544:622:144:Times:144:Times:A:i) The  motive   for eliciting  that potential  source  of  bias  (B-   factor)  and the  mechanism  
67:2181:163402:21:20:126:411:601:144:Times:144:Times:A:through which that factor might affect financial condition;  
67:2182:163462:20:21:90:354:581:144:Times:144:Times:A:ii) our elicitation method and its key antecedents;  
67:2183:163514:21:21:90:384:560:144:Times:144:Times:A:iii) data quality indicators, including item non-response;  
67:2184:163573:21:20:90:396:539:144:Times:144:Times:A:iv) sample size (as it compares to that for other B-factors);  
67:2185:163635:20:21:90:543:519:144:Times:144:Times:A:v) definitions and prevalence estimates of behavioral indicators, with background on the 
67:2186:163723:21:21:126:543:498:144:Times:144:Times:A:distinctions  between  expected  direction  (standard)  vs. less-expected  (non-standard) 
67:2187:163813:21:20:126:294:477:144:Times:144:Times:A:direction biases where applicable;  
67:2188:163848:20:21:90:544:457:144:Times:144:Times:A:vi) descriptions  of  the  magnitude  and heterogeneity of  behavioral  deviations,  including  
67:2189:163942:21:21:126:547:436:144:Times:144:Times:A:descriptions  of  the  distribution  and—where  the  data  permit—estimates  of  key  
67:2190:164028:21:29:126:316:415:144:Times:144:Times:A:parameters used in behavioral models;  
67:2191:164067:29:20:72:543:386:144:Times:144:Times:B:Since  our  empirical  work  here  is  purely  descriptive,  we  focus  on  our Round  1  data  (ALP 
67:2192:164168:20:21:72:543:366:144:Times:144:Times:B:modules 315 and 352) to get the largest possible sample of panelists. We provide comparisons to 
67:2193:164264:21:29:72:219:345:144:Times:144:Times:B:prior work wherever possible. 
67:2194:164294:29:20:72:316:316:144:Times:144:Times:B:A. Present- or future-biased discounting (money) 
67:2195:164342:20:21:72:544:296:144:Times:144:Times:B:Time-inconsistent discounting has been linked, both theoretically and empirically, to low levels 
67:2196:164439:21:21:71:542:275:144:Times:144:Times:B:of saving and high levels of borrowing (e.g., Laibson 1997; Meier and Sprenger 2010; Toubia et 
67:2197:164534:21:20:71:120:254:144:Times:144:Times:B:al. 2013). 
67:2198:164545:20:21:89:545:234:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  measure  discounting  biases  with  respect  to  money  using  the  Convex  Time  Budgets  
67:2199:164640:21:21:71:543:213:144:Times:144:Times:B:(CTB) method created by Andreoni and Sprenger (2012). In our version, fielded in ALP module 
67:2200:164732:21:20:72:542:192:144:Times:144:Times:B:315 (the first of our two surveys), subjects make 24 decisions, allocating 100 hypothetical tokens 
67:2201:164831:20:21:72:542:172:144:Times:144:Times:B:each between (weakly) smaller-sooner and larger-later amounts. See Data Appendix Figure 1 for 
67:2202:164925:21:21:72:543:151:144:Times:144:Times:B:an example.  The 24 decisions are spread across 4 different screens with 6 decisions each. Each 
67:2203:165021:21:21:72:543:130:144:Times:144:Times:B:screen varies start date (today or 5 weeks from today) x delay length (5 weeks or 9 weeks); each 
67:2204:165118:21:20:72:545:109:144:Times:144:Times:B:decision  within  a  screen  offers  a  different  yield  on  saving.  Among  the  1,515  individuals  who  
67:2205:165226:20:0:72:543:89:144:Times:144:Times:B:take  our  first  module  in  Round  1,  1,502 subjects  make  at  least  one  CTB  choice,  and the  1,422 
68:2206:165336:0:20:72:544:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:who  complete  at  least  the  first  and  last  decisions  on  each  of  the  4  screens  comprise  our  CTB  
68:2207:165447:20:21:72:112:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:sample. 
68:2208:165455:21:21:90:543:667:144:Times:144:Times:A:The CTB already has been implemented successfully in field contexts in the U.S. (Barcellos 
68:2209:165546:21:21:72:544:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:and  Carvalho  2014;  Carvalho,  Meier,  and  Wang  2016)  and  elsewhere  (Giné    et  al.  2018).  In  
68:2210:165651:21:20:72:543:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:exploring data quality and prevalence below we focus on comparisons to Andreoni and Sprenger 
68:2211:165744:20:21:72:545:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:(2012), and  Barcellos  and  Carvalho  (2014).1  AS  draw  their  sample  from  university  students.  
68:2212:165847:21:21:72:546:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:BC’s  sample  is  drawn  from  the  ALP,  like  ours  (module  212  in  their  case),  but  they  use  a  
68:2213:165953:21:21:72:229:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:different adaptation of the CTB. 
68:2214:165986:21:20:90:543:542:144:Times:144:Times:A:Indicators of response quality are encouraging for the most part. Interior allocations are more 
68:2215:166082:20:21:72:544:522:144:Times:144:Times:B:common  in  our  sample  than  in  AS,  and  comparable  to  BC.  More  of  our  subjects  exhibit  some  
68:2216:166188:21:21:72:542:501:144:Times:144:Times:B:variance in their allocations than AS or BC. Our subjects are internally consistent overall—e.g., 
68:2217:166286:21:20:72:543:480:144:Times:144:Times:B:exhibiting  strong  correlations  in  choices  across  different  screens  and  delay  dates—but  41%  do 
68:2218:166392:20:21:72:544:460:144:Times:144:Times:B:exhibit  some  upward-sloping  demand  among  20  pairs  of  decisions,  a  figure  that  is  within  the  
68:2219:166499:21:21:72:505:439:144:Times:144:Times:B:range commonly found in discount rate elicitations but high compared to the 8% in AS.2   
68:2220:166588:21:21:90:545:418:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  calculate  biased  discounting,  for  each  individual,  by  subtracting  the  consumption  rate  
68:2221:166690:21:21:72:545:397:144:Times:144:Times:B:when  the  sooner  payment  date  is  five  weeks  from  today  from  the  consumption  rate  when  the  
68:2222:166795:21:20:72:545:376:144:Times:144:Times:B:sooner  payment  date  is  today,  for  each  of  the  two  delay  lengths.  We  then  average  the  two  
68:2223:166901:20:21:72:545:356:144:Times:144:Times:B:differences  to  get  a  continuous  measure  of  biased  discounting.  In  keeping  with  AS,  BC  and  
68:2224:167006:21:21:72:543:335:144:Times:144:Times:B:several  other  recent  papers  (including  Carvalho,  Meier,  and  Wang  (2016) and  Goda  et  al. 
68:2225:167105:21:20:72:544:314:144:Times:144:Times:B:(2017)), we find little if any present-bias on average, with a median discount bias of zero, and a 
68:2226:167204:20:29:72:238:294:144:Times:144:Times:B:1pp mean tilt toward future bias.3  
68:2227:167240:29:21:90:540:265:144:Times:144:Times:A:Indicators  of  behavioral  deviations  here  are  bi-directional:  we  label  someone  as  present-
68:2228:167340:21:21:71:542:244:144:Times:144:Times:B:biased (future-biased) if the average difference is >0 (<0). We deem present-bias the “standard” 
68:2229:167437:21:31:71:539:223:144:Times:144:Times:B:direction,  since  future-bias  is  relatively  poorly  understood.4  Counting any  deviation  from  time-
68:2230:167543:31:16:72:543:192:42:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:1  Carvalho,  Meier,  and  Wang  use  the  American  Life  Panel  like  we  and  Barcello  and  Carvalho,  but  on  a  
68:2231:167662:16:9:72:258:176:121:Times:121:Times:F:lower-income sample (ALP module 126). 
68:2232:167699:9:17:72:544:167:42:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:2  High  rates  of  non-monotonic  demand  are  not  uncommon  in  discount  rate  elicitation:  Andreoni  and  
68:2233:167811:17:12:71:543:150:121:Times:121:Times:F:Sprenger  (2012)  report  rates  ranging  from  10  to  50  percent  in  their  literature  review.  In  Barcellos  and  
68:2234:167932:12:13:71:543:138:121:Times:121:Times:F:Carvalho 26% of subjects exhibit some upward-sloping demand, among only 4 pairs of decisions. In our 
68:2235:168033:13:13:71:546:125:121:Times:121:Times:F:sample  non-monotonic  demand  is  strongly  correlated  within-subject  across  the  four  screens,  and  
68:2236:168140:13:9:71:519:112:121:Times:121:Times:F:decreases slightly by the final screen, suggesting that responses are picking up something systematic. 
68:2237:168243:9:16:72:522:103:42:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:3 Bradford et al. (2017) do find present-bias on average in their Qualtrics sample, classifying >50% as 
68:2238:168347:16:9:71:255:87:121:Times:121:Times:F:present-biased and 26% as future-biased. 
68:2239:168387:9:0:72:442:78:42:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:4 Although see Koszegi and Szeidl (2013) for a theory of future-biased discounting. 
69:2240:168472:0:20:72:543:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:consistent discounting as biased, 26% of our sample is present-biased and 36% is future-biased. 
69:2241:168568:20:21:71:542:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:These prevalence estimates fall substantially if we set    a higher threshold for classifying someone 
69:2242:168670:21:21:71:540:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:as behavioral; e.g., if we count only deviations > |20|pp, then only 3% of the sample is present-
69:2243:168767:21:21:71:544:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:biased  and  5%  future-biased.  Compared  to  prior  prevalence  estimates,  our  zero-threshold  ones  
69:2244:168872:21:20:71:543:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:are in the middle of the range (Data Appendix Table 1). E.g., BC’s CTB elicitation in the ALP 
69:2245:168966:20:21:71:544:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:shows  29%  with  any  present-bias,  and  37%  with  any  future-bias.  Goda et  al.  use  a  different  
69:2246:169072:21:21:71:543:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:elicitation method—a “time-staircase” multiple price list (Falk et al. 2016)—and classify 55% of 
69:2247:169169:21:20:71:543:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:their nationally representative sample (from the ALP and another online panel) as present-biased. 
69:2248:169267:20:29:71:426:543:144:Times:144:Times:B:In the AS sample 14% exhibit any present-bias and 12% any future-bias.  
69:2249:169339:29:21:89:542:514:144:Times:144:Times:A:Interestingly, if we follow AS and use the CTB data to structurally estimate discounting-bias 
69:2250:169433:21:20:71:544:493:144:Times:144:Times:B:parameter  values  for  each  individual,  we  find  that  90%  of  our  subjects  with  no  monotonicity  
69:2251:169540:20:21:71:544:473:144:Times:144:Times:B:violations lie within the interval [0.93, 1.07] (Data Appendix Table 1, Columns 11-13).5 This is 
69:2252:169637:21:21:72:546:452:144:Times:144:Times:B:noteworthy  because  behavioral  macro  papers  sometimes  assume  representative  agents  with  
69:2253:169734:21:21:72:542:431:144:Times:144:g_d0_f52:B:present bias that lies strictly below our 5th percentile (see, e.g., (İmrohoroğlu, İmrohoroğlu, and 
69:2254:169834:21:21:71:543:410:144:Times:144:Times:B:Joines 2003; Graham and Snower 2013; Pérez Kakabadse and Palacios Huerta 2013). As Harris 
69:2255:169924:21:20:71:544:389:144:Times:144:Times:B:and Laibson (2013) state: “the short-run discount factor... is typically thought to lie between ½ 
69:2256:170022:20:21:71:515:369:144:Times:144:Times:B:and 1.” Our estimates should give researchers pause before choosing a value much below 1. 
69:2257:170112:21:21:89:543:348:144:Times:144:Times:A:Previous  studies estimate relationships  between  directly  elicited  discounting  biases  and 
69:2258:170208:21:20:71:542:327:144:Times:144:Times:B:outcomes  in  broad  samples  (Bradford  et  al.  2017;  Eisenhauer  and  Ventura  2006;  Goda  et  al. 
69:2259:170312:20:21:71:542:307:144:Times:144:Times:B:2017).6  We  use CTBs  rather  than  Multiple  Price  Lists,  test  more  flexible  functional  forms,  and 
69:2260:170420:21:21:72:543:286:144:Times:144:Times:B:control for  a  much  richer  set  of  (behavioral)  factors  that  could  be  correlated  with  both 
69:2261:170522:21:21:72:210:265:144:Times:144:Times:B:discounting and outcomes.7  
69:2262:170550:21:29:72:306:244:144:Times:144:Times:B:B. Present- or future-biased discounting (food) 
69:2263:170597:29:21:90:542:215:144:Times:144:Times:A:In   light   of   evidence   that   discounting   can   differ   within-subject   across   domains   (e.g., 
69:2264:170705:21:20:72:546:194:144:Times:144:Times:B:Augenblick,  Niederle,  and  Sprenger  2015),  we  also  obtain  a  coarse  measure  of  discounting  
69:2265:170807:20:32:72:545:174:144:Times:144:Times:B:biases  for  consumption  per  se,  by  asking  two  questions  that  follow  Read  and  van  Leeuwen

70:2287:172887:29:28:71:509:381:144:Times:144:Times:B:C. Inconsistency with General Axiom of Revealed Preference (and dominance avoidance) 
70:2288:172971:28:21:89:545:353:144:Times:144:Times:A:Our  third  and  fourth  behavioral  factors  follow  Choi  et  al.  (2014),  which  measures  choice  
70:2289:173074:21:21:71:547:332:144:Times:144:Times:B:inconsistency   with   standard   economic   rationality.   Choice   inconsistency   could indicate   a   
70:2290:173180:21:20:72:543:311:144:Times:144:Times:B:tendency  to  make  poor  (costly)  decisions  in  field  contexts;  indeed,  Choi  et  al.  (2014)  find  that  
70:2291:173293:20:21:71:542:291:144:Times:144:Times:B:more choice inconsistency is conditionally correlated with less wealth in a representative sample 
70:2292:173391:21:29:71:179:270:144:Times:144:Times:B:of Dutch households.  
70:2293:173413:29:20:89:544:241:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  use  the  same  task  and  user  interface  as  in  Choi  et  al.  (2014) but  abbreviate  it  from  25  
70:2294:173522:20:22:71:543:221:144:Times:144:Times:B:decisions to 11.9 Each decision confronts respondents with a linear budget constraint under risk: 
70:2295:173620:22:20:71:543:199:144:Times:144:Times:B:subjects choose a point on the line, and then the computer randomly chooses whether to pay the 
70:2296:173715:20:25:71:542:179:144:Times:144:Times:B:point value of the x-axis or the y-axis. 1,270 of the 1,427 individuals taking our second Round 1 
70:2297:173813:25:17:72:540:154:42:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:8  If  we  limit  the  sample  to  those  who  did  not  receive  the  informational/debiasing  treatment  about  self-

70:2300:174046:9:17:72:543:116:42:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:9 We were quite constrained on survey time and hence conducted a pilot in which we tested the feasibility 
70:2301:174152:17:12:72:542:99:121:Times:121:Times:F:of capturing roughly equivalent information with fewer rounds. 58 pilot-testers completed 25 rounds, and

71:2304:174472:0:21:72:543:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:module make  all  11  decisions,  and  comprise  our  sample  for  measuring  choice  inconsistency.10

71:2306:174617:28:21:90:543:659:144:Times:144:Times:A:Following Choi et al., we average across these 11 decisions, within-consumer, to benchmark 
71:2307:174708:21:21:72:547:638:144:Times:144:Times:B:choices  against  two  different  standards  of  rationality.  One  benchmark  is  a  complete  and  
71:2308:174809:21:21:72:544:617:144:Times:144:Times:B:transitive  preference  ordering  adhering  to  the  General  Axiom  of  Revealed  Preference  (GARP),  
71:2309:174913:21:20:72:543:596:144:Times:144:Times:B:as captured by the Afriat (1972) Critical Cost Efficiency Index. 1-CCEI can be interpreted as the 
71:2310:175011:20:21:71:544:576:144:Times:144:Times:B:subject’s  degree  of  choice  inconsistency:  the  percentage  points  of  potential  earnings  “wasted”  
71:2311:175118:21:21:71:542:555:144:Times:144:Times:B:per the GARP standard. But as Choi et al. discuss, consistency with GARP is not necessarily the 
71:2312:175214:21:20:71:542:534:144:Times:144:Times:B:most appealing measure of decision quality because it allows for violations of monotonicity with 
71:2313:175311:20:21:71:543:514:144:Times:144:Times:B:respect  to  first-order  stochastic  dominance  (FOSD).11  Hence,  again  following  Choi  et  al.,  our 
71:2314:175417:21:21:72:546:493:144:Times:144:Times:B:second measure captures inconsistency with both GARP and FOSD.12   Note that these measures 
71:2315:175509:21:29:72:479:472:144:Times:144:Times:B:of inconsistency are unidirectional: there is no such thing as being overly consistent. 
71:2316:175596:29:21:90:543:443:144:Times:144:Times:A:Our distribution of individual-level CCEI estimates is nearly identical to Choi et al.’s— if we 
71:2317:175692:21:21:72:544:422:144:Times:144:Times:B:use only the first 11 rounds of choices from Choi et al. to maximize comparability to our setup. 
71:2318:175789:21:20:72:543:401:144:Times:144:Times:B:Our median (1-CCEI) is 0.002, suggesting nearly complete consistency with GARP. The mean is 
71:2319:175880:20:21:72:543:381:144:Times:144:Times:B:0.05. The median (1-combined-CCEI), capturing FOSD violations as well, is 0.10, with a mean 
71:2320:175972:21:21:72:543:360:144:Times:144:Times:B:of  0.16.  C hoice  inconsistency  is  substantially  higher  when  using  the  full  25  rounds  in  both  our  
71:2321:176085:21:20:72:544:339:144:Times:144:Times:B:pilot  data  and  Choi  et  al.  ( e.g.,  mean  CCEI  of  0.12  in  both  samples),  and  we  have  verified  that  
71:2322:176201:20:21:72:545:319:144:Times:144:Times:B:this  is  a  mechanical  effect  (more  rounds  means  more  opportunities  to  exhibit  inconsistency)  
71:2323:176306:21:21:72:543:298:144:Times:144:Times:B:rather than deterioration in consistency as rounds increase, by finding that CCEIs measured over 
71:2324:176403:21:20:72:542:277:144:Times:144:Times:B:small blocks of consecutive rounds remain constant as the average round number of those blocks 
71:2325:176498:20:29:71:121:257:144:Times:144:Times:B:increases.

71:2330:176900:9:17:72:544:154:42:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:11  E.g.,  someone  who  always  allocates  all  tokens  to  account  X  is  consistent  with  GARP  if  they  are

72:2337:177555:0:20:72:543:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:GARP, and 96% exhibit any inconsistency with GARP or FOSD. If we set a 20pp threshold for
