72:2343:178105:29:29:72:315:568:144:Times:144:Times:B:D. Risk attitude re: certainty (certainty premium) 
72:2344:178154:29:20:90:543:539:144:Times:144:Times:A:Behavioral researchers have long noted a seemingly disproportionate preference for certainty 
72:2345:178247:20:21:72:544:519:144:Times:144:Times:B:(PFC) among  some  consumers  and  posited  various  theories  to  explain  it:  Cumulative  Prospect  
72:2346:178350:21:21:72:543:498:144:Times:144:Times:B:Theory (Daniel   Kahneman   and   Tversky   1979;   Amos   Tversky   and   Kahneman   1992), 
72:2347:178443:21:20:72:543:477:144:Times:144:Times:B:Disappointment Aversion (Bell 1985; Loomes and Sugden 1986; Gul 1991), and u-v preferences 
72:2348:178534:20:21:72:544:457:144:Times:144:Times:B:(Neilson 1992; Schmidt 1998; Diecidue, Schmidt, and Wakker 2004). PFC may help to explain 
72:2349:178624:21:21:72:539:436:144:Times:144:Times:B:seemingly  extreme  risk  averse  behavior,  which  could  in  turn  lead  to  lower  wealth  in  the  cross-
72:2350:178733:21:28:71:111:415:144:Times:144:Times:B:section. 
72:2351:178741:28:22:89:543:387:144:Times:144:Times:A:We use Callen et al.’s (2014) two-task method13 for measuring a subject’s certainty premium 
72:2352:178833:22:21:72:542:365:144:Times:144:Times:B:(CP).14 Similar to Holt and Laury tasks, in one of the Callen et al. tasks subjects make 10 choices 
72:2353:178933:21:20:72:543:344:144:Times:144:Times:B:between two lotteries, one a (p, 1-p) gamble over X and Y > X , (p; X, Y), the other a (q, 1-q) 
72:2354:179029:20:21:72:545:324:144:Times:144:Times:B:gamble  over  Y  and  0,  (q;  Y,  0).  Both  Callen  et  al. and  we  fix  Y  and  X  at  450  and  150  
72:2355:179135:21:21:72:544:303:144:Times:144:Times:B:(hypothetical dollars in our case, hypothetical Afghanis in theirs), fix p at 0.5, and have q range 
72:2356:179235:21:20:72:544:282:144:Times:144:Times:B:from  0.1  to  1.0  in  increments  of  0.1.  In  the  other  task,  p  =  1,  so  the  subject  chooses  between  a  
72:2357:179353:20:21:72:543:262:144:Times:144:Times:B:lottery and a certain option. Our two tasks are identical to Callen et al.’s except for the currency 
72:2358:179454:21:21:72:544:241:144:Times:144:Times:B:units.  But  our  settings,  implementation,  and  use  of  the  elicited  data  are  different.  Callen  et  al.  
72:2359:179569:21:21:72:547:220:144:Times:144:Times:B:administer  the  tasks  in-person,  using  trained  surveyors,  at  polling  centers  and  homes  in  
72:2360:179671:21:28:72:489:199:144:Times:144:Times:B:Afghanistan. They use the data to examine the effects of violence on risk preferences.  
72:2361:179758:28:21:90:543:171:144:Times:144:Times:A:1,463  of  1,505  (97%)  of  our  subjects  who  started  the  tasks  completed  all  20  choices 
72:2362:179856:21:34:72:543:150:144:Times:144:Times:B:(compared to 977/1127 = 87% in Callen et al.). As is typical with Holt-Laury tasks, we exclude 
72:2363:179951:34:16:72:543:116:42:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:13 Callen et al. describes its task as “a field-ready, two-question modification of the uncertainty equivalent 
72:2364:180062:16:10:72:272:100:121:Times:121:Times:F:presented in Andreoni and Sprenger (2016).” 
72:2365:180106:10:16:72:544:90:42:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:14  The  Callen  et  al.  tasks  also  elicit  non-parametric  measures  of  classical  risk  aversion:  a  higher  switch  
72:2366:180230:16:0:71:481:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:point indicates greater risk aversion. We discuss these measures in Section 1-D of the paper. 
73:2367:180326:0:20:72:543:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:some subjects whose choices indicate miscomprehension of or inattention to the task. 11% of our 
73:2368:180422:20:21:72:547:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:subjects multiple-switch  on  our  two-lottery  task  (compared  to  10%  in  Callen  et  al.),  and  9%  of  
73:2369:180532:21:21:72:542:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:our subjects multiple-switch on the lottery vs. certain option tasks (compared to 13% in Callen et 
73:2370:180631:21:21:72:543:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:al.). 14% of our subjects switch too soon for monotonic utility in the two-lottery—in rows [2, 4] 
73:2371:180729:21:20:71:542:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:in the two-lottery task—compared to 13% in Callen et al. All told, 19% of our subjects exhibit a 
73:2372:180826:20:21:71:545:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:puzzling  switch  (17%  in  Callen  et  al.),  leaving  us  with  1,188  usable  observations.  Of  these  
73:2373:180933:21:21:71:542:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:subjects,  1,049 switch  on  both  tasks,  as  is  required  to  estimate  CP.  Of  these  1,049,  only  30% 
73:2374:181042:21:28:71:426:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:switch at the same point on both tasks, in contrast to 63% in Callen et al.  
73:2375:181119:28:21:89:543:535:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  estimate  CP  for  each  respondent  i  by  imputing  the  likelihoods  q*  at  which  i  expresses  
73:2376:181224:21:21:71:542:514:144:Times:144:Times:B:indifference  as  the  midpoint  of  the  q  interval  at  which  i  switches,  and  then  using  the  two 
73:2377:181331:21:20:71:542:493:144:Times:144:Times:B:likelihoods to estimate the indirect utility components of the CP formula. As Callen et al. detail, 
73:2378:181431:20:21:71:542:473:144:Times:144:Times:B:the CP “is defined in probability units of the high outcome, Y, such that one can refer to certainty 
73:2379:181532:21:21:71:543:452:144:Times:144:Times:B:of  X  being  worth  a  specific  percent  chance  of  Y  relative  to  its  uncertain  value.”  We  estimate  a  
73:2380:181646:21:21:71:543:431:144:Times:144:Times:B:mean CP of 0.16 in our sample (SD=0.24, median =0.15), compared to 0.37 (SD=0.15) in Callen 
73:2381:181738:21:20:72:543:410:144:Times:144:Times:B:et al. Their findings suggest that much of the difference could be explained by greater exposure 
73:2382:181835:20:29:71:208:390:144:Times:144:Times:B:to violence in their sample.  
73:2383:181864:29:21:89:542:361:144:Times:144:Times:A:As Callen et al. detail, the sign of CP also carries broader information about preferences. CP 
73:2384:181959:21:20:71:543:340:144:Times:144:Times:B:= 0 indicates an expected utility maximizer. CP>0 indicates a preference for certainty (PFC), as 
73:2385:182056:20:21:71:543:320:144:Times:144:Times:B:in models of disappointment aversion or u-v preferences. We classify 77% of our sample as PFC 
73:2386:182150:21:21:71:542:299:144:Times:144:Times:B:type based on an any-deviation threshold. This falls to 73%, 60%, or 42% if we count only larger 
73:2387:182247:21:20:71:543:278:144:Times:144:Times:B:deviations >0 (5pp, 10pp, or 20pp) as behavioral. In Callen et al. 99.63% of the sample exhibits 
73:2388:182344:20:21:71:543:258:144:Times:144:Times:B:PFC. CP<0 indicates a cumulative prospect theory (CPT) type, and we classify 23%, 20%, 13% 
73:2389:182435:21:21:71:544:237:144:Times:144:Times:B:or  7%  as  CPT  under  the  different  deviation  thresholds. We  denote  PFC  as  the  standard  bias,  
73:2390:182541:21:28:71:504:216:144:Times:144:Times:B:simply because CP>0 is far more common than CP<0 in both our data and Callen et al.’s. 
73:2391:182628:28:21:89:552:188:144:Times:144:Times:A:Callen et  al.  find significant  correlations    between  the  CP  and  financial  outcomes,  in  
73:2392:182727:21:21:71:544:167:144:Times:144:Times:B:particular  with  avoiding  late  loan  repayments,15  but  their  data  lack  controls  for  cognitive  skills  
73:2393:182840:21:43:72:170:146:144:Times:144:Times:B:and other B-factors. 
73:2394:182860:43:16:72:545:103:42:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:15 The theoretical mapping from late loan repayments to our indices of financial condition is  unclear under 
73:2395:182969:16:13:71:520:87:121:Times:121:Times:F:limited liability, and the average relationship (not conditioning on borrowing) more ambiguous, since 
73:2396:183071:13:0:71:523:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:borrowing could lead to (weakly) greater or lesser wealth if consumers are behavioral (Zinman 2014). 
74:2397:183174:0:28:72:287:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:E. Loss aversion/small-stakes risk aversion 
74:2398:183216:28:21:90:543:680:144:Times:144:Times:A:Loss aversion refers to placing higher weight on losses than gains, in utility terms. It is one of 
74:2399:183315:21:21:72:542:659:144:Times:144:Times:B:the  most  influential  concepts  in  the  behavioral  social  sciences,  with  seminal  papers—e.g., 
74:2400:183417:21:21:72:546:638:144:Times:144:Times:B:Tversky  and  Kahneman  (1992) and  Benartzi  and  Thaler  (1995)—producing  thousands  of  
74:2401:183509:21:20:72:542:617:144:Times:144:Times:B:citations. Loss  aversion  has  been  implicated  in  various  portfolio  choices  (Barberis  2013)  and 
74:2402:183614:20:29:71:459:597:144:Times:144:Times:B:consumption dynamics (Kőszegi and Rabin 2009) that can lead to lower wealth. 
74:2403:183691:29:21:89:542:568:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  measure  loss  aversion  using  the  two  choices  developed  by  Fehr  and  Goette  (2007) in 
74:2404:183790:21:20:71:546:547:144:Times:144:Times:B:their  study  of  the  labor  supply  of  bike  messengers  (see  Abeler  et  al.  (2011) for  a  similar  
74:2405:183897:20:21:71:542:527:144:Times:144:Times:B:elicitation method). Choice 1 is between a lottery with a 50% chance of winning $80 and a 50% 
74:2406:183991:21:21:71:542:506:144:Times:144:Times:B:chance of losing $50, and zero dollars. Choice two is between playing the lottery in Choice 1 six 
74:2407:184089:21:20:71:542:485:144:Times:144:Times:B:times,  and  zero  dollars.  As  Fehr  and  Goette  (FG)  show,  if  subjects  have  reference-dependent 
74:2408:184194:20:21:71:543:465:144:Times:144:Times:B:preferences, then subjects who reject lottery 1 have a higher level of loss aversion than subjects 
74:2409:184293:21:21:71:544:444:144:Times:144:Times:B:who accept lottery 1, and subjects who reject both lotteries have a higher level of loss aversion 
74:2410:184391:21:20:71:543:423:144:Times:144:Times:B:than subjects who reject only lottery 1. In addition, if subjects' loss aversion is consistent across 
74:2411:184493:20:21:71:543:403:144:Times:144:Times:B:the two lotteries, then any individual who rejects lottery 2 should also reject lottery 1 because a 
74:2412:184593:21:21:71:543:382:144:Times:144:Times:B:rejection  of  lottery  2  implies  a  higher  level  of  loss  aversion  than  a  rejection  of  only  lottery  1.  
74:2413:184710:21:21:71:543:361:144:Times:144:Times:B:Other  researchers  have  noted  that,  even  in  the  absence  of  loss  aversion,  choosing  Option  B  is  
74:2414:184820:21:21:71:545:340:144:Times:144:Times:B:compatible  with  small-stakes  risk  aversion.16  We  acknowledge  this  but  use  “loss  aversion”  
74:2415:184922:21:20:72:545:319:144:Times:144:Times:B:instead  of  “loss  aversion  and/or  small-stakes  risk  aversion”  as  shorthand.  Small-stakes  risk  
74:2416:185027:20:21:71:544:299:144:Times:144:Times:B:aversion  is  also  often  classified  as  behavioral  because  it  is  incompatible  with  expected  utility  
74:2417:185138:21:29:71:174:278:144:Times:144:Times:B:theory (Rabin 2000). 
74:2418:185159:29:20:89:543:249:144:Times:144:Times:A:Response  rates  suggest  a  high  level  of  comfort  with  these  questions;  only  two  of  our  1,515  
74:2419:185266:20:21:71:542:229:144:Times:144:Times:B:subjects skip, and only two more who answer the first question do not answer the second. 37% of 
74:2420:185362:21:21:72:546:208:144:Times:144:Times:B:our  1,511  respondents reject  both  lotteries,  consistent  with  relatively  extreme  loss  aversion,  
74:2421:185468:21:21:72:546:187:144:Times:144:Times:B:compared  to  45%  of  FG’s  42  subjects.  Another  36%  of  our  subjects  accept  both  lotteries,  
74:2422:185571:21:20:72:544:166:144:Times:144:Times:B:consistent with classical behavior, compared to 33% in FG. The remaining 27% of our subjects 
74:2423:185664:20:21:72:543:146:144:Times:144:Times:B:(and 21% of FG’s) exhibit moderate loss aversion, playing one lottery but not the other, with our 
74:2424:185762:21:35:72:545:125:144:Times:144:Times:B:main  difference  from  FG  being  that  14%  of  our  subjects  (vs.  only  2%  of  theirs)  exhibit  the  
74:2425:185870:35:16:72:544:90:42:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:16  A  related  point  is  that  there  is  no  known  “model-free”  method  of  eliciting  loss  aversion  (Dean  and  
74:2426:185990:16:0:72:144:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:Ortoleva 2018). 
75:2427:186008:0:20:72:545:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:puzzling  behavior  of  playing  lottery  1  but  not  lottery  2.  Although  one  wonders  whether  these  
75:2428:186116:20:21:72:543:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:14% misunderstood the questions, we find only a bit of evidence in support of that interpretation: 
75:2429:186215:21:21:72:544:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:those playing the single but not compound lottery have slightly lower cognitive skills than other 
75:2430:186313:21:21:72:544:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:loss  averters,  conditional  on  our  rich  set  of  covariates, but  actually  have  higher  cognitive  skills  
75:2431:186427:21:20:72:547:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:than   the   most-classical   group.   And   playing   the   single   but   not   the   compound   lottery   is   
75:2432:186541:20:21:72:543:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:uncorrelated with our measure of ambiguity aversion, pushing against the interpretation that the 
75:2433:186638:21:21:72:542:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:compound  lottery  is  sufficiently  complicated  as  to  appear  effectively  ambiguous  (Dean  and 
75:2434:186739:21:28:72:151:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:Ortoleva 2018). 
75:2435:186755:28:21:90:542:535:144:Times:144:Times:A:All  told  64%  of  our  subjects  indicate  some  loss  aversion,  defined  as  rejecting  one  or  both 
75:2436:186861:21:21:72:542:514:144:Times:144:Times:B:small-stakes lotteries, as do 67% in FG. In Abeler et al.’s (2011) student sample, 87% reject one 
75:2437:186959:21:20:72:546:493:144:Times:144:Times:B:or  more  of  the  four  small-stakes  lotteries  with  positive  expected  value.  The  Abeler  et  al.  
75:2438:187065:20:21:72:543:473:144:Times:144:Times:B:questions were also fielded in an ALP module from early 2013 used by Hwang (2016); 70% of 
75:2439:187155:21:21:72:545:452:144:Times:144:Times:B:that  sample  exhibits some  loss  aversion.  In  von  Gaudecker  et  al.’s nationally  representative  
75:2440:187259:21:21:72:543:431:144:Times:144:Times:B:Dutch  sample,  86%  exhibit  some  loss  aversion,  as  inferred  from  structural  estimation  based  on 
75:2441:187365:21:20:72:543:410:144:Times:144:Times:B:data from multiple price lists. We also order sets of deviations to indicate greater degrees of loss 
75:2442:187466:20:21:72:545:390:144:Times:144:Times:B:aversion,  based  on  whether  the  individual  respondent  rejects  the  compound  but  not  the  single  
75:2443:187573:21:29:72:416:369:144:Times:144:Times:B:lottery, rejects the single but not the compound lottery, or rejects both.  
75:2444:187649:29:20:90:543:340:144:Times:144:Times:A:Despite  the  massive  amount  of  work  on  loss  aversion,  research  exploring links  between 
75:2445:187745:20:21:72:544:320:144:Times:144:Times:B:directly elicited measures of loss aversion and field  behavior is  only  beginning. von  Gaudecker  
75:2446:187846:21:21:72:546:299:144:Times:144:Times:B:et  al.  (2011) do  not  explore  links  between  loss  aversion  and  field  behavior. Dimmock  and  
75:2447:187948:21:20:72:543:278:144:Times:144:Times:B:Kouwenberg  (2010) do,  like  von  Gaudecker  et  al.  using  CentERdata,  but  lack  many  important 
75:2448:188050:20:21:72:546:258:144:Times:144:Times:B:covariates. Fehr  and  Goette  (2007) find  that  loss  aversion  moderates  the  effect  of  a  wage  
75:2449:188153:21:21:72:545:237:144:Times:144:Times:B:increase,  but  their  sample  includes  only  bike  messengers  and  lacks  measures  of  many  other  
75:2450:188257:21:20:72:544:216:144:Times:144:Times:B:potentially moderating factors. Abeler et al. (2011) find that loss aversion is strongly correlated 
75:2451:188357:20:21:72:545:196:144:Times:144:Times:B:with  effort  choices  in  the  lab  among  their  student  sample,  but  again  they  lack  data  on  many  
75:2452:188466:21:21:72:543:175:144:Times:144:Times:B:covariates of interest. Hwang (2016) uses the Abeler et al. measures to infer a strong correlation 
75:2453:188565:21:21:72:546:154:144:Times:144:Times:B:between  loss  aversion  and  insurance  holdings  in an  earlier  ALP  module,  but  lacks  many  
75:2454:188664:21:0:72:544:133:144:Times:144:Times:B:important  covariates  and  the  only  other  behavioral  factor  considered  is  an  interaction  between  
76:2455:188774:0:20:72:546:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:loss  aversion  and  a  measure  of  the  Gambler’s  Fallacy  (labeled  “Heuristics”  in  the  Hwang  
76:2456:188876:20:29:72:116:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:paper).17 
76:2457:188885:29:29:72:294:659:144:Times:144:Times:B:F. Narrow bracketing and dominated choice 
76:2458:188926:29:21:90:544:630:144:Times:144:Times:A:Narrow  bracketing  refers  to  the  tendency  to  make  decisions  in  (relative)  isolation,  without  
76:2459:189031:21:21:72:545:609:144:Times:144:Times:B:full  consideration  of  other  choices  and  constraints.  Rabin  and  Weizsacker  (2009) show  that  
76:2460:189134:21:20:72:543:588:144:Times:144:Times:B:narrow  bracketing  can  lead  to  dominated  choices—and  hence  expensive  and  wealth-reducing 
76:2461:189232:20:29:72:258:568:144:Times:144:Times:B:ones—given non-CARA preferences.  
76:2462:189265:29:21:90:545:539:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  measure  narrow  bracketing  and  dominated  choice  (NBDC)  using  two  of  the  tasks  in  
76:2463:189362:21:20:72:546:518:144:Times:144:Times:B:Rabin  and  Weizacker  (2009).  Each  task  instructs  the  subject  to  make  two  decisions.  Each  
76:2464:189464:20:21:72:543:498:144:Times:144:Times:B:decision presents the subject with a choice between a certain payoff and a gamble. Each decision 
76:2465:189561:21:21:72:544:477:144:Times:144:Times:B:pair  appears  on  the  same  screen,  with  an  instruction  to  consider  the  two  decisions  jointly.  RW  
76:2466:189672:21:20:72:545:456:144:Times:144:Times:B:administer  their  tasks  with  students  and,  like  us,  in  a  nationally  representative  online  panel  
76:2467:189781:20:29:72:522:436:144:Times:144:Times:B:(Knowledge Networks in their case). Like us, payoffs are hypothetical for their online panel.  
76:2468:189876:29:21:90:543:407:144:Times:144:Times:A:Our first task follows RW’s Example 2, with Decision 1 between winning $100 vs. a 50-50 
76:2469:189964:21:21:72:543:386:144:Times:144:Times:B:chance of losing $300 or winning $700, and Decision 2 between losing $400 vs. a 50-50 chance 
76:2470:190057:21:21:72:543:365:144:Times:144:Times:B:of losing $900 or winning $100.18 As RW show, someone who is loss averse and risk-seeking in 
76:2471:190150:21:20:71:544:344:144:Times:144:Times:B:losses  will,  in  isolation  (narrow  bracketing)  tend  to  choose  A  over  B,  and  D  over  C.  But  the  
76:2472:190261:20:21:71:540:324:144:Times:144:Times:B:combination  AD  is  dominated  with  an  expected  loss  of  $50  relative  to  BC.  Hence  a  broad-
76:2473:190363:21:21:71:542:303:144:Times:144:Times:B:bracketer will never choose AD.  29% of our subjects choose AD, compared to 53% in the most 
76:2474:190455:21:28:71:210:282:144:Times:144:Times:B:similar presentation in RW.  
76:2475:190483:28:21:89:543:254:144:Times:144:Times:A:Our second task reproduces RW’s Example 4, with Decision 1 between winning $850 vs. a 
76:2476:190569:21:21:71:540:233:144:Times:144:Times:B:50-50 chance of winning $100 or winning $1,600, and Decision 2 between losing $650 vs. a 50-
76:2477:190661:21:20:71:543:212:144:Times:144:Times:B:50  chance  of  losing  $1,550  or  winning  $100.  As  in  task  one,  a  decision  maker  who  rejects  the  
76:2478:190772:20:21:71:542:192:144:Times:144:Times:B:risk in the first decision but accepts it in the second decision (A and D) violates dominance, here 
76:2479:190872:21:42:71:543:171:144:Times:144:Times:B:with an expected loss of $75 relative to BC. 23% of our subjects choose AD, compared to 36% 
76:2480:190964:42:17:72:526:129:42:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:17 Hwang (2016) also discusses the potential (mediating) role of narrow framing/bracketing but lacks a 
76:2481:191067:17:9:71:220:112:121:Times:121:Times:F:directly elicited measure of such. 
76:2482:191101:9:16:72:542:103:42:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:18  Given  the  puzzling  result  that  RW’s  Example  2  was  relatively  impervious  to  a  broad-bracketing 
76:2483:191212:16:13:72:542:87:121:Times:121:Times:F:treatment,  we  changed  our  version  slightly  to  avoid  zero-amount  payoffs.  Thanks  to  Georg  Weizsacker 
76:2484:191325:13:0:71:158:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:for this suggestion. 
77:2485:191347:0:20:72:543:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:in  the  most  similar  presentation  in  RW.  As  RW  discuss,  a new  feature  of  task  two  is  that  AD  
77:2486:191457:20:21:72:540:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:sacrifices expected value in the second decision, not in the first. This implies that for all broad-
77:2487:191557:21:21:71:544:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:bracketing  risk  averters  AC  is  optimal:  it  generates  the  highest  available  expected  value  at  no  
77:2488:191668:21:21:71:542:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:variance. 50% of our subjects choose AC, compared to only 33% in the most similar presentation 
77:2489:191763:21:28:71:524:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:in RW. I.e., 50% of our subjects do NOT broad-bracket in this task, compared to 67% in RW. 
77:2490:191854:28:21:89:545:597:144:Times:144:Times:A:Reassuringly,  responses  across  our  two  tasks  are  correlated;  this  is  especially  reassuring  
77:2491:191957:21:21:71:545:576:144:Times:144:Times:B:given  that  the  two  tasks  appear  non-consecutively  in  the  survey,  hopefully  dampening  any  
77:2492:192059:21:20:71:543:555:144:Times:144:Times:B:tendency for a mechanical correlation. E.g., the unconditional correlation between choosing AD 
77:2493:192154:20:29:71:208:535:144:Times:144:Times:B:across the two tasks is 0.34. 
77:2494:192183:29:21:89:545:506:144:Times:144:Times:A:1,486 subjects  complete  both  tasks  (out  of  the  1,515 who  respond  to  at  least  one  of  our  
77:2495:192286:21:20:71:543:485:144:Times:144:Times:B:questions in module 315). Putting the two tasks together to create summary indicators of narrow 
77:2496:192381:20:21:71:543:465:144:Times:144:Times:B:bracketing,  we  find 59%  of  our  subjects  exhibiting some  narrow  bracketing  in  the  sense  of  not  
77:2497:192489:21:21:71:539:444:144:Times:144:Times:B:broad-bracketing  on  both  tasks,  while  13%  narrow-bracket  on  both  tasks. These  are  uni-
77:2498:192586:21:20:71:543:423:144:Times:144:Times:B:directional indicators: we either classify someone as narrow-bracketing, or not. RW do not create 
77:2499:192684:20:21:71:543:403:144:Times:144:Times:B:summary  indicators  across  tasks,  but,  as  noted  above,  their  subjects  exhibit  substantially  more  
77:2500:192793:21:29:71:342:382:144:Times:144:Times:B:narrow bracketing at the task level than our subjects do. 
77:2501:192851:29:21:89:544:353:144:Times:144:Times:A:Research  linking  directly-elicited  measures  of  NBDC  to  field outcomes  is  just  beginning.  
77:2502:192951:21:20:71:544:332:144:Times:144:Times:B:The  only  paper  we  know  of  in  this  vein,  Gottlieb  and  Mitchell  (2015), uses  a  different  method  
77:2503:193061:20:21:71:543:312:144:Times:144:Times:B:for  measuring  narrow  bracketing—one  that  does  not  allow  for  dominated  choice—the  Tversky  
77:2504:193162:21:21:71:545:291:144:Times:144:Times:B:and  Kahneman (1981) “sensitivity  to  framing”  questions  regarding  the  policy  response  to  an  
77:2505:193264:21:20:71:543:270:144:Times:144:Times:B:epidemic.  30%  of  subjects  in  the  Health  and  Retirement  Study  choose  different  policy  options  
77:2506:193371:20:21:71:546:250:144:Times:144:Times:B:under  the  two  different  frames,  an  indicator  of  framing  sensitivity,  and  this  indicator  is  
77:2507:193476:21:21:71:543:229:144:Times:144:Times:B:negatively  correlated  with  the  holding  of  long-term  care  insurance,  conditional  on  a  rich  set  of  
77:2508:193588:21:29:71:281:208:144:Times:144:Times:B:covariates include a measure loss aversion. 
77:2509:193631:29:28:71:186:179:144:Times:144:Times:B:G. Ambiguity aversion 
77:2510:193651:28:21:89:549:151:144:Times:144:Times:A:Ambiguity   aversion   refers   to   a   preference   for   known   uncertainty   over   unknown   
77:2511:193750:21:21:71:549:130:144:Times:144:Times:B:uncertainty—preferring,   for   example,   a   less-than-50/50   gamble   to   one   with   unknown   
77:2512:193852:21:20:71:539:109:144:Times:144:Times:B:probabilities.  It  has  been  widely  theorized  that  ambiguity  aversion  can  explain  various  sub-
77:2513:193956:20:0:71:546:89:144:Times:144:Times:B:optimal  portfolio  choices,  and  Dimmock  et  al.  (2016) find  that  it  is  indeed  conditionally  
78:2514:194061:0:20:72:545:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:correlated  with  lower  stockholdings  and  worse  diversification  in  their  ALP  sample  (see  also  
78:2515:194166:20:29:72:297:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:Dimmock, Kouwenberg, and Wakker (2016)). 
78:2516:194207:29:21:90:544:659:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  elicit  a  coarse  measure  of  ambiguity  aversion  using  just  one  or  two  questions  about  a  
78:2517:194312:21:21:72:543:638:144:Times:144:Times:B:game that pays $500 if you select a green ball. The first question offers the choice between a Bag 
78:2518:194411:21:20:72:543:617:144:Times:144:Times:B:One with 45 green and 55 yellow balls vs. a Bag Two of unknown composition. 1,397 subjects 
78:2519:194502:20:21:72:544:597:144:Times:144:Times:B:respond to this question (out of 1,427 who answer at least one of our questions on ALP module 
78:2520:194596:21:21:72:545:576:144:Times:144:Times:B:352). 73%  choose  the  45-55  bag,  and  we  label  them  ambiguity  averse.  The  survey  then  asks  
78:2521:194700:21:20:71:542:555:144:Times:144:Times:B:these subjects how many green balls would need to be in Bag One to induce them to switch. We 
78:2522:194793:20:21:71:542:535:144:Times:144:Times:B:subtract this amount from 50, dropping the 99 subjects whose response to the second question is 
78:2523:194889:21:21:71:544:514:144:Times:144:Times:B:>45 (and  the  10  subjects  who  do  not  respond),  to  obtain  a  continuous  measure  of  ambiguity  
78:2524:194994:21:20:71:544:493:144:Times:144:Times:B:aversion  that  ranges  from  0  (not  averse  in  the  first  question)  to  50  (most  averse===  the  three  
78:2525:195106:20:21:71:543:473:144:Times:144:Times:B:subjects  who  respond  “zero”  to  the  second  question).  The  continuous  measure  (N=1,288)  has  a  
78:2526:195212:21:21:72:543:452:144:Times:144:Times:B:mean of 14 (median=10), and a SD of 13. If we impose a large-deviation threshold of 10 (20% of 
78:2527:195307:21:21:72:544:431:144:Times:144:Times:B:the  max)  for  labeling  someone  as  ambiguity  averse,  50%  of  our  sample  exceeds  this  threshold  
78:2528:195414:21:20:72:540:410:144:Times:144:Times:B:and  another  16%  are  at  the  threshold.  Our  elicitation  does  not  distinguish  between  ambiguity-
78:2529:195520:20:21:72:544:390:144:Times:144:Times:B:neutral  and  ambiguity-seeking choices (for more comprehensive but still tractable methods see, 
78:2530:195617:21:21:72:543:369:144:Times:144:Times:B:e.g., Dimmock, Kouwenberg et al. (2016), Dimmock, Kouwenberg, and Wakker (2016), Gneezy 
78:2531:195705:21:28:71:488:348:144:Times:144:Times:B:et al. (2015)), and so our measure of deviation from ambiguity-neutrality is one-sided. 
78:2532:195793:28:21:90:543:320:144:Times:144:Times:A:Despite the coarseness of our elicitation, comparisons to other work suggest that it produces 
78:2533:195887:21:21:72:543:299:144:Times:144:Times:B:reliable data. Our ambiguity aversion indicator correlates with one constructed from Dimmock et 
78:2534:195983:21:20:72:542:278:144:Times:144:Times:B:al.’s  elicitation  in  the  ALP  (0.14,  p-value  0.0001,  N=789),  despite  the  elicitations  taking  place 
78:2535:196094:20:21:72:544:258:144:Times:144:Times:B:roughly  3  years  apart.  Prevalence  at  our  10pp  large-deviation  cutoff  nearly matches that  from  
78:2536:196200:21:21:72:543:237:144:Times:144:Times:B:Dimmock, Kouwenberg et al.’s (2016) ALP sample and Butler et al.’s (2014) Unicredit Clients’ 
78:2537:196293:21:20:72:545:216:144:Times:144:Times:B:Survey  sample  from  Italy,  and  our prevalence  of  any  ambiguity  aversion,  0.73  is  similar  to  
78:2538:196398:20:29:72:502:196:144:Times:144:Times:B:Dimmock, Kouwenberg, and Wakker’s (2016) 0.68 from the Dutch version of the ALP .  
78:2539:196481:29:21:90:542:167:144:Times:144:Times:A:Our examination of links to field behaviors builds on the papers by Dimmock and co-authors 
78:2540:196572:21:0:72:544:146:144:Times:144:Times:B:cited  above,  which  estimate conditional correlations  between  ambiguity  aversion  and  financial  
79:2541:196677:0:20:72:540:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:market behavior. We broaden the set of both outcomes and control variables (especially other B-
79:2542:196772:20:29:71:122:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:factors).19 
79:2543:196783:29:29:72:248:659:144:Times:144:Times:B:H. Overconfidence: Three varieties 
79:2544:196816:29:21:90:540:630:144:Times:144:Times:A:Overconfidence has been implicated in excessive trading (Daniel and Hirshleifer 2015), over-
79:2545:196908:21:21:72:543:609:144:Times:144:Times:B:borrowing on credit cards (Ausubel 1991), paying a premium for private equity (Moskowitz and 
79:2546:197001:21:20:72:543:588:144:Times:144:Times:B:Vissing-Jorgensen 2002; although see Kartashova 2014), and poor contract choice (Grubb 2015), 
79:2547:197095:20:29:72:340:568:144:Times:144:Times:B:any of which can reduce wealth and financial security.  
79:2548:197151:29:29:90:543:539:144:Times:144:Times:A:We elicit three distinct measures of overconfidence, following e.g., Moore and Healy (2008).  
79:2549:197245:29:20:90:545:510:144:Times:144:Times:A:The  first  measures  it  in  level/absolute  terms,  by  following  the  three  Banks  and  Oldfield  
79:2550:197348:20:21:71:543:490:144:Times:144:Times:B:numeracy questions, in our second Round 1 module, with the question: “How many of the last 3 
79:2551:197441:21:21:71:544:469:144:Times:144:Times:B:questions  (the  ones  on  the  disease,  the  lottery  and  the  savings  account)  do  you  think  you  got  
79:2552:197552:21:20:71:542:448:144:Times:144:Times:B:correct?” We  then  subtract  the  respondent’s  assessment  from  her  actual  score.  39%  of  1,366 
79:2553:197655:20:21:71:543:428:144:Times:144:Times:B:subjects  are  overconfident  (“overestimation”  per  Moore  and  Healy)  by  this  measure  (with  32%  
79:2554:197760:21:21:71:542:407:144:Times:144:Times:B:overestimating by one question), while only 11% are underconfident (with 10% underestimating 
79:2555:197853:21:21:71:543:386:144:Times:144:Times:B:by one question). Larrick et al. (2007), Moore and Healy, and other studies use this method for 
79:2556:197949:21:20:71:544:365:144:Times:144:Times:B:measuring overestimation,  but  we  are  not  aware  of  any  that  report  individual-level  prevalence  
79:2557:198055:20:21:71:547:345:144:Times:144:Times:B:estimates   (they   instead   focus   on   task-level   data,   sample-level   summary   statistics,   and/or   
79:2558:198167:21:29:71:390:324:144:Times:144:Times:B:correlates of cross-sectional heterogeneity in estimation patterns). 
79:2559:198235:29:20:89:544:295:144:Times:144:Times:A:The  second  measures  overconfidence  in  precision,  as  indicated  by  responding  “100%”  on  
79:2560:198333:20:21:71:543:275:144:Times:144:Times:B:two sets of questions about the likelihoods (of different possible Banks and Oldfield quiz scores 
79:2561:198431:21:21:71:544:254:144:Times:144:Times:B:or  of  future  income  increases).  This  is  a  coarse  adaptation  of  the  usual  approaches  of  eliciting  
79:2562:198544:21:20:71:544:233:144:Times:144:Times:B:several  confidence  intervals  or  subjective  probability  distributions  (Moore  and  Healy).  In  our  
79:2563:198651:20:29:71:495:213:144:Times:144:Times:B:data 34% of 1,345 responding to both sets respond 100% on >=1 set, and 10% on both.  
79:2564:198736:29:21:89:543:184:144:Times:144:Times:A:The  third  measures  confidence  in  placement  (relative  performance),  using  a  self-ranking 
79:2565:198834:21:21:71:543:163:144:Times:144:Times:B:elicited before taking our number series test: “We would like to know what you think about your 
79:2566:198930:21:20:71:544:142:144:Times:144:Times:B:intelligence  as  it  would  be  measured  by  a  standard  test.  How  do  you  think  your  performance  
79:2567:199037:20:32:71:540:122:144:Times:144:Times:B:would rank, relative to all of the other ALP members who have taken the test?” We find a better-
79:2568:199133:32:16:72:531:90:42:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:19 The other paper we know of examining correlations between ambiguity attitudes and field behavior is 
79:2569:199236:16:0:72:306:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:Sutter et al.’s (2013) study of adolescents in Austria. 
80:2570:199293:0:20:72:544:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:than-average  effect in  the  sample  as  a  whole  (70%  report  a  percentile>median)  that  disappears  
80:2571:199400:20:21:72:543:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:when we ask the same question immediately post-test, still not having revealed any scores (50% 
80:2572:199495:21:21:71:545:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:report  a  percentile>median).  We also  construct  an  individual-level  measure  of  confidence  in  
80:2573:199598:21:21:72:542:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:placement  by  subtracting  the  subject’s  actual  ranking  from  his  pre-test  self-ranking  (N=1,395). 
80:2574:199705:21:20:72:547:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:This  measure  is  useful  for  capturing  individual-level  heterogeneity  ordinally,  but  not  for  
80:2575:199808:20:21:71:544:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:measuring  prevalence  because  the  actual  ranking  is  based  on  a  15-question  test  and  hence  its  
80:2576:199916:21:29:72:317:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:percentiles are much coarser than the self-ranking. 
80:2577:199968:29:20:90:544:555:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  are  not  aware  of  any  prior  work  exploring  conditional  correlations  between  the  sorts  of  
80:2578:200074:20:29:72:375:535:144:Times:144:Times:B:overconfidence measures described above and field outcomes. 
80:2579:200134:29:29:72:287:506:144:Times:144:Times:B:I. Non-belief in the Law of Large Numbers 
80:2580:200175:29:20:90:546:477:144:Times:144:Times:A:Under-weighting  the  importance  of  the  Law  of  Large  Numbers  (LLN)  can  affect  how  
80:2581:200268:20:21:72:545:457:144:Times:144:Times:B:individuals  treat  risk  (as  in  the  stock  market),  or  how  much  data  they  demand  before  making  
80:2582:200376:21:21:72:542:436:144:Times:144:Times:B:decisions. In this sense non-belief in LLN (a.k.a. NBLLN) can act as an “enabling bias” for other 
80:2583:200474:21:29:72:385:415:144:Times:144:Times:B:biases like loss aversion (Benjamin, Rabin, and Raymond 2016). 
80:2584:200537:29:20:90:543:386:144:Times:144:Times:A:Following Benjamin, Moore, and Rabin (see also D Kahneman and Tversky 1972; Benjamin, 
80:2585:200623:20:21:72:545:366:144:Times:144:Times:B:Rabin,  and  Raymond  2016),  we  measure  non-belief  in  law  of  large  numbers  (NBLLN)  using  
80:2586:200723:21:29:71:250:345:144:Times:144:Times:B:responses to the following question:  
80:2587:200761:29:20:89:526:316:144:Times:144:Times:A:...  say  the  computer  flips  the  coin  1000  times,  and  counts  the  total  number  of  heads.  
80:2588:200863:20:21:89:525:296:144:Times:144:Times:A:Please tell us what you think are the chances, in percentage terms, that the total number 
80:2589:200953:21:21:90:469:275:144:Times:144:Times:A:of heads will lie within the following ranges. Your answers should sum to 100. 
80:2590:201032:21:20:90:93:254:144:Times:144:Times:A: 
80:2591:201032:20:21:72:543:234:144:Times:144:Times:B:The ranges provided are [0, 480], [481, 519], and [520, 1000], and so the correct answers are 11, 
80:2592:201130:21:29:72:111:213:144:Times:144:Times:B:78, 11.  
80:2593:201139:29:20:90:543:184:144:Times:144:Times:A:1,375 subjects respond (out of the 1,427 who answer at least one of our questions in Module 
80:2594:201231:20:22:72:544:164:144:Times:144:Times:B:352),20  with  mean  (SD)  responses  of  27  (18),  42 (24),  and  31  (20).  We  measure  NBLLN  using  
80:2595:201337:22:20:72:543:142:144:Times:144:Times:B:the distance between the subject’s answer for the [481, 519] range and 78. Only one subject gets 
80:2596:201434:20:32:72:544:122:144:Times:144:Times:B:it  exactly  right.  87%  underestimate;  coupled  with  prior  work,  this  result leads  us  to  designate  
80:2597:201544:32:16:72:543:90:42:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:20  Only  26  subjects  provide  responses  that  do  not  sum  to  100  after  a  prompt,  and  each  response  for  an  
80:2598:201666:16:0:72:452:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:individual range is [0, 100], so we do not exclude any subjects from the analysis here. 
81:2599:201755:0:20:72:542:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:underestimation  as  the  “standard”  directional  bias.  The  modal  underestimator  responds  with  50 
81:2600:201860:20:21:71:542:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:(18% of the sample). The other most-frequent responses are 25 (10%), 30 (9%), 33 (8%), and 40 
81:2601:201954:21:21:72:544:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:(7%).  Few  underestimators—only  4%  of  the  sample—are  within  10pp  of  78,  and  their  mean  
81:2602:202054:21:21:72:543:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:distance  is  43,  with  an  SD  of  17. 9%  of  the  sample  underestimates  by  20pp  or  less.  13% 
81:2603:202157:21:20:71:542:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:overestimate relative to 78, with 5% of the sample quite close to correct at 80, and another 5% at 
81:2604:202256:20:21:71:547:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:100.   Benjamin,   Moore,   and   Rabin   (2017) do   not   calculate   individual-level   measures   of   
81:2605:202363:21:21:71:544:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:underestimation  or  overestimation  in  their  convenience  sample,  but  do  report  that  the  sample  
81:2606:202469:21:20:71:542:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:means are 35%, 36%, and 29% for the three bins. The comparable figures in our data are 27%, 
81:2607:202561:20:29:71:147:543:144:Times:144:Times:B:42%, and 31%. 
81:2608:202575:29:21:89:540:514:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  are  not  aware  of  any  prior  work  exploring  conditional  correlations  between  directly-
81:2609:202674:21:28:71:253:493:144:Times:144:Times:B:elicited NBLLN and field outcomes.  
81:2610:202710:28:29:71:191:465:144:Times:144:Times:B:J. Gambler’s Fallacies 
81:2611:202731:29:21:89:545:436:144:Times:144:Times:A:The Gambler’s Fallacies  involve  falsely  attributing  statistical  dependence to  statistically  
81:2612:202830:21:21:72:544:415:144:Times:144:Times:B:independent events,  in  either  expecting  one  outcome  to  be  less  likely  because  it  has  happened  
81:2613:202938:21:20:72:545:394:144:Times:144:Times:B:recently  (recent  reds  on  roulette  make  black  more  likely  in  the  future)  or  the  reverse,  a  “hot  
81:2614:203050:20:21:72:547:374:144:Times:144:Times:B:hand”  view  that  recent  events  are  likely  to  be  repeated.  Gambler’s  fallacies  can  lead  to  
81:2615:203154:21:21:72:545:353:144:Times:144:Times:B:overvaluation  of  financial  expertise  (or  attending  to  misguided  financial  advice),  and  related  
81:2616:203261:21:20:72:543:332:144:Times:144:Times:B:portfolio  choices  like  the  active-fund  puzzle,  that  can  erode  wealth  (Rabin  and  Vayanos  2010). 
81:2617:203369:20:21:71:545:312:144:Times:144:Times:B:Because  the  hot  hand  fallacy  is  more  closely  linked  to  harmful  financial  behaviors  such  as  
81:2618:203475:21:21:71:546:291:144:Times:144:Times:B:“return-chasing”  or  over-valuing  the  talent  of  stock-pickers  (Rabin  and  Vayanos  2010),  for  
81:2619:203578:21:20:71:545:270:144:Times:144:Times:B:analyses  linking  the  fallacies  to  field  behavior  we  denote  hot-hand as  the  “standard”  bias  and  
81:2620:203687:20:29:71:215:250:144:Times:144:Times:B:cold-hand as “non-standard.” 
81:2621:203716:29:29:89:497:221:144:Times:144:Times:A:We take a slice of Benjamin, Moore, and Rabin’s (2017) elicitation for the fallacies: 
81:2622:203801:29:20:89:524:192:144:Times:144:Times:A:"Imagine that we had a computer “flip” a fair coin... 10 times. The first 9 are all heads. 
81:2623:203892:20:29:89:464:172:144:Times:144:Times:A:What are the chances, in percentage terms, that the 10th flip will be a head?" 
81:2624:203971:29:21:71:542:143:144:Times:144:Times:B:1,392 subjects  respond,  out  of  the  1,427 respondents  to  module  352.  The cold-hand  fallacy 
81:2625:204071:21:21:72:544:122:144:Times:144:Times:B:implies  a  response  <  50%,  while  the  hot-hand fallacy  implies  a  response  >  50%.  Our  mean  
81:2626:204174:21:20:72:544:101:144:Times:144:Times:B:response  is  45%  (SD=25),  which  is  consistent  with  the  cold-hand  but substantially  above  the  
81:2627:204279:20:0:72:540:81:144:Times:144:Times:B:32% in Benjamin, Moore, and Rabin. Another indication that we find less evidence of the cold-
82:2628:204374:0:20:72:539:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:hand  fallacy  is  that,  while  they  infer  that  “at  the  individual  level,  the  gambler’s  fallacy  [cold-
82:2629:204487:20:21:72:546:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:hand] appears  to  be  the  predominant  pattern  of  belief”  (2013,  p.  16),  we  find  only  26%  
82:2630:204589:21:21:72:543:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:answering  <  “50.” 14%  of  our  sample  responds  with  >”50”  (over  half  of  these  responses are at 
82:2631:204695:21:21:72:543:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:“90”  or  “100”).  So  60%  of  our  sample  answers  correctly.  Nearly  everyone  who  responds  with  
82:2632:204800:21:20:72:542:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:something other than “50” errs by a substantial amount—e.g., only 2 % of the sample is [30, 50) 
82:2633:204896:20:21:72:543:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:or  (50,  70].  Sixteen  percent  of  our  sample  answers  “10,”21  which  Benjamin,  Moore,  and  Rabin  
82:2634:205003:21:21:71:543:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:speculates is an indicator of miscomprehension; we find that while subjects with this indicator do 
82:2635:205102:21:21:71:545:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:have  significantly  lower  cognitive  skills  than  the  unbiased  group,  they  actually  have  higher  
82:2636:205208:21:28:71:413:542:144:Times:144:Times:B:cognitive skills than the rest of subjects exhibiting a gambler’s fallacy. 
82:2637:205283:28:21:89:546:514:144:Times:144:Times:A:Dohmen et  al.  (2009) measure the  fallacies  using  a  similar  elicitation  that  confronts  a  
82:2638:205382:21:29:71:484:493:144:Times:144:Times:B:representative sample of 1,012 Germans, taking an in-person household survey, with: 
82:2639:205466:29:20:89:539:464:144:Times:144:Times:A:Imagine you are tossing a fair coin. After eight tosses you observe the following result: tails-
82:2640:205562:20:21:71:543:444:144:Times:144:Times:B:tails-tails-heads-tails-heads-heads-heads. What is the probability, in percent, that the next toss is 
82:2641:205664:21:29:71:115:423:144:Times:144:Times:B:“tails”? 
82:2642:205672:29:21:89:543:394:144:Times:144:Times:A:986  of  Dohmen  et  al.’s  respondents  provide  some  answer  to  this  question,  95  of  whom  say 
82:2643:205775:21:20:71:543:373:144:Times:144:Times:B:“Don’t  know.” Among  the  remaining  891,  23%  exhibit  cold-hand (compared  to  26%  in  our 
82:2644:205871:20:21:71:543:353:144:Times:144:Times:B:sample), and 10% exhibit hot-hand (compared to 14% in our sample). Conditional on exhibiting 
82:2645:205964:21:21:71:540:332:144:Times:144:Times:B:cold-hand, on average subjects err by 29pp (40 pp in our sample). Conditional on exhibiting hot-
82:2646:206060:21:28:71:355:311:144:Times:144:Times:B:hand, the mean subject error is 27pp (39pp in our sample). 
82:2647:206118:28:21:89:542:283:144:Times:144:Times:A:Dohmen et al. also explore correlations between unemployment or bank overdrafts and their 
82:2648:206208:21:21:72:544:262:144:Times:144:Times:B:directly-elicited  fallacy  measures,  conditional  on  age,  gender,  education,  income,  and  wealth.  
82:2649:206314:21:20:72:544:241:144:Times:144:Times:B:They  find  evidence  of  positive  correlations  between  hot-hand and  unemployment  and  between  
82:2650:206415:20:29:72:209:221:144:Times:144:Times:B:cold-hand and overdrafting. 
82:2651:206443:29:29:72:283:192:144:Times:144:Times:B:K. Exponential growth bias: Two varieties 
82:2652:206484:29:21:90:547:163:144:Times:144:Times:A:Exponential  growth  bias  (EGB)  produces  a  tendency  to  underestimate  the  effects  of  
82:2653:206578:21:20:72:546:142:144:Times:144:Times:B:compounding  on  costs  of  debt  and  benefits  of  saving.  It  has  been  linked  to  a  broad  set  of  
82:2654:206686:20:32:72:414:122:144:Times:144:Times:B:financial outcomes (Levy and Tasoff 2016; Stango and Zinman 2009). 
82:2655:206753:32:16:72:544:90:42:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:21  34%  of  the  sample  in  Benjamin,  Moore,  and  Raymond  respond  “10%”  on  one  or  more  of  their  ten  
82:2656:206867:16:0:72:119:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:questions. 
83:2657:206879:0:20:90:546:708:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  measure  EGB,  following  previous  papers,  by  asking  respondents  to  solve  questions  
83:2658:206975:20:21:72:543:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:regarding an asset’s future value or a loan’s implied annual percentage rate. Our first measure of 
83:2659:207074:21:21:72:545:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:EGB  follows  in  the  spirit  of  Stango  and  Zinman  (2009,  2011)  by  first  eliciting  the  monthly  
83:2660:207181:21:21:72:544:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:payment the respondent would expect to pay on a $10,000, 48 month car loan. The survey then 
83:2661:207273:21:20:72:543:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:asks “... What percent rate of interest does that imply in annual percentage rate ("APR") terms?” 
83:2662:207371:20:21:72:546:605:144:Times:144:Times:B:1,445 panelists  answer  both  questions,  out  of  the  1,515 respondents  to  Module  315.  Most  
83:2663:207471:21:21:72:544:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:responses  appear  sensible given  market  rates;  e.g.,  there  are  mass  points at  5%,  10%,  3%,  6%  
83:2664:207578:21:28:71:113:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:and 4%. 
83:2665:207586:28:21:89:544:535:144:Times:144:Times:A:We calculate an  individual-level  measure  of  “debt-side  EGB”  by  comparing  the  difference  
83:2666:207684:21:21:71:542:514:144:Times:144:Times:B:between the APR implied by the monthly payment supplied by that individual, and the perceived 
83:2667:207777:21:20:71:539:493:144:Times:144:Times:B:APR  as  supplied  directly  by  the  same  individual.  We  start  by  binning  individuals  into  under-
83:2668:207883:20:21:71:543:473:144:Times:144:Times:B:estimators  (the  standard  bias),  over-estimators,  unbiased,  and  unknown  (37%  of  the  sample).22 
83:2669:207987:21:21:72:545:452:144:Times:144:Times:B:The  median  level  difference  between  the  correct  and  stated  value  is  500bp,  with  a  mean  of  
83:2670:208093:21:21:72:542:431:144:Times:144:Times:B:1,042bp and SD of 1,879bp. Among those with known bias, we count as biased 70%, 64%, 47%, 
83:2671:208183:21:21:72:544:410:144:Times:144:Times:B:and  34%  under  error  tolerance  of  zero,  100bp,  500bp,  and  1000bp.  Under  these  tolerances  we  
83:2672:208289:21:20:72:543:389:144:Times:144:Times:B:count 3%, 13%, 41%, and 61% as unbiased, and 27%, 22%, 10%, and 3% as negatively biased. 
83:2673:208378:20:21:72:542:369:144:Times:144:Times:B:This is less EGB than Stango and Zinman (2009, 2011) see from questions in the 1983 Survey of 
83:2674:208472:21:21:72:543:348:144:Times:144:Times:B:Consumer Finances, where 98% of the sample underestimates, and the mean bias is 1,800bp or 
83:2675:208563:21:20:72:543:327:144:Times:144:Times:B:3,800bp  depending  on  the  benchmark. The  time  frames  of  the  questions  differ,  which may 
83:2676:208661:20:21:72:543:307:144:Times:144:Times:B:account for the difference (and is why we do not estimate an EGB structural model parameter to 
83:2677:208756:21:29:72:349:286:144:Times:144:Times:B:compare with our prior work or that of Levy and Tasoff). 
83:2678:208812:29:20:90:545:257:144:Times:144:Times:A:Stango  and  Zinman  (2009;  2011)  find  that  more debt-side  EGB  is  strongly  conditionally  
83:2679:208910:20:21:72:544:237:144:Times:144:Times:B:correlated  with  debt  composition, worse  loan  terms,  and  less  savings  and  wealth.  But  those  
83:2680:209014:21:29:72:437:216:144:Times:144:Times:B:papers lack direct controls for cognitive skills and other behavioral factors.  
83:2681:209094:29:21:90:543:187:144:Times:144:Times:A:Our  second  measure  of  EGB  comes  from  a  question popularized  by  Banks  and  Oldfield 
83:2682:209187:21:42:72:544:166:144:Times:144:Times:B:(2007) as  part  of  a  series  designed  to  measure  basic  numeracy:  “Let's  say  you  have  $200  in  a  
83:2683:209297:42:17:72:543:124:49:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:22 Non-response is relatively small, as only 4% of the sample does not respond to both questions. Most of 
83:2684:209403:17:12:71:542:107:121:Times:121:Times:F:those we label as unknown-bias give responses that imply or state a 0% APR. 7% state payment amounts 
83:2685:209504:12:13:71:542:95:121:Times:121:Times:F:that imply a negative APR, even after being prompted to reconsider their answer. We also classify the 4% 
83:2686:209609:13:0:71:378:82:121:Times:121:Times:F:of respondents with implied APRs >=100% as having unknown bias. 
84:2687:209674:0:20:72:543:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:savings account. The account earns 10 percent interest per year. You don’t withdraw any money 
84:2688:209768:20:21:72:543:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:for two years. How much would you have in the account at the end of two years?” 1,389 subjects 
84:2689:209863:21:21:72:539:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:answer this  question  (out  of  the  1,427 respondents  to  Module  352),  and  we  infer  an  individual-
84:2690:209970:21:21:72:543:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:level measure of “asset-side EGB” by comparing the difference between the correct future value 
84:2691:210065:21:21:72:544:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:($242),  and  the  future  value  supplied  by  the  same  individual.23  We  again  bin  individuals  into  
84:2692:210174:21:20:72:547:604:144:Times:144:Times:B:underestimators  (the  standard  bias),  overestimators,  unbiased,  and  unknown  (14%  of  the  
84:2693:210272:20:21:72:544:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:sample).24  Among  those  with  known  bias  (N=1,222),  the  median  bias  is  $0,  with  a  mean  of  $2  
84:2694:210380:21:21:72:543:563:144:Times:144:Times:B:and SD of $14.25 44% of our sample provides the correct FV. 47% of our sample underestimates 
84:2695:210473:21:21:72:546:542:144:Times:144:Times:B:by  some  amount,  with  most  underestimators  (29% of  the  sample)  providing  the  linearized  
84:2696:210572:21:21:72:543:521:144:Times:144:Times:B:(uncompounded) answer of $240. Nearly all other underestimates provide an answer that fails to 
84:2697:210667:21:20:72:542:500:144:Times:144:Times:B:account for even simple interest; the most common reply in this range is “$220.” Only 9% of our 
84:2698:210763:20:29:72:461:480:144:Times:144:Times:B:sample overestimates the FV, with small mass points at 244, 250, 400, and 440.  
84:2699:210843:29:21:90:543:451:144:Times:144:Times:A:Other  papers  have  used  the  Banks  and  Oldfield  question,  always—to  our  knowledge— 
84:2700:210935:21:21:71:542:430:144:Times:144:Times:B:measuring accuracy as opposed to directional bias and then using a 1/0 measure of correctness as 
84:2701:211032:21:20:71:542:409:144:Times:144:g_d0_f52:B:an input to a financial literacy or numeracy score (e.g., James Banks, O ’Dea, and Oldfield 2010; 
84:2702:211130:20:21:71:543:389:144:Times:144:Times:B:Gustman, Steinmeier, and Tabatabai 2012). Our tabs from the 2014 Health and Retirement Study 
84:2703:211223:21:21:71:546:368:144:Times:144:Times:B:suggest, using  only  the  youngest  HRS  respondents  and our  oldest  respondents  to  maximize  
84:2704:211322:21:20:71:547:347:144:Times:144:Times:B:comparability  (ages  50-60 in  both  samples),   that  there  is  substantially  more  underestimation  in  
84:2705:211431:20:21:71:542:327:144:Times:144:Times:B:the HRS (74%, vs. 48% in our sample). 14% overestimate in the HRS among those aged 50-60, 
84:2706:211521:21:29:71:178:306:144:Times:144:Times:B:vs. 9% in our sample. 
84:2707:211543:29:20:89:543:277:144:Times:144:Times:A:Goda et al. (2017) and Levy and Tasoff (2016) measure asset-side EGB using more difficult 
84:2708:211633:20:21:72:544:257:144:Times:144:Times:B:questions  in  their  representative  samples.  They  find  that  9%  and  11%  overestimate FVs,  while  
84:2709:211739:21:48:72:545:236:144:Times:144:Times:B:69% and  85%  underestimate.  We  do  not  construct  an  EGB  parameter  to  compare  to  theirs,  
84:2710:211839:48:16:72:543:188:42:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:23 Responses to this question are correlated with responses to two other questions, drawn from Levy and 
84:2711:211943:16:13:71:543:172:121:Times:121:Times:F:Tasoff (2016), that can also be used to measure asset-side EGB, but our sample sizes are smaller for those 
84:2712:212050:13:9:71:320:159:121:Times:121:Times:F:two other questions and hence we do not use them here. 
84:2713:212104:9:17:72:543:150:49:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:24 We label as unknown the 8% of the sample answering with future value < present value, the 3% of the 
84:2714:212207:17:13:71:543:133:121:Times:121:Times:F:sample  answering  with  a  future  value  >  2x  the  correct  future  value,  and  the  3%  of  the  sample  who  skip  
84:2715:212329:13:17:71:132:120:121:Times:121:Times:F:this question. 
84:2716:212344:17:16:72:544:103:42:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:25  For  calculating  the  mean  and  SD  we  truncate  bias  at  -42  for  the  4%  sample  answering  with  future  
84:2717:212462:16:13:72:543:87:121:Times:121:Times:F:values 284<FV<485, to create symmetric extrema in the bias distribution since our definition caps bias at 
84:2718:212568:13:0:72:91:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:42.  
85:2719:212574:0:20:72:540:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:because  our  questions  lack  their  richness  and  yield  heavy  mass  points  at  unbiased  and  linear-
85:2720:212681:20:29:71:158:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:biased responses. 
85:2721:212698:29:21:89:543:659:144:Times:144:Times:A:The only prior paper we know looking directly at links between a measure of asset-side EGB 
85:2722:212789:21:21:71:542:638:144:Times:144:Times:B:and field outcomes is Goda et al., who use data on fewer behavioral factors. They find significant 
85:2723:212888:21:28:71:412:617:144:Times:144:Times:B:negative correlations between asset-side EGB and retirement savings.  
85:2724:212958:28:29:71:274:589:144:Times:144:Times:B:L. Limited attention and limited memory 
85:2725:212997:29:21:89:543:560:144:Times:144:Times:A:Prior  empirical  work  has  found  that  limited  attention  affects  a  range  of  financial  decisions 
85:2726:213102:21:20:72:545:539:144:Times:144:Times:B:(e.g.,  Barber  and  Odean  2008;  DellaVigna  and  Pollet  2009;  Karlan  et  al.  2016;  Stango  and  
85:2727:213206:20:21:72:542:519:144:Times:144:Times:B:Zinman  2014).  Behavioral  inattention  is  a  very  active  line  of  theory  inquiry  as  well    (e.g., 
85:2728:213314:21:29:72:494:498:144:Times:144:Times:B:Bordalo, Gennaioli, and Shleifer 2017; Kőszegi and Szeidl 2013; Schwartzstein 2014).  
85:2729:213400:29:20:90:544:469:144:Times:144:Times:A:In  the  absence  of  widely  used  methods  for  measuring  limited  attention  and/or  memory,  we  
85:2730:213502:20:29:72:337:449:144:Times:144:Times:B:create our own, using five simple questions and tasks.  
85:2731:213558:29:21:90:546:420:144:Times:144:Times:A:The  first  three  ask,  “Do  you  believe  that  your  household's  [horizon]  finances...  would  
85:2732:213658:21:20:72:539:399:144:Times:144:Times:B:improve  if  your  household  paid  more  attention  to  them?”   for  three  different  horizons:  “day-to-
85:2733:213766:20:21:72:542:379:144:Times:144:Times:B:day  (dealing  with  routine  expenses,  checking  credit  card  accounts,  bill  payments,  etc.)” 
85:2734:213866:21:21:72:542:358:144:Times:144:Times:B:“medium-run  (dealing  with  periodic  expenses  like  car  repair,  kids’  activities,  vacations,  etc.)” 
85:2735:213974:21:21:72:543:337:144:Times:144:Times:B:and “long-run (dealing with kids' college, retirement planning, allocation of savings/investments, 
85:2736:214073:21:20:72:550:316:144:Times:144:Times:B:etc.)”   Response  options  are  the  same  for  each  of  these  three  questions:  “Yes,  and  I/we  often  
85:2737:214183:20:21:72:545:296:144:Times:144:Times:B:regret  not  paying  greater  attention”  (26%,  23%,  and  35%),  “Yes,  but  paying  more  attention  
85:2738:214287:21:21:72:544:275:144:Times:144:Times:B:would  require  too  much  time/effort”  (8%,  11%,  and  12%),  “No,  my  household  finances  are  set  
85:2739:214393:21:20:72:543:254:144:Times:144:Times:B:up  so  that  they  don't  require  much  attention”  (15%,  16%,  and  13%),  and  “No,  my household is 
85:2740:214499:20:21:72:546:234:144:Times:144:Times:B:already  very  attentive  to  these  matters”  (52%,  51%,  and  41%).  We  designed  the  question  
85:2741:214600:21:21:72:547:213:144:Times:144:Times:B:wording  and  response  options  to  distinguish  behavioral  limited  inattention  (“Yes...  I/we  
85:2742:214700:21:20:72:543:192:144:Times:144:Times:B:often...”)—which also includes a measure of awareness thereof in “regret”—from full attention 
85:2743:214794:20:21:72:543:172:144:Times:144:Times:B:(“...  already  very  attentive”), rational  inattention,  and/or  a  sophisticated  response  to  behavioral  
85:2744:214905:21:21:72:545:151:144:Times:144:Times:B:inattention (“Yes,  but...  too  much  time/effort”;  “...  set  up  so  that  they  don’t  require  much  
85:2745:215012:21:29:72:129:130:144:Times:144:Times:B:attention”). 
85:2746:215024:29:20:90:545:101:144:Times:144:Times:A:Responses  are  strongly  but  not  perfectly  correlated  (ranging  0.56  to  0.69  among  pairwise  
85:2747:215126:20:0:72:544:81:144:Times:144:Times:B:expressions of regret). A fourth measure of limited attention is also strongly correlated with the 
86:2748:215227:0:20:72:545:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:others,  based  on  the  question:  “Do  you  believe  that  you  could  improve  the  prices/terms  your  
86:2749:215334:20:21:72:543:688:144:Times:144:Times:B:household  typically  receives  on  financial  products/services  by  shopping  more?”26  18%  respond  
86:2750:215438:21:21:72:551:667:144:Times:144:Times:B:“Yes, and I/we often regret not shopping more,”    and the likelihood of this response is correlated 
86:2751:215539:21:21:72:543:646:144:Times:144:Times:B:0.25 with each of the regret measures above. 1,483 subjects answer all four questions, out of the 
86:2752:215637:21:21:72:542:625:144:Times:144:Times:B:1,515 respondents to Module 315. Summing the four indicators of attentional regret, we find that 
86:2753:215734:21:20:72:543:604:144:Times:144:Times:B:49% of subjects have one or more (earning a classification of behavioral inattention), 29% have 
86:2754:215830:20:29:71:362:584:144:Times:144:Times:B:two or more, 19% three or more, and only 6% have all four. 
86:2755:215889:29:21:89:543:555:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  also  seek  to  measure  limited  prospective  memory,  following  previous  work  suggesting  
86:2756:215988:21:20:71:542:534:144:Times:144:Times:B:that limited memory entails real costs like forgetting to redeem rebates (e.g., Ericson 2011). We 
86:2757:216085:20:21:71:546:514:144:Times:144:Times:B:offer  an  incentivized  task  to  subjects  taking  module  352:  “The  ALP  will  offer  you  the  
86:2758:216186:21:21:71:543:493:144:Times:144:Times:B:opportunity to earn an extra $10 for one minute of your time. This special survey has just a few 
86:2759:216283:21:20:71:544:472:144:Times:144:Times:B:simple  questions  but  will  only  be  open  for  24  hours, starting  24  hours  from  now.  During  this  
86:2760:216392:20:21:71:542:452:144:Times:144:Times:B:specified time window, you can access the special survey from your ALP account. So we can get 
86:2761:216486:21:21:71:544:431:144:Times:144:Times:B:a  sense  of  what  our  response  rate  might  be,  please  tell  us  now  whether  you  expect  to  do  this  
86:2762:216598:21:21:71:544:410:144:Times:144:Times:B:special  survey.”  97%  say  they  intend  to  complete  the  short  survey,  leaving us with a sample of 
86:2763:216704:21:28:71:330:389:144:Times:144:Times:B:1,358. Only 14% actually complete the short survey.  
86:2764:216757:28:21:89:542:361:144:Times:144:Times:A:Our indicator of behavioral limited memory— (not completing the follow-up task conditional 
86:2765:216848:21:21:71:542:340:144:Times:144:Times:B:on intending to complete)—is a bit coarse. We suspect that some noise is introduced because our 
86:2766:216944:21:20:71:544:319:144:Times:144:Times:B:elicitation  makes  it costless to  express  an  intention  to  complete  (in  future  research  we  plan  to  
86:2767:217055:20:21:71:543:299:144:Times:144:Times:B:explore charging a small “sign up” fee), thereby including in the indicator’s sample frame some 
86:2768:217151:21:21:71:543:278:144:Times:144:Times:B:subjects who rationally do not complete the task. Relatedly, although we set the payoff for task 
86:2769:217248:21:20:71:542:257:144:Times:144:Times:B:completion  to  be  sufficiently  high  to  dominate  any  attention/memory/time  costs  in  marginal 
86:2770:217349:20:21:71:543:237:144:Times:144:Times:B:terms for most subjects (the effective hourly wage is in the hundreds of dollars), it may well be 
86:2771:217447:21:29:71:371:216:144:Times:144:Times:B:the case that the fixed cost exceeds $10 for some respondents. 
86:2772:217510:29:21:89:542:187:144:Times:144:Times:A:Ours is the first work we know of estimating conditional correlations between field outcomes 
86:2773:217603:21:28:71:447:166:144:Times:144:Times:B:and directly elicited measures of limited attention/memory in a broad sample. 
86:2774:217680:28:48:71:218:138:144:Times:144:Times:B:  
86:2775:217680:48:16:72:509:90:42:g_d0_f51:121:Times:F:26 This question is motivated by evidence that shopping behavior strongly predicts borrowing costs 
86:2776:217779:16:0:72:198:74:121:Times:121:Times:F:(Stango and Zinman 2016).  
87:2777:217808:0:30:72:231:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:2. Measuring Cognitive Skills 
87:2778:217836:30:21:90:545:678:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  measure  fluid  intelligence  using  a  15-question,  non-adaptive  number  series  (McArdle,  
87:2779:217935:21:21:72:544:657:144:Times:144:Times:B:Fisher,  and  Kadlec  2007).  Number  series  scores  correlate  strongly  with  those  from  other  fluid  
87:2780:218043:21:31:72:257:636:144:Times:144:Times:B:intelligence tests like IQ and Raven’s. 
87:2781:218083:31:20:90:546:605:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  measure  numeracy  using:  “If  5  people  split  lottery  winnings  of  two  million  dollars  
87:2782:218183:20:21:72:543:585:144:Times:144:Times:B:($2,000,000) into 5 equal shares, how much will each of them get?” and “If the chance of getting 
87:2783:218280:21:21:72:544:564:144:Times:144:Times:B:a  disease  is  10  percent,  how  many  people  out  of  1,000  would  be  expected  to  get  the  disease?”  
87:2784:218391:21:20:72:542:543:144:Times:144:Times:B:(Banks and Oldfield 2007). Response options are open-ended. These questions have been used in 
87:2785:218485:20:21:72:542:523:144:Times:144:Times:B:economics as  numeracy  and/or  financial  literacy  measures  since  their  deployment  in  the  2002 
87:2786:218588:21:21:72:548:502:144:Times:144:Times:B:English   Longitudinal   Study   of   Ageing,   with   subsequent   deployment   in   the   Health   and   
87:2787:218695:21:30:71:291:481:144:Times:144:Times:B:Retirement Study and other national surveys. 
87:2788:218740:30:21:89:543:451:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  measure  financial  literacy  using  Lusardi  and  Mitchell’s  (2014)  “Big  Three”:  “Suppose  
87:2789:218840:21:21:71:543:430:144:Times:144:Times:B:you  had  $100  in  a  savings  account  and  the  interest  rate  was  2%  per  year.  After  5  years,  how  
87:2790:218951:21:21:71:542:409:144:Times:144:Times:B:much do you think you would have in the account if you left the money to grow?”; “Imagine that 
87:2791:219046:21:20:71:542:388:144:Times:144:Times:B:the interest rate on your savings account was 1% per year and inflation was 2% per year. After 1 
87:2792:219143:20:21:71:543:368:144:Times:144:Times:B:year, how much would you be able to buy with the money in this account?”; and “Please tell me 
87:2793:219237:21:21:71:542:347:144:Times:144:Times:B:whether this statement is true or false: "Buying a single company's stock usually provides a safer 
87:2794:219336:21:30:71:399:326:144:Times:144:Times:B:return than a stock mutual fund." Response options are categorical.  
87:2795:219405:30:21:89:545:296:144:Times:144:Times:A:We  measure  executive  function  using  a  two-minute  Stroop  task  (MacLeod  1991).  Our  
87:2796:219498:21:21:72:545:275:144:Times:144:Times:B:version  displays  the  name  of  a  color  on  the  screen  (red,  blue,  green,  or  yellow)  and  asks  the  
87:2797:219610:21:20:72:544:254:144:Times:144:Times:B:subject to click on the button corresponding to the color the word is printed in (red, blue, green, 
87:2798:219710:20:21:72:543:234:144:Times:144:Times:B:or yellow; not necessarily corresponding to the color name). Answering correctly tends to require 
87:2799:219808:21:21:72:544:213:144:Times:144:Times:B:using  conscious  effort  to  override  the  tendency  (automatic  response)  to  select  the  name  rather  
87:2800:219917:21:20:72:542:192:144:Times:144:Times:B:than  the  color.  The  Stroop  task  is  sufficiently  classic  that  the  generic  failure  to  overcome 
87:2801:220024:20:21:72:543:172:144:Times:144:Times:B:automated behavior (in the game “Simon Says,” when an American crosses the street in England, 
87:2802:220118:21:21:72:543:151:144:Times:144:Times:B:etc.) is sometimes referred to as a “Stroop Mistake” (Camerer 2007). Before starting the task, the 
87:2803:220217:21:21:72:543:130:144:Times:144:Times:B:computer shows demonstrations of two choices (movie-style)—one with a correct response, and 
87:2804:220309:21:20:71:545:109:144:Times:144:Times:B:one  with  an  incorrect  response—and  then  gives  the  subject  the  opportunity  to  practice  two  
87:2805:220413:20:0:71:414:89:144:Times:144:Times:B:choices on her own. After practice ends, the task lasts for two minutes. 
88:2806:220488:0:30:72:389:708:144:Times:144:Times:B:3. Survey Formatting and Non-classical Measurement Error 
88:2807:220544:30:21:89:543:678:144:Times:144:Times:A:Data Appendix Table 3 provides reassurance that, a priori, there is little reason to think that 
88:2808:220640:21:21:71:544:657:144:Times:144:Times:B:low  survey  effort  per  se could  contribute  to  a  mechanical  correlation  between  worse  financial  
88:2809:220746:21:21:71:545:636:144:Times:144:Times:B:condition  and  more  behavioral  biases.  A  necessary  condition  for  that  confound  is  that  it  is  
88:2810:220853:21:20:71:546:615:144:Times:144:Times:B:somehow  easier,  from  a  survey  effort  perspective,  to  indicate  worse  than  better  financial  
88:2811:220956:20:21:71:543:595:144:Times:144:Times:B:condition. The table shows that this is unlikely to be the case, given how questions are scripted 
88:2812:221054:21:31:71:237:574:144:Times:144:Times:B:and response options are arrayed.  
88:2813:221089:31:20:89:542:543:144:Times:144:Times:A:Data Appendix Table 4 provides some additional descriptive reassurance with data, showing 
88:2814:221179:20:21:71:543:523:144:Times:144:Times:B:a lack of systematic relationship between survey time spent (across all questions for both Round 
88:2815:221276:21:21:71:545:502:144:Times:144:Times:B:1  modules)  and  financial  condition  responses,  with  the  possible  exception of  the  lowest  time  
88:2816:221382:21:30:72:143:481:144:Times:144:Times:B:spent quintile. 
88:2817:221398:30:21:90:543:451:144:Times:144:Times:A:As  the  main  text  details,  we  deal  with  this  potential  confound  formally,  by  controlling 
88:2818:221499:21:21:72:544:430:144:Times:144:Times:B:flexibly  for  survey  effort  in  both  survey  rounds  with  flexible  controls  for  non-response  and  for  
88:2819:221611:21:21:72:545:409:144:Times:144:Times:B:survey  time  spent,  and  by  dropping  those  in  the  lowest  decile  of  time  spent  as  a  robustness  
88:2820:221720:21:30:72:105:388:144:Times:144:Times:B:check.
